THE RED PAGE.
Verses by Robert Buchanan.
DE BERNY.
louI ou knew him slightly. We, who knew him
well, v
,w something in his soul you could not see:
strength wherein his very vices throve,
power that darken’d much the outer man,
range, yet angelically innocent,
is views were none of ours; his morals —well,
ot English morals at the best; and yet
e loved him, and we miss him —the old haunts
iem dull without that foolish full-grown child ;
le world goes on without him: —London throngs
ith sport and festival; and something less
ran poor De Berry haunts us everywhere—
le buying and the selling, and the strife
t little natures.
What a man was that—■
ist picture him as you perceived him, Noel,
landing beyond his circle. Spare and tall,
lack-bearded and black-eyed ; a sallow face,
r ith lines of idle humour round the lips ;
nose and eyebrow proudly curved ; an eye
lear as a child’s. But thirty summers old !
et wearied out, save only when he waifa’d
is graces in the sunshine. What an air
fas his, when, cigarette in mouth, and bunds
hrust in the pocket of his pantaloons,
le took his daily walk down Regent-street,
bared at the pretty girls, saluted friends,
nd,. pleased as any lady, stopp’d to study
he fashions in the windows of the shops !
'id he not walk af if he walk’d on thrones,
Tith smiles of vacant patronage for all ?
.nd who could guess he had not breakfasted,
[ad little chance of dining, since his purse
leld just the wherewithal to buy a loaf—
Ihange from the shilling spent in purchasing
'he sweet post-prandial cigar !
He lived—
Lh 1 Heaven knew how —for ’twas a mystery !
Vhile the sun shone, he_ saunter’d in the sun ;
lut late at night sat scribbling, by the light
3f .a wax-candle. Wax ? De Berny’s way ;
for, mark, this wanderer let his body suffer,
lunger’d and pinch’d, rather than bate a jot
)f certain very useless luxuries:
imolced nought but real Havannah, ’tis averr’d,
Lid sat at night within his dingy lodging,
Vrapt, king-like, in a costly dressing-gown
lis mother gave him ; slippers on his feet
lis cat, Miguonne, the silken-hair’d Chinese,
Seated upon his shoulder, purring low ;
Lid something royal in his look, despite
His threadbare, pantaloons!
A clever man—
A nature sparkling o’er with jeux d’esprit /
Well read in certain light philosophies
Down from Voltaire ; and, in his easy way,
A sceptic—one whose heart belied his brain.
Oft, leaning back and puffing his cigar,
Pushing his wan, white fingers through his hair—
His cat Mignonne, the velvet-paw’d Chinese,
Rubbing her soft, white cheek against his beard,
And purring her approval—he would sit,
Smiling his sad, good-humour’d, weary smile,
And lightly launch his random, reckless shafts
At English thrift, the literary cant,
The flat, unearnest living of the world,
And (last and lightest) at the tender sex,
Their little virtue and their mighty vows.
This was the man whose face went pale with pain,
When that shrill shriek from Poland fill’d his
ear;
This was the man who pinch’d himself to send
A mite to Garibaldi and the Cause ;
Who cried, or nearly cried, o’er Lamartine,
And loved the passionate passages of Sand ;
Who would have kiss’d the ground beneath the
feet
Of any shape called “ Woman,” plain or fair ;
Gave largess royal to children in the streets;
Treated an unclean beggar seeking alms
To a clean shirt, and sent him off amazed ;
And when he heard sweet voice of- instrument,
Breath’d passionate breath, like one that drinks
with pain
An atmosphere too heavenly rare and sweet.
Pleasure? All me ! what pleasure garner’d he,
Who fasted oftener than ate; who pawn’d
His coat to serve a neighbour, and was cold;
Whose only little joy was promenading
On sunny summer days in Regent-street ?
Hirf talk ? Why, how he talk’d, as I have said J
[ncubus could not prove his neighbours worse,
3r himself blacker, or the cold world colder;
His jests so oft too broad for decent ears,
His impiousness so insolently strong,
His languid grace so callous unto all >
Save the sad sunshine that it flutter’d in.
Yet, Noel, I oould swear that Spirits—those
Who see beneath the eyes, and hear the breathing
The Soul makes as it stirs within the breast-
lent not unlovingly, not angrily,
Lbove that weary, foolish, full-grown Child !
Veary—of what? Weary, I think, for want
)f something whose existence he denied;
fot sick of life, since he had never felt
'he full of living—wearied out, because
'he world look’d falsehood, and his turn was
truth.
fell, late one morning in the summer time,
I hey found him lying in his easy-chair,
frapt royally in the costly dressing-gown
lis mother gave him, slippers on his feet,
nd something royal in his look—cold, dead 1
smell of laudanum sicken’d all the air
round him ; on the table at-his side
copy of De Musset’s Elle et Lui;
nd close at hand a crumpled five-pound note,
a which was written in his round, clear hand
Pour Garibaldi. Vive la Liberte l*’
WHEN WE ARE ALL ASLEEP.
hen He returns, and finds the World so drear—
All sleeping,—young and old, unfair and fair,
ill He stoop down and whisper in each ear,
11 Awaken ! ” or for pity’s sake forbear, —
laying, “ How shall I meet their frozen stare
wonder, and their eyes so full of fear ?
How shall I comfort them in their despair,
they cry out, ‘ Too late ! let us sleep here ’ ? ”
rebance He will not wake us up, but when
He sees us look so happy in our rest,
ill murmur, “ Poor dead women and dead men !
Dire was their doom, and weary was their quest,
herefore awake them unto life again?
Let them sleep on uutroubled —it is best.”

A tall stalwart Lancer lay dying,
And as on his death-bed he lay,
rnu fr ‘ ends who around him were sighing,
these last dying words he did say :
Chorus.
Wrap me up in my tarpaulin jacket,
And say a poor buffer lies low,
And six stalwart Lancers shall carry me •
With steps solemn, mournful, and slow.
Had I the wings of a little dove,
Far, far away would I fly,
Straight to the arms of my true love
And there would 1 lay me and die.
Then get you two little white tombstones,
Put them one at my head and my toe,
And get you a penknife and scratch there,
‘ Here lies a poor buffer below.”
And get you six brandies and sodas,
And set them all out in a row,
And get you six jolly good fellows,
To drink to this buffer below.
And then in the calm of the twilight,
When the soft winds are whispering low,
And the darkening shadows are falling,
Sometimes think of the buffer below.
the burlesque drama, “Little Jack Shep-
pard, by H. P. Stephens and W. Yardley (music
by four others), first produced at the Gaiety
1 heatre, Boxing night, 1885, a song, “ Botany
Bay, occurs. In verse 4 the lines sire very raucn
like verse 2of “ The Tarpaulin Jacket viz.—
Oh, had I the wings of a turtle dove 1
I’d soar on my pinions so high ;
Slap bang to the arms of my Polly love,
And in her sweet presence I’d die.
The prototype of the “Botany Bay” song,
under title of “ Farewell to Judges and Juries,” is
at pages 364-5 of John Ashton’s “ Modern Street
Ballads ’ (London, 1888). The rhythm is similar,
and many of the lines are well nigh word for word
alike the seventh and last verse running—
How often I wish that the eagle,
Would lend me her wings, I would fly ;
Then I’d fly to the arms of my Polly,
And on her soft bosom I’d lie.
There is also a song called “Botany Bay” in
Ashton’s book, but the air and words are different
from the above. These songs are traced back to
early part of last century.

W. R. Campbell writes —
As to “ The Walerman’s Tree." You suggest It is a
new version of an old-time piece, or, at the least, that the
inspiration has been derived from some such source.
You are wrong. ' I wrote it, as an original piece, at
Easter, 1897: The word “ wold,” which is not in Aus-
tralian use, may have caused you to doubt originality.
The second or third State-school reader, in use till a few
years ago, was the means of fixing the word closely on
my mind, and only when too late was the incongruity
noticed. I deny any “historic source,” Australian or
foreign, but admit the influence, in the last three verses,
of “ Wrap me up in my stockwhip and blanket,” though I
only know one verse of that.
——* ♦
Y. J.D. : I have a crow to pluck with Ogilvie.
In his poem called “The Reiver’s Heart” (June
22) he makes several statements which give fine
colour to the verse, but,. so far as my reading'goes,
are quite unauthorised by fact, e.g. —
To lift the Southern lasses.
I lift the soft-eyed maiden
Across the champing steed.
And hear the lone maid weeping
Beneath my bride-arm.
Twice —for our maids rough-kissed.
Nor South for white-armed plunder
The wild moss-troopers ride.
These five distinct references to Violent Abduc-
tion, in the space of six eight-line verses, make it
clear that Ogilvie believes that the lifting of
women was a chief motive of Border forays. The
stalwart dalesmen on the English side of the
Marches were just about the last people in the
world to allow Sabine rapes of this kind to take
place without bloody reckonings. And of such
bloody reckonings on this account there is. I main-
tain, no trace in Border history. The old ballad-
writer who—on the English side—could tell of a
great slaughter that occurred at Chevy Chase
because the Percy of Northumberland went hunt-
ing the deer in the Douglas country—and, on the
Seotcli side, could tell how, because of the capture
of a stark moss-trooper like Kinmont Willie, Buc-
cleucli, the Scottish Warden, swore to
Set Carlisle Castle in a lowe,
And slokketi it with English blood,
would have made a cycle of ballads rivalling those
made about Helen of Troy if women (and not
cattle and cattle-thieves and game) had been the
booty. Therefore, I say Ogilvie was entirely
wrong when he should have been entirely right—
as it is the duty of every man to know the history
and traditions of his own country.
A fine, stalwart stockman lay dying,
His saddle supporting his head,
While his maces all around him were crying,
He rose on his elbow and said :
Chorus.
Wrap me up with my stockwhip and bluey,
And bury me deep down below,
Where the dingoes and crows cannot find me,
In the shade where the coolibahs grow.
Then cut you two stringy-bark saplings,
Place one at my head and my toe ;
Carve on them crossed stockwhip and bridle
To show there’s a stockman below.
And bring out the battered old billy,
Put the pannikins all in a row ;
And drink to the health of the stockman
Who soon will be lying below.
But hark ! ’bis the howl of the dingo,
Watchful and weird —I must go ;
For he tolls the death-knell of the stockmaß
Who soon will be lying below.
And another from C.T. —
A stalwart young digger lay dying,
His bluey supporting his head,
His two mates beside him were crying
As he rose on his pillow and said :
Chorus : s
“ Wrap me up in my old tent and blanket
And bury me deep down below,
Where the dingoes and crows won’t molest me,
In the shade where the coolibahs grow."
There is tea in the battered old billy;
Place the pannikins out in a row,
And we’ll drink to our next merry meeting
In the place where all good fellows go.
Hark to the wail of the dingo I
Watchful and.weird —I must go,
For it sounds the death-knell of the digger
From the gloom of the scrub down below.
And oft in the shades of the twilight,
.When the soft winds are whispering low,
And the twinkling shadows are falling,
Sometimes think of the digger below !
Buchanan and Besant .
“ URPRLSED,” writes a little buzzing cor-
respondent, “that you have not noticed
K_y the deaths of Walter Besant and .Robert
Buchanan, recently cabled on the same
day.” Well, a pleasure of writing, as of life,
consists in always being and doing the unexpected,
in refraining from the expected. Observance of
times and seasons—for meals, or love, or labour—
is unworthy of a man free. The cult of anniver-
saries belongs to savage minds: the civilised affirm
for good things “every place a temple, all seasons
summer.” We see the brutes waking at dawn
and sleeping at dusk all the year round—how
mechanical ! We see King Billy of Bandaroo,
our proud autochthonous monarch, with his life
regulated by suns and moons and periodical ritual
—typical, and how slavish ! We see primi-
tive agricultural communities perpetuating,
the antique observance of Mayday—typical,
and how monotonous ! Why, the whole
virtue of human evolution is that it brings
us power to make Spring in the brain
all the year round, whatever the skies say. Civi-
lisation has endeavoured to end the servitude of
bodies, but has hardly begun to emancipate
minds. We need another Wilberforce to preach
a new crusade on behalf of the religious savage,
pioils one day in seven ; the official savage, with
his regulated holidays; the woman savage, with
her fetish of birthdays and wedding-days, and her
annual repetition of a death-advertisement.
« * * *
These things are all traditions of ancient
slavery—chain-galls and ‘whip-marks. Often the
stick has been' removed, but silly human sheep
still jump at the plaoe where the first sheep
jumped. To be joyous or grievous by the calen-
dar, willy-nilly, what a degrading surrender of
liberty! A man should be self-poised, judging
and deciding his life at every moment, or
(if that seem better) yielding absolutely to
the moment’s influence. Mr. Shandy’s Saturday
formula satisfied the tidy mind of Mrs. Shandy,
no doubt; but he should have been thoroughly
ashamed of himself. Yet how many Mr. Shaudys
move like maohines between hedges of “prin-
ciples,” along a road of “duties,” taking their
pleasure like medicine, by the clock, and “ saving
their souls ” when they reach the Sabbath mile-
post—as if the “kingdom of God ” were a seven-
days bicycle-race. We want new maxims for better
minds. A timefor nothing, and nothing in its time ;
a place for nothing, and nothing in its place.- Are
you hungry ?—eat ! Are you thirsty ’—drink !
Gay ?—rejoice ! Sad ?—grieve ! Refuse Phyllis
if your mood is not Corydon’s; never die before you
are ready. If nothing else had shown the power-
ful individuality of Parnell, it would have been
shown in this,: that he never made appointments
and never,answered letters. In other words, he re-
fused to bind himself for to-morrow, and he refused
to submit his own moods to alien disturbance.
There are few things more hateful than the neces-
sity of keeping in a cold mood last week’s pledge
made in a hot one—so the wise man never pledges
himself. There is nothing more hateful than the
demand that you shall write letters when you
don’t want to —so the strong man never writes
them.
* - * * *
In this matter, as in many others,; woman is
man’s foe. She is essentially a periodic creature,
with the phases of the moon, the ebb and flow of
the tides—a Tuesday-and-'Friday formula, a
machine for the eternal repetition of plum-pudding
at Christmas. Her brain is so impressionable that
one act begins a habit—and every habit is a
fetter : it is partly for this reason that woman is a
hereditary slave. A great part of woman’s life is
spent in endeavouring to tether more-iudividual-
ised man by her habits; and, for the sake of
peace, he often permits the tie. 4 lt is dinner-
time” —and you must eat; “It is bed-time”
—and you must go to sleep; “It is church-
time ” —and you follow her sadly. Trailed in
her triumphal march, you are none the less a vic-
tim that your car is garlanded, your chains gilded.
Sometimes a man revolts—the Sex declares him
“ heartless, unfeeling.” Sometimes he disappears
from the procession of habits—it is in my nund that
the authenticated “missing men’’—silently lost
from their places at the workshop, by the fireside,
are men who have suddenly realised the blank
monotony of their lives, their ignoble servitude, and
have craved and craved free air until the closest
bonds snapped—until they shook oh at once all the
shackles of‘home and “ business,” and went forth
to submerge their identity in a new life of free-
dom. It is the better, way of suicide.
* # * *
You who read this— You /—whoever you may
be, wherever you are, pause for a moment and
consider yourself sub specie acterni : see yourself
in the perspective of the universe. What life do
you lead ? How many of your acts are done to
please yourself ? how many because the fetish of
Habit compels you?—because you have deliberately
or unconsciously bound yourself with silken
threads that have grown to ropes, to chains, to a
vast burden which you carry forward day after
day until you die. What tame pack-horses most
of us are, plodding under loads of yhabit! And
life for most of us, is like a pack-hofse road, worn
in regular hollows where our predecessors have
stepped, and where vre step blindly. Or the
whim-horse would be a better metaphor, foe the
path of the pack-horse varies: the whim-horse
joes in an endless circle. Next whim-horse you
see—the older and blinder the better —trudging
round the track with his head down, just call him
by your own name, and ten to one he’ll answer!
For most men in a brazen prison live,
Where, in the sun's hot eye,
With heads bent down, they languidly
Their lives to some unmeaning task-work give.
Dreaming of naught beyond their prison-wall.
And as, day after day,
Fresh fruits of-barren labour fall
From their tired hands . . .
Gloom settles down more slowly o'er their breast
. . . They cannot stem.
The waves of mournful thought by which they are
prest.
Death in their prison reaches them,
Unfreed, having seen nothing, still unblest *
» * * *
Of course, there'are compensations. We yield
so much liberty to gain so much satisfaction of
instincts ; but there is always danger that the loss
is greater than the gain. What unprofitable bur-
dens You are carrying, for example ; to what life-
wasteful habits You are slave ! Of course, too,
some concession must be made to habit and con-
vention for the sake of peace and quietness—when
in Rome, it usually pays to do as the Romans do. I
remember a second-rate and half-intoxicated
actor delaying me on a midnight kerbstone to
recount, almost with tears, how for the third time
he had forgotten his wife’s birthday ; though she
always remembered his. “This time I wrote it
in a diary a month beforehand ; I thought of it
two days before, and turned a ring on my finger;
I even wrote to So-and-So to ring me up on the
telephone, and tell me about it. But I didn’t
look at my diary ; the ring slipped round while I
was warning; So-and-So went out of town. Her
birthday was on Monday ; and I had a little pre-
sent all ready—and just came across it to-day—
Thursday !—when I went to the drawer. I’d
have given anything to have remembered. She
doesn’t value it half sb much now.”
* • • •
Well, that was an emotion creditable to the
second-rate, half-intoxicated actor; and, while
our wives retain their savage reverence for times
and seasons, it is possibly better to humour them
at some cost of intelligent individuality, and trust
gradual race - progress to educate them to the
knowledge that proofs of affection have at all
times the same essential value. But Be3ant and
Buchanan need not be considered like our wives.
Their critical place in literature has been fixed at
any time during the last ten years, or nearly:
their death has not altered it. It is pleasant to
flout the Time-and-Seaaon fetish demanding
worship on their behalf, and to approach
by one iota the ideal of Journalism. For the
ideal of Journalism naturally follows the ideal of
Life in abolishing the expected-whieh-happens.
Before you open any newspaper of to-day, you
know almost precisely what to expect—and you
.very rarely receive the pleasure of disappointment.
There is the leading-article, saying on the accus-
tomed page the accustomed thing in the accus-
tomed way. There are the paragraphs ; there
the reports ; there the illustrations—everything
just as you have been used-to. It is very stupid ;
but readers will have it so. Most of them are
creatures of habit, glorying in their chains, in-
capable of the mental effort required to break
them. If the place of the dull article
were taken by a story or a poem, their
habit-heavens would fall, —and they would
cease to buy the paper? Would they? Well,
no one knows till the experiment is made,
and no one lias been bold enough to make the ex-
periment. Perchance—who knows ?—the journal-
istic Habit is a hollow sham, only waiting to be
pierced. Perchance, unknown to themselves,
readers, weary of monotony, are only waiting to
trirowoff their chains and welcome the unexpected-
which-never-happens.
* * . * t *
There is this to be said for the buzzing corres-
pondent : that Death is the one Time-post which
perforce must be perpetuated, because it perpe-
tuates itself. “ Why permit it ? ” you say. Really,
there is no reason, if somebody can show us a better
way of evading the perpetuity of Life. The choice
between Life and Death is a choice of bad habits,
and the answer to vour demurrer is simply, “ Why
perpetuate Life?” And while argument is pro-
ceeding Death continues to be the unescapable
season—the “ Hitherto hath the Lord helped us”
mark, at which you turn round to put a full-stop
to life-achievement and to consider its worth.
And, striking a mean of pro and con, answer to
the buzzing correspondent has been delayed for a
month—to show that, if we do bring tribute to
the Time-and-Season fetish, we bring it of our
own free will, in our own time and season, have
duly asserted our civilised prerogative of staying
away. As to Besant and Buchanan, we have
B.
*That’s all I remember. Gave away Matthew Arnold
recently. Just sent to “leading 1 Sydney bookseller" tor
another copy. “None in stock.” Back for Golden
Treasury “ Selections.” “ None in stock.” Similar ex-
perience with Rossetti four years ago. But pass the
stigma from bookseller to public—“ he gives them what
they want.”

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sundown in the Bnsh.
The sun sinks, and the bush grows grey and pale,
Like a grim giant in his mortal swoon ;
The jackass strokes the twilight with the flail
Of his harsh humor —gibing at the moon—
Yon film-eyed corse, whose cerements avail
Scarce to conceal her from that bird-buffoon
Whose ribald laughter shakes the silence dim,
Stirring the shroud that hideth her from him.
The gum-trees spread wan hands, fingering the
dusk
As if to feel the darkness that is near;
Like hoary misers groping at the husk
And warding off the gold in panic-fear ;
And thou, dread beldame Bush, whose tattered
busk
And tangled elf-locks shroud thy bosom sere,
Mock’st me, thy foster starveling, with the wail
Of bloodless lips, and eyes than death more pale.
Oh, ghostly land, that weigheth on the soul,
Like the long ages darkened by Eve’s sin !
Oh, ghostly phantom, that doth man cajole,
With gold, to thy dark wilderness, to win
Life’s bitter lies : thy vampire lips control
His heart and brain, making his bosom thin ;
Sapping the soul’s bright courage with caress
Of gilded, barren-breasted cold traitress !
Though man brings to thee all that he can give—
Courage and steadfastness—his talents here
Must faint or languish, stricken by the bane
Thy pores distil. His spirit, shining clear,
Seareheth thy dark dominions all in vain,
And sinketh to the socket, as in fear !
Thrice blest are they who shrink not from thy
spell :
Who grieves not here would never grieve in hell !
N.s.w.
T.H.M.
The Old Yahrung.
Where wombats roll in the twilight dim,
Swans sleep afloat, and black-ducks swim,
And teal disport the green reeds among ;
Where the mopokes cry in the scrub below,
And the curlews scream by the glow-worm’s glow—
Are the lone green shores of the old Yahrung.
They paced that sward in the moonlit night,
They heard the whirr of wild wings in flight,
And refrains of songs that were long years sung ;
The little squirrels that gambolled round
They heeded not, for their hearts had found
Their Heaven on Earth by the old Yahrung.
He galloped away in the moonlit night
To his cattle camped in a far-off bight,
Where the clatter of hoofs in the wild woods rung ;
But he thought of her as the nights went past,
Till the trip was o’er and he came at last
Back to the shores of the old Yahrung.
“ Where’s Alice ? Ay, my fair bride to be,
My blue-eyed beauty—say, where is she ? ”
And the mother sighed, for her heart was stung.
“ Not of this world is thy sweetheart; know
At dawn of day, where the lilies grow,
She was drowned last June in the old Yahrung.
“That snow-white swan that is swimming there
Like a mist-wraith came thro’ the morning air,
And cried aloud with its wings out-flung :
’Tis my dear lost child, I have heard them say
(A strange, weird tale !), yet it swims for aye
O’er the one sad spot on the old Yahrung.”
Then a drover, mad, trudged those banks in quest
Of a fair sweet girl as the sun went west,
And the swan cried loud as Love’s song was
sung:
“ I’m Alice ! thine Alice ! ” it seemed to say.
He paused and he wooed, and he bore one day
A bride from the shores of the old Yahrung.
Such is the tale that the drovers tell,
Encamped by the spot that they know so well,
Harking to days when the world was young,
While the dingoes howl by the brumbies’ trail;
The curlews scream, and the night-winds wail
By the lone, weird shores of the old Yahrung.
N.s.w.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
Rain in the Forest.
Rain in the forest!
And down the trees the water crawls;
There is a weeping everywhere,
And far, far off, a lone bird calls.
Here in the forest,
While tall trees stoop and wild winds blow,
I catch, at times, the interchange
Of many whispers, vague and low.
Rain in the forest!
And all the ways are desolate,
And that small bird that calls at times
Seems calling for a vanished mate.
Tree-tops are tossing,
And rain-drops fall from every leaf :
Is there a rage beyond man’s rage,
And, out beyond his grief, a grief ?
Who shall make answer ?
Who reveal the soul that seems
So intimate, so far, so sad,
So made of tears and sobs and dreams ?
n.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.

THE AUSTRALASIAN SUPREME COURT OF THE FUTURE.
The 27th clause of the Federal Government’s Post and Telegraph Bill gives the P.M.G. or his deputy authority—not only to refuse to transmit, but to destroy —any posted
newspaper containing “ seditious. ht[?]phemous, indecent or obscene matter."
NOW WHAT IS SEDITIOUS MATTER, OR BLASPHEMOUS MATTER, OR INDECENT MATTER, OR OBSCENE MATTER? EVERYTHING THAT YOU LIKE TO
CALL so; but nothing until it is held to be so by judge and jury.

SATURDAY, JULY 20, 1901. •
Poreign-trade and Sweating.
A Parliamentary Commission which has
been drifting round Australia inquiring into
the working of factory laws, minimum-wage
laws, anti-sweating laws, early-closing laws,
and other legislation of an allegedly shock-
ing character, lately held some sittings in
Sydney. And in that hot-bed of Foreign-
trade, where the importing monopolist
makes huge fortunes and runs the
country, and where the blessed policy
that is said to make the community Jolly
and Fat has a better show than in any
other part of the world, save Britain,
the Commission collected some really re-
markable evidence. Sydney has just finished
with a huge strike of ironworkers who
wanted their wages raised to the standard of
Protectionist Victoria. It has heard recently
of grumblings in its police-force, which
'also wants its pay and privileges raised
to the standard of Protectionist Victoria.
These may be small things, but they
all tend to reflect more or less on the
great Jolly and Fat superstition. And
now the merchants and manufacturers
of Sydney have cast further discredit on the
legend by such evidence as this—taken from
the Sydney daily paper reports of last
Thursday and Friday :
Alfred Hra sen (of R. I). Brascij & Co., clothing manu-
facturers), said that the price for making trousers for
warehouses was IOJd. per pair. The lowest price he paid
was 6d. per pair. The men had to work very hard from
12 to 14 hours per day in order to make from 10s. to
12s. 6d. per week. Prices given 12 months ago for making
a line of 500 pairs were 16jd. per dozen ; 16£d. for trim-
ming, 6s. per dozen for stamping, 15d. per dozen for
pressing, and 9d. per dozen for cutting. . . .
Apprentices started in the clothing trade at 2s. 6d, 45.,
and ss. per week for the first six months, and received a
2s. 6d. rise then if they were worth it. Finishers were
paid from 7s. 6d. up to 15s. and 20s. per week. Two
hands making vests could earn 275. 6d. per week on the
prices paid outside factories. The outside prices were a
good deal lower than the prices they paid in the factories.
Their price for trousers was 10Jd. for making, but outside
they were made for about Bd., and working at the latter
rate about 12 to 14 hours daily about 10s. to 12s. 6d. per
week could be made. Sac coats were made from Is. 9d.
to Bs. each, and up to 6s. for Chesterfields. He thought
that workers were better off in Victoria on account of the
law regulating the wages there. About 30 per cent, of
the women engaged in the clothing factories, he con-
sidered, were young girls from 15 to 19 years of age.
Outside labor was principally done by married women.
Think of these things in the Freetrade land
of Jolly and Fat! Twelve to 14 hours hard
work per day in order to make from 10s. to
12s. 6d. per week ! ! Pants made at Bd.
per pair !! ! Finishers can really earn up
to £1 per week ! ! 1 !
* * * *
And here is some more evidence, mostly
on the subject of wages as paid in Sydney :
Mr. J. T. Robertson, clothing manufacturer, 77 York-
street, stated that he manufactured for Messrs.
M‘Arthur’s warehouse. He was in favor of factory
legislation that would place manufacturers on an even
basis and avoid sweating. He had to send to Mel-
bourne last year, when busy, for hands, and paid one £2,
two 325. 6d, and two 30s. per week. These wages loere
higher than he paid here. His highest rate to girls in
Sydney was 275. 6d. per week. Witness gave particulars
regarding the cost of making vests, prices paid being
from 6s. to Bs. per dozen ; trousers, ss. 6d. per dozen
with plain seams and 6s. per dozen for raised seams ;
whilst for better-class goods Bs. to 9s. per dozen was paid.
He considered they could compete with Melbourne, as
wages were lower here. In Melbourne he believed the cost
of living was cheaper.
Mr. T. B. Clegg, clerk in charge Department of Labor
and Industry : We have in our reports repeatedly called
attention to the low prices paid for certain work, and
have figures which go to support Mr. Brasch’s state-
ments. Continuing, witness said in cross-examination,
that 10s. per week and perhaps a little more would be a
fair representative wage earned by out-door women in
the trousers line.
Mr. Charles P. Neilson, factory manager for Messrs.
Morgan, Smith and Whybrow, boot manufacturers,
Sydney, said that he paid boj's commencing, at the rate
of 6s. per week. He appreciated the factories legislation
m this State, because they enjoyed more freedom com-
pared with Victoria. He would favor a uniform Shops
and Factories Act for the Commonwealth. The class of
labor here was, in his opinion, not so high as in Victoria.
He encouraged no sweating, but heard that it existed in
one or two places. The retail price of boots in Sydney
was about the same as in Melbourne. The Victorian Act
applied in this State, he ventured to say, would be simply
ruinous under freetrade. Its effect in Victoria had not
been to make boots any dearer to the consumer.
Thomas Willson, describing himself as a draper’s assis-
tant, said that for 21 years he had served in a shop from
8 a.m. to 9 and 9.80 p.m., to the great detriment of his
health. He believed the warmest supporters of the Early
Closing Act at the present time were those who at first
condemned it. The current wages for drapers’ assistants
were very low Some large firms were giving only £1 and
255. per week. They were also bringing in a lot of boy
labor, starting the youngsters at as low as 2s. 6d. per
week.
* * * *
These witnesses show up the conditions
of labor under the great Freetrade policy
in a truly mournful aspect. And they do
more than that. It is directly stated that
“Workers are better off in Victoria on
account of the law regulating wages there.
Also that Sydney can compete with Mel-
bourne because wages are lower in Sydney,
and in further support of this statement the
man who sent to Melbourne for workers had
to pay them at a higher rate than the
Sydney standard. It is further declared
that the Protected State has developed a
better class of operatives ; and that in addi-
tion to getting higher wages in the Pro-
tected State the cost of living is cheaper.
Therefore it seems that Protection benefits
the worker at both ends ; it costs him less
to live, and he has more money to live
upon. These statements do not rest, by
any means, on the unsupported testimony
of interested manufacturers. N.S. Wales
Statistician Coghlan, in his chapter on
the “ Cost of Living ” (“ Seven Colo-
nies of Australasia ” for 1899-1900, pages
419 and 420) says that the standard of
living is much the same in all the States, but
that the cost is so widely different that it
runs in N.S.W. to £39 14s. lid. per inhabi-
tant per annum, while if the six cheaper
Protectionist States are lumped in with
N.S.W., the average for all seven States is
reduced to £36 19s. 5d per inhabitant per
annum. And if his latest list of the highest
and lowest wages paid in all trades (“ Statis-
tical Register of N.S.W.” for 1899, pages
967 to 1013) is compared with the correspond-
ing Victorian official return (“ Victorian
Year Book,” part 111., pages 602 to 606) ic
is found that in 106 cases the rate paid is
higher in Victoria, and in only 68 cases is it
higher in N.S.W. And out of 87 trades
there are 71 where the lowest wage paid in
Victoria is higher than the lowest wage
paid in N.S. W. Since these figures
were issued the Victorian minimum wage-
law has been much further extended, to the
still greater advantage of the Victorian
worker. These are corroborative evidences
which can’t be ignored or explained away.
The interested manufacturer might strain the
truth on one side, just as the interested im-
porter and the papers which represent him
lie on the other side ; but when the disin-
terested State Statistician is on the manu-
facturer’s side his case becomes overwhelm-
ing.
• * * »
The invariable rep' • of .he Foreign-trader
is, that even if Pr.,cection makes wages
higher or the selling price of goods lower, it
is against reason and logic to suppose that it
can do both things. A Sydney bootmaker
appeared before the Commission to explain
how it did both things, and the Sydney
Telegraph of last Friday reported his words
as follows :
Mr. J. P. Wright, boot manufacturer, said his firm
exported to Tasmania, West Australia, and Maoriland,
but did not do trade with Victoria, because the duties
were prohibitive. Even after the duties were removed
he did not think there would be much export to Victoria,
because the Victorians were already able to successfully
compete with the local factories in the Riverina. During
the past year Victoria’s export of boots to this State
amounted to between £70,000 and £BO,OOO. Local men
were, however, hopeful of being able to do a fair business
with Queensland when the duties were removed. His
firm employed about 220 hands, and paid clickers from
20s. to £2, machinists (females) from 12s. to 15s.
A Commissioner: You say that you cannot compete
with Victoria. How is it that, paying a lower Tate of
wages than the Act makes imperative in Victoria, you are
unable to make your goods cheaper ?
Mr. Wright : Because the Victorian factories are larger,
and work more continuously. Furthermore, the Victorian
manufacturer has the whole trade to himself. We in New
South Wales have nothing to ourselves. The importations
of American boots alone last month came to £30,000.
Then there is the prejudice of local merchants and shop-
keepers for imported as against local goods. Still another
reason is that a more highly-finished class of goods is
made in Victoria than is the custom in New South Wales.
Our workmen are not trained to make the better class of
goods because the demand for them is satisfied by im-
portations. Also, the best class of raw material is not
produced in New South Wales. If we want especially
good leather we have to send elsewhere for it.
A Commissioner : Suppose you had a duty on boots
coming into New South Wales equal to that on boots
coming into Victoria, would that remove your objection
to the legal minimum wage ?—No, but I would be in favor
of a uniform minimum rate of payment on the piecework
principle. They pay all on piecework in the trade in
America, and in the factory which is the largest exporter
to Australia an average of £4 per week is earned by the
hands.
A Commissioner : Then how is it that that firm, paying-
such wages and insurance and freight, can land goods
here cheaper than you can sell them at ? Is it not a re-
flection upon Australian intelligence and enterprise?
Mr. Wright : No. They can make a so much better
article than we can, because they are able to “ specialise,”
and they can specialise because they have got such a large
home market. Whole townships are engaged in the
making of a single line. In Brockton, where there are
about 120 factories, not a single lady’s boot is made—all
men’s. On the other hand, you find towns where ladies’
boots alone are made. And so on right through.
* • • »
Think of that ! The Protected country
pays an average wage of £4 per week, and
yet can send its goods to the Foreign-trade
country, which pays from 12s. to £2 per
week, and beat it badly in its own market,
for the United States is now by far the
largest exporter of boots to New South
Wales. And the whole explanation lies in
economy of labor. The Protected manufac-
turer has his own local market secured to
him. With that big and reliable demand he
can put up larger factories, get better
machinery, and go in for economical sub-
division of labor. With a more reliable
trade and more reliable employment a
better-trained class of workmen grow up,
who can not only make more wages for
themselves but also make better profits for
their employers. And meanwhile Foreign-
trade New South Wales has never been able
to capture its own market so far as the
better class of boots is concerned. It makes
large quantities of cheap bluchers and the
like, for on the very cheap and common
blucher the freight and other charges
attached to importation are themselves a
considerable measure of Protection. And
the common variety of boot, being practi-
cally the only local product, it is made in a
common sort of way by a common and very
cheap variety of artisan. The better class of
artisan doesn’t grow in N.S.W. for want of
a better class of work to employ him at.
The amount saved by low wages is lost by
working in a crude and Common sort of way
with the inferior raw material which the
Foreign-trade system produces, and no one
is sufficiently sure of the local market to
start work in a larger and better way. So
N.S.W. pays low wages and spends over
£400,000 a year in buying boots from Vic-
toria, South Australia, Germany, but most
of all from the United States ; while Victoria
and the United States pay high wages, and
not only supply their own wants but export
boots as well.
• * • *
But perhaps the most notable item in the
evidence which the Commission collected in
Sydney was the frank acknowledgment of
boot-manufacturer Neilson that under
N.S. W.’s Foreign-trade policy the Victorian
minimum wage law was impossible. Foreign-
trade and sweating must go together. They
are inseparable. The minimum wage law
had not raised the price of boots, nor
destroyed the boot industry in Victoria.
With a reliable local market, a large business,
workmen, and good machinery,
it was possible for the Victorian employer
to pay the high wage fixed by law, and sell
boots as cheaply as in low-wage N.S.W.,
and still prosper. But to pay that high
wage in a country where inferior workmen
make an inferior class of goods with inferior
unprotected raw material, and where the
manufacturer has no reliable local market
secured to him, is said to be impossible.
Doubtless it is. Certainly no Foreign-trade
State has ever attempted to establish a
minimum-wage law, nor is it likely to. And
when all Australia has a Protective Federal
tariff, but no inter-State Protection, a new
situation will arise. It will then be possible
to have a legal minimum wage all over Aus-
tralia, but it must be all over Australia or
nowhere. If Protected Victoria pays a high
wage fixed by law, while the other equally-
Protected States pay about half that wage,
Victorian industries are doomed. The manu-
facturer, who is a sordid being with no
romance about him, will simply drift to the
cheaper wage States and make larger profits
there on sweating principles. Either an
equal Federal minimum wage law must be
applied to the whole Commonwealth, or Vic-
toria must repeal its Act in self-defence and
fight the other States on their own sweating
principles till better times arrive. Unless
the democratic party is willing to see the
abandonment of the only serious attempt
yet made to abolish sweating in Aus-
tralia it must make a great effort
to have this question handed over bv
the State Legislatures to the Federal Par-
liament, so that it can be dealt with
on an all-Australian basis. Of course if,
by any incredible chance, the Reid and
M‘ Mil t.AN and Puls for n Revenue Tariff
party comes uppermost in the Federal
Parliament, then the Minimum Wage Law
of Victoria must be repealed in red-hot
haste, and any idea of extending it to the
other States must needs be abandoned. Its
abolition would mean keeping industries
alive on a badly-paid basis till saner coun-
sels prevailed and it was possible to again do
something to mitigate the sweating evil.
Its retention, while the Revenue Tariff folly
prevailed, would mean presently having no
industries worth mentioning to keep alive
on any basis at all.
The Labor Party Anomaly.
The Labor Party has been a power in New South
Wales politics for the past ten years, and
almost the whole of that time the Government has
lived solely by its permission. It has controlled
the State as no Parliamentary party of its size lias
ever done before, and much good has resulted.
Also much harm has resulted, though the good
has outweighed the harm. The position of
the Labor Party in Parliament is an anomaly.
It has power without responsibility. It never
attempts to form a Ministry of its own, though it
is quite possible that it could draw to itself suffi-
cient of the floating elements of the Assembly to
make it the dominant section if it liked to bid for
power. Never having had.charge of the Treasury,
it declines to be responsible in any way for the
incredible muddle of the State’s finances, and the
discreditable shifts and devices that are adopted
to make the State look solvent. It has helped
to unmake three Ministries, but it didn’t take the
responsibility of helping, in any direct sense, to
make the Ministries that succeeded them. Dibbs,
Reid and Line each formed his Cabinet out of
his own personal following— Reid certainly tried
to put some direct responsibility oil the Labor
Party by including Cook in his Ministry, but the
party promptly dropped Cook out of its ranks,
and disclaimed him, and thus preserved its neutral
attitude.
* * * #
Then the Labor Party, without taking any re-
sponsibility for the Ministerial existence of Dibbs,
or Reid, or Lyne, or See, gave each its support
on certain conditions. So it was able to say, if
Dibbs, or Reid, or Lyne, or See was convicted of
so conducting the finances that N.S. W. was drift-
ing down the broad road to Insolvency: “We
didn’t make this man Premier, and therefore we
n °t responsible for his political dishonesty.”
The Labor Party kept Reid in office through
the worst days of his worst political dodges and
swindles, yet it was always in a position to point
out that it didn’t put the famous ledger-cooker
into office (it only found him there); that he
wasn’t its leader, and that the sin of his political
arrival didn’t lie at its door. It was able to say
the same thing of Lyne and See. Yet all the dis-
graceful faking of the public accounts, all the
frauds, swindles, and dirty financial dodges of the
past 10 years are, indirectly but none the less
certainly, the Labor Party’s doing. Every one of
the Governments which did these unclean things
was kept in office by the Labor Party. In one
sense it is worse than the old Freetrade Party, and
worse than the old Protectionist Party. The
Freetrade Party is responsible for Reid’s fakes,
and the other crowd is responsible for Lyne’s
fakes ; but the Labor Party goes one better: it is
responsible for the fakes of both men. They both
had to do what the Labor Party bade them in
order to retain their positions. If the Labor Party
had made honest finance one of the conditions of
its support N.S. W, would have had honest finance.
But it didn’t. In its own negative way it
apparently cared as little for honest finance as the
worst of them. But it is specially memorable as
the only crowd which connived at all the public’
swindles of the last ten years—bar the little item
of a few pounds paid to Jawbone Neild. No
other crowd has quite such a vast record of conni-
vance as that, though it is quite probable the
others would have connived just as much in the
same circumstances.
* * * *
The Labor Party’s attitude of irresponsibility is
curious. It was solely through its support that
George R,eid, when there was already a stupen-
dous deficit, which was growing every year, was
able to take off £700,000 a year customs duty and
put on only £400,000 a year direct taxation in its
place, thereby making the deficit £300,000 a year
worse than it had been before. The Labor Party
got great credit as the body which really estab-
lished a system of direct taxation in N.S.W., but
somehow, on the ground that it wasn’t part of the
direct Reid following, and for the further reason
that it didn’t profess to have any complete finan-
cial policy of its own, it didn’t share the obloquy
of the financial wreckage which ensued. The finan-
cial wreckage killed the Reid party in the end,
while the Labor men rather gained strength than
otherwise. The same men are responsible for the
fact that the Old Age Pensions Bill was passed,
aiid are equally responsible for the fact that the
Bill didn’t provide a copper to pay the pensions
with, yet somehow the credit falls mostly on the
Labor Party and the obloquy drops on the Lyne-
See Government. The Labor Party was largely
responsible for the Sydney land resumptions—an
eminently wise idea which should have been
carried further than it was. It also heard Lyne
expound that sinful and politically-fraudulent
financial statement wherein he proposed to fake
the accounts by treating the rents of the resumed
area as revenue, while making no provision for the
payment of interest on the purchase-money—a
necessary outlay which would absorb all these
rents. It heard the swindle denounced and
fully exposed ; and then it voted for Lyne
and for the swindle. Yet, during the re-
cent State elections a Labor member assured
the public that if the Labor Party discovered
that this disgraceful proceeding had been done by
the Lyne-See Government, that party would do
its duty with Roman fortitude, and throw the
Government out. The Labor Party had done the
disgraceful proceeding itself. It heard the matter
fully exposed, and then it deliberately voted for
the Estimates, and for the Lyne Government,
and for the disgraceful proceeding. Yet it keeps
up the farce of being a Harold Skimpole in
politics—a gushing child of Nature which can’t be
held responsible in matters of finance—and one of
its members assured the country that if it found,
to its own great surprise, that it had done this
thing, it would indignantly eject the other man.
* * * *
The Labor Party can’t keep any man in office
without being responsible for what he does while
in office. It is the only party which had power
to make both Reid and Lyne go straight, and to its
disgrace it didn’t use that power. It badly needs
to make honesty one of the planks of its inany-
planked platform. The policy of support in return
for concessions is good enough—if honesty, and sol-
vency, and decent Government, and the"payment
of the country s liabilities are among the conces-
sions ; but unfortunately they are not. Since the
Labor Party has been a power in N.S. W. politics
the finances of the State have been better than
they were before—and they have also been worse.
They have been better because the State has not
run into debt after the frenzied fashion of the 80’s.
In the 10 years between 1881 and 1891, when there
was no Labor Party, N.S.W. borrowed and spent
the incredible sum of £36,000,000, or £3,600,000 a
year. In the nine years from 1891 to 1900 it only
added about £13,000,000 to its visible debts, and,
even allowing for hidden deficits, the increase
was probably not much over £2,000,000 a year—
though this was partly because Britain would no


longer lend money as in former times. And the
finances have also been much worse, for, whereas
the large borrowings of former days were mostly for
productive works, the comparatively small borrow-
ings novv are greatly used for ordinary expenditure,
and political fraud, and cooking of the accounts.
Out of £2,000,000 borrowed in one year now a
much larger sum is shamefully and corruptly mis-
applied than out of £4,000,000 borrowed in one
year the decade before last. The Labor Party
didn’t invent this new swindle, but it is respon-
sible none the less. When Reid’s great Cash
Basis sin might otherwise have justly cost him his
billet the Labor Party kept him in office, and
didn’t even bring serious pressure to bear on him
to make him go straight. When Lyne did his
mean and discreditable dodge by misapplying the
rents of the resumed area, that might have justly
lost him his billet, only the Labor Party kept
him also in office. Almost any discreditable and
bankrupt dodge is possible to the politician who
has the support of the Labor Party. It doesn’t
originate swindles, nor does it openly defend
them. It simply goes on the theory that it can’t
be expected (the Lord only knows why) to take
any interest in finance —except in such small,
crude, childish matters as a few pounds paid with-
out authority to Jawbone Neied.
• * * *
And, despite all this, The Bulletin wants to
see the N.S.W. Labor Party in office. Then it
will either have to give the State clean finance
or invent its own swindles and be responsible for
them. As it hasn’t invented any swindles of its
own up to now, this paper has hopes that it will
prove by far the most desirable party of the three
to hold the Ministerial billets. Under the present
system of power without responsibility it has done
an immense amount of good in many ways, but
the financial way isn’t one of them. In that way
it has probably done more harm than good.
The Attack on Freedom of the Press.
Every democrat in the Commonwealth . House of
Representatives should resist to the utmost
the passage of the clauses in the Govt.
Post and Telegraphs Bill attacking the free-
dom of the press. Than the liberty of the
press we have no more precious possession.
At this day the growth and development of the
popular intelligence, and its attitude towards
national questions, are chiefly dependent upon
the news gathered and transmitted by the press,
and upon the free expression of opinion ; and the
day when the Commonwealth Govt, succeeds in
gaining a tyrant’s power of censorship and sup-
pression will be a bad day for Australia.
* * * *
The 41st clause of the Bill gives the P.M.G. or
his deputy, authority—not only to refuse to trans-
mit, but to destroy —any postal article containing
“ profane, blasphemous, indecent, obscene, offensive,
or libellous matter.” What is the qualification
of a Postmaster-General to usurp the function
of a judge and jury, and to decide what is blas-
phemous, libellous, or obscene ? He has none
whatever. What is “ libellous ” matter ? What
is “ blasphemous ” matter ? Everything that you
like to call so ; nothing until it is proved before a
judge and jury. To permit any Govt, official to
abolish the forms of justice at his whim or preju-
dice is a monstrous outrage on liberty.
* • * *
And “offensive!” Why, every line, every word
almost, that can be printed is “offensive” to
somebody! Every comment upon a politician may
be easily “ offensive ” to the politician. And the
Commonwealth Govt, of politicians asks for
power not to detain, but to destroy, “offensive”
matter—to destroy the evidence impeaching its
extra-judicial judgments, the evidence of its poli-
tical usurpation of a jury’s functions !
* * * *
It is not to the point to argue that the Post*
master-General “ would not do this ; ” the Govt.
“ would never dream of doing that.” The Govt,
has no right to ask for the power of doing this or
that. Censorship of public morals is not its busi-
ness ; a politician or a Cabinet of politicians is not
fitted to be censor. Decision as to what is libellous
or profane is not its business : it is the business of
the constituted courts of justice, whose functions
the Govt, is not fitted to perform, is essentially
unfitted to perform.
* * * *
The only power the Govt, is entitled to ask for,
the only power Parliament should ever dream of
granting, is a rower to “refuse to transmit any
postal m itter which is plainly and scandalously
hbcl'ous or obscene .” And the Act should contain
a specific provision giving the right of immediate
redress, with damages, before a Judge and jury,
in the event of injustice being done.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Inter-State Commission Grievance.
The fact that the Federal Bill constituting an
Inter-State Commission gives that body power to
interfere with the rates charged by private car-
riers—ship-owners and such—has aroused a blast
of grievous indignation among the shipping com-
panies. There is a general threat to close up, to
close down, to sell the ships, to take capital out of
the country, and to raise Cain generally. Even
if the Bill passes in its present form, however, it
is practically certain that the companies will cool
off wonderfully. The chief purpose of the Inter-
State Commission is to prevent the charging of
rates (principally on State railways) which will
put one State at a disadvantage as compared with
another. There is nothing gained by prohibiting
Victoria from charging a customs duty on N.S.W.
beef, if Victoria can get level by charging the
extra duty in the shape of special freights on
N.S.W. beef sent over its railwa3 T s, or special tolls
on N.S.W. cattle passing over its roads. But
if the powers of the Inter-State Commission
don’t extend to private railroads, then the
Broken Hill line, for instance, if its mostly-
Victorian owners were interested in Vic-
torian manufactures, might get a monopoly
of the Broken Hill trade for their State by
charging prohibitive rates over their bit of line
for N.S.W. and S.A. goods, while carrying Vic.
goods at a low tariff. A very similar thing might
happen with road or sea carriage. If the Aus-
tralian shipping companies amalgamated (and
t sing dike that do happen) and such a corporation
as the Colonial Sugar Co. was a huge shareholder
in the concern, it might easily get much higher
rates levied on the sugar of any possible rival
combination, and to a great extent crush it out of
the market. Sea freights are so much cheaper
than land freights that this could be done without
bringing the railways into serious competition, so
far as the trade between the large coastal cities is
concerned. Again, a State might become a
ship-owner itself, and carry the goods of
its own people at specially low rates,
which would again be a form of Inter-State
Protection. There are dozens of ways in which
■both land an I sea carriers might discriminate, or
their own profit, between different people and
different States. Britain has, at times, seen the
astounding spectacle of its own ships carrying
continental goods to British colonies at cheaper
rates than British goods. The co.’s' put up the
freights in Britain by a combination among them-
selves, but carried goods from foreign ports at the
old low charge in order to compete with foreign
companies that were outside the ring. The
arrangement may have been good enough for the
companies, but it was very bad for British manu-
facturers. The theory that the Inter-State Com-
mission will use its powers for mere mischievous
and purposeless meddling is based on the idea
that the Commission will naturally be a dangerous
lunatic at a high salary, and the Parliament
another dangerous lunatic at a moderate salary.
In the same way the power which any Govern-
ment possesses of calling the whole population to
arms at a crisis might be objected to on the ground
that the Government, being naturally a complete
idiot, would be calling the whole male population
out three times a week to the destruction of all
industry. Also the proposal to give Sydney Cor-
poration power to condemn buildings might be
objected to for fear it would at once order the
pulling down of the Equitable Building and the
G.P.O. Some little latitude must be allowed
on the assumption that the Government will have
lucid intervals.

Federal Defence.
The Federal Defence Bill is a document which
seems to have been drawn up with great care, and
with a large amount of intelligence. Australia,
unfortunately, must have an army, for the suffi-
cient reason that this is a cold world, and full of
wickedness. It needs, in proportion to popula-
tion, almost the biggest army on eartb, for it has
a huge country to defend, and not many people to
defend it. And as it can’t afford to have all its
male population standing about in uniform and
holding up a gun, the best device is to rely mainly
on a citizen army. There is to be a small per-
manent force, and a larger volunteer force behind
that; and in the rear of both practically the whole
male community between the ages of 18 and 00 is
liable to be called out. The latter departure isn’t
so radical as it looks, for, law or no law, every
Government has power, as an Act of State, to call
out every male citizen on an emergency. It has
the same power to call on women to enlist if the
emergency is large enough. So in this
respect the Act merely defines on paper
a power already existing. 'Rifle clubs are to be
encouraged and assisted, so as to make sure that
some at least of the reserves will know one end of
a gun from the other. The retirement of officers
is provided for—a major-general goes out at 62,
but if a man reaches the age of 45, and has got no
further than the grade of Second-lieutenant, he
goes out remorselessly at 45, and men of higher
rank are ejected at varying ages. In military
matters, despite the isolated examples of old men
who have done great deeds, the general rule is
that the ancient must go. And it would be a
wise idea to add a proviso that the dispro-
portionately-obese man, as weU as the one above
a certain age, should be retired ; the fat
man should go as well as the greybeard. No
provision is made as yet for compulsory and un -
versal military training, though that most essen-
tial matter will probably be attended to when the
Commonwealth has more money and more
time. The Permanent Forces are to be
liable for foreign service, though the rest
of the army is for defensive purposes onlv.
The proviso allowing for service outside Australia
was probably unavoidable —if an army isn’t
allowed to follow a defeated invader and hit him
again before he has time to recover, then half its
usefulness is lost. Also, if it was impossible to
send Australian military aid to repel an attack
on, say, Fiji or Maoriland, it might lead to worse
calamities later on. All the same the outside ser-
vice idea is a misfortune —however unavoidable
the misfortune may be. It makes the way easier
for an unlimited amount of expensive and
idiotic contingenting which may lead to any
amount of trouble hereafter. All the same, if
this country is bent, at any time, on being another
great fool in addition to the fool it has been
already, it is tolerably certain that no law could
be devised that would prevent it. And if it isn’t
bent on being a fool the foreign service proviso in
the Federal Defence Bill won’t make it one. No
country can tie itself so securely to a post of its
own construction that it can’t get away and do
something ridiculous if it has a widespread incli-
nation that way, and no laws that a man can pass
prohibiting himself from being a lunatic will pre-
vent him being one if he is one.

A State Grog Monopoly.
Russia —slow, backward, semi-barbarie Russia,
which moves occasionally even faster than civilised
Anglo-Saxondom—tried a notable experiment in
1895. It established a State, liquor monopoly in
four of the eastern provinces ; and next year it
extended the experiment to other parts of the
Empire. As fast as the machinery can be got
into working order the system is being carried all
over Russia, and the results up to date are de-
scribed by the Minister of Finance as follows :
Now that four years have passed since the monopoly
was put in force in the eastern provinces, and two and a
half years since it came into operation in the vast regions
of the south and south-west, it is permissible to express a
judgment on its moral and economic effects, and to pre-
sent some data for estimating its financial results. . . .
The better quality of the brandy, the considerable reduc-
tion in the number of places of sale, the establishment of
uniform prices strictly proportionate to the quantity sold,
the impossibility of obtaining alcoholic liquors except for
ready money—all these advantages, and others which are
brought about by the rectification and sale of spirits
under the care of the State, have already practically
demonstrated their happy influence. Drunkenness has
perceptibly diminished, debauchery with its inevitable
consequences has given place to a' more regular use of
alcohol, offences and crimes provoked by drunkenness
have become rarer. Nor has the usefulness of the reform
been limited to the preservation of health and good
morals ; it exercises a salutary effect upon the material
resources of the people. The economic progress is con-
firmed both by the increase of the fiscal receipts and by
the inflow of deposits to the savings banks, a double
phenomenon which is to be observed in the four eastern
provinces since the new regulations as to spirituous
liquors have been in force there. . . . Certain details of
the reform have as yet been simply indicated ; others
must be remoulded and completed; the whole needs
attentive management. Nevertheless this vast enter-
prise rests henceforward on a rational basis.”
It should pay some up-to-date Australian legislator
to send to Russia for a copy of the regulations
under which that great empire contrives to be its
own public-house, and try if the system would not
fit Australia. The Australian is intensely con-
servative in many things. British traditions sit
in a large square lump on his chest, and it is diffi-
cult to get him out of the Anglo-Saxon rut except
when he gets out of it in the wrong direction.
Still there is always hope. What a chance there
is for N.S.W. to make the beginnings of a State
Grog Monopoly in the Resumed Area of Sydney,
where some 30 public-houses are now State pro-
perty.
G-old-Dredging in Maoriland.
An embittered Bulletin correspondent writes
from Dunedin (Maoriland): —
The dredging boom which recently smote Otago has
probably absorbed more canny Scotch and other savings
than any previous speculation. Beginning in a small
way, with private syndicates and small companies,
dredges paid handsomely. They had the pick of loca-
tions on the golden Molyneux, and, with small-capacity
machines and an absence of hook-nosed directors and
other extraneous official fry, dividends were paid with
clockwork regularity. Then the famous Hartley and
Riley 00. got to work, a rich lead was struck, and the
yellow metal fished up at the rate of hundreds of ozs. per
week. This fertilised the fever-germs, and in less than a
tick the boom was in full swing. Now was the oppor-
tunity ripe for all the brokers, confidence - men and
experts in the country to get to work, and they didn’t
neglect their opportunities. Prospectuses rained down
on the just and the unjust. Everyone, from the humble
office-boy to the Presbyterian shepherd, took a hand.
Meanwhile, the nimble pegger-out was not slothful.
Almost every mile of almost every river, stream or
rivulet in Otago was staked and applied for, and as this
supply became duly bagged, they went back further and
secured beaches, plateaux and mountains —anything, in
fact. As progress-payments on the dredges were made
and companies (limited liability) pushed for calls with re-
morseless precision, the contributing-stock-sodden public
began to stagger. Shares dropped gradually from premium
to par and even discount. Then the brokers and stock-job
artificers, after a vain attempt to keep the thing going
amongst themselves with the aid of a brand-new stock
exchange and other boodling paraphernalia, had to con-
fess the boom had burst. To increase the gathering
gloom certain new dredges began operations. Had they
been of the Molyneux fleet something might have hap-
pened to restore confidence. But they were chiefly of
the outsiders, dwelling up God-forsaken creeks filled with
boulders, or in narrow patches where the ladders failed
to reach the golden bottom. These after struggling con-
vulsively a few weeks had to liquidate. Also, about this
time some of the side effects of being in a hurry became
visible. Dredge-designers—bustled for plans by super-
heated directors—didn’t care a rap. They had one good
solid design and “ made ’em look just the same ” regard-
less of the nature of the claim or of the size of the
shareholders. And the foundries did likewise. Their
heterogeneous army of mechanics turned out work which,
when erected, tried to jolt itself to pieces for very shame’s
sake.
Now, as far as the dredging industry goes, it must not
be supposed that it is all one prodigious fake. It is per-
fectly legitimate and under proper conditions will, in
many cases, pay handsomely. There are to-day many
claims on the Molyneux and a few other places which will
prove top-notchers in spite of all the boodling, heavy
capitalisation, &o.—when the river goes down. But most
of the £1,500,000 put up as capital is hopelessly lost.
Australia, England, America, and even Germany have
captured some of it for machinery—which will eventually
be used for scrap-iron. The balance has been consumed
locally, and the produce thereof will make just as good
scrap-iron as the imported article. If a claim proves a
failure, and the adjoining one needs a dredge, and can
move it without re-construction, that machine is worth
half its original cost. But taking a dredge to pieces and
re-erecting it on a distant claim is a costly failure. The
liquidators now hover on the horizon like so many vul-
tures, and once let them get their claws in and it’s a
mighty poor feed for the rank-and-file. These are the
gentry who will really benefit by a general collapse. The
system is simple and effective. A co. starts with £BOOO
capital. Dredge absorbs, per original contract, £7OOO.
Within a year or two co. gets to work £lOOO or much more
in debt. How does it happen to hap? Foundries tell tear-
ful tale that contract won’t pay; engineer suggests
subsequent improvements in dredge which abrogate con-
tract, and up goes the price. Then for 18 months or more
seven directors and a secretary gnaw the funds with file-
sharpened incisors.- Is it any wonder the co. starts in
debt ? Then to raise more capital a thousand or two
preference shares are issued, and the ordinary shares are
flattened right out for good under cumulative preferen-
tial dividends—if there are any—and the preferential
claim of the fresh shares in case of liquidation. Then the
gentlemen with the “ gommission aspect” bobs up,
collars the whole outfit for a song, and for the rest of his
natural life dredges on his own account. And before
they have finished liquidating every Moses in Otago will
own a dredge, and the original shareholders will own
little or nothing save a summons for unpaid calls.

[Our Colored Fellow-Subject.
Last year the Queensland Parliament passed four
Bills authorising the construction of privately-
owned railways, and each Bill contained this
clause: —
No aboriginal native of Asia, Africa, or the Pacific
Islands shall be employed by the company, or any of its
contractors or sub-contractors, in or about' the construc-
tion, maintenance, or management of the railway, or in
or about the mineral lands referred to in this Act, under
a penalty of one pound for each such person for every
day during which such person is employed. The onus of
proof that this section is being complied with shall rest
the person or persons charged with employing the
sane.
It also passed a Bill providing for State advances
to sugar-mills, and it contained this clause :
From and after the passing of this Act no aboriginal
native of Asia, Africa, or the Pacific Islands shall be em-
ployed by any company to which any advance has been
or may hereafter be made, under the provisions of the
Sugar Works Guarantee Acts of 1593 to 1900, in or about
any sugar mill or permanent tramway owned or worked
by the company ; and the company and every managing
director, manager, superintendent, or person in charge of
such mill or permanent tramway shall be liable to a
penalty, to be recovered in a summary way before any
two justices, of one pound in respect of each such native
for every day during which such native is employed con-
trary to the provisions of this section.
“Our coloured fellow-subject” from India and
and China, and our friend the Jap. were shut out
in each case under exactly the same penalty. Yet
Governor Lamington cheerfully gave his assent
to the four syndicate-railway measures, while
declaring, almost on the same day, that he couldn’t
give his assent to the sugar - works measure
(containing the same prohibition) without
authority from England! And the British
Government vetoed the sugar-works measure
because it imposed disabilities on our colored
fellow-subject from India, and because it was cal-
culated to aggravate our Jap. ally, while making
not the least attempt to vetof the four syndicate-
railway measures, which did exactly the same
thing. Why ? Well, presumably because the
Philp Government persuaded Lamington to do
so, and pulled various strings to persuade the
British Government to do so. The Philp crowd
doesn’t want to see a beginning made at the ejec-
tion of its cheap nigger from its sugar industry.
But it would rather not see foreign railway-build-
ing corporations employing its cheap nigger on
their railway works, and thereby taking them
away from its sugar industry. Anyhow, the fact
that the clause made no difference as regards the
Railway Bills shows that J oseph Chamberlain’s
explanation as to its inadmissability, for high
State reasons, in the Sugar-works Bill was a lie, or
else Joseph was badly misled. Anyhow, strenu-
ous efforts are going to be made to draw his atten-
tion to the fact that, by law, the British Govern-
ment has still a year in which to put its veto on
the Syndicate Railway Bills, and it is its duty,
by its own showing, to put it there—hard.

The supreme foolishness of building Sydney’s new
big railway station alongside the old one, a mile or
so away from the business centre of the city, was
boldly advertised by the Anthony Hordern fire.
It was as unmistakable an advt. as when that
alleged supernatural hand wrote its message on
the walls of Belshazzar’s lunatic asylum. Here
i 3 the situation condensed:
Sydney is about the most weirdly-constructed new city-
on this earth. There is one narrow street which carries
practically all the traffic that goes out to the grea
southern and south-western suburbs, and one othe'
narrow street which carries almost all the traffic to th e
eastern suburbs. You can get into Sydney from the land
side by crawling through two key-holes. George-street
West is one of them and Oxford-street the other. For
want of straightening and levelling the other exits are
almost impracticable for traffic.
In order to needlessly double and treble and quadruple
the traffic through these two keyholes there is no railway
at all to the big eastern and SiE. suburbs—Bondi, Pad-
dington, Waverley, &c. And the railway to the southern
and south-western suburbs stops short outside the city
proper and pours its traffic into the bottle-neck at George-
street. And this insane system is to be perpetuated by
placing the new railway-station outside the city, just
alongside the old one. It was difficult to decide where
to put the new station inside the city. Half-a-dozen
different sites were proposed. No Government had
courage enough to select its site and stand or fall by its
selection. It was easier to shirk the trouble for the time
by leaving things practically as they were.
Yet the trouble is only postponed after all, for when
the new station is built the question is to be considered
of bringing a branch line into Sydney, as if it were the
wayside township of Goshen Flat, and the fight over the
proper location of that idiotic branch and its stations will
be just as bad as the fight over the proper location of a
main line and a main station. But by perpetuating the
present absurd system the Government gets a little
breathing time.
Sydney found out the complete absurdity of its
railway system when the Hordern fire practically
blocked one of its two street exits, and the city
itself had no railway exit whatever. And the
See-O’Sullivan plan still leaves it with two
street exits and no railway exit.
♦
The best news that N.S.W. has heard for a long’
time is that the See Government proposes intro-
ducing a Minimum Wage Bill during the coming
session. The pitiable revelations made publicly
by Sydney employers to the Victorian Commission
which visited that city, the other day, as to the
starvation wages paid in many Sydney trades as
compared with Melbourne rates, show that a
Minimum Wage Act can’t come too soon. The
Law of Demand and Supply must take a rest.
The said alleged law is an unwritten Freetrade
statute to the effect that the amount spent by an
employer in wages to each employee, and in pro-
viding comfortable or habitable quarters for his
workers, and (if he pays, say, 20s. per week) the
number of hours, whether eight or eighteen per
day, that he may exact for the 205., are matters
which must be settled by competition only,
and with which it is ruinous and absurd
and “ Socialistic ” for the State to interfere.
Of course the employer must pay his adult em-
ployee a wage on which he can exist in some sort
of fashion, and when the Early-Closing Law com-
pelled him to greatly reduce the amount of work
which he exacted for the money, it interfered
quite as badly as if it had started at the other end
and compelled him to increase the amount of
money he gave for the work. As the Law of
Demand and Supply stood one shock, it can pro-
bably stand the other also. As a blessed side
issue, the See Government’s Minimum Wage Law
will make Freetrade impossible. Freetrade can
only exist by sweating the worker as badly as he
is sweated by foreign competitors. No Freetrade
country has ever dared to establish a Minimum
Wage.

The amateur theologians in the Commonwealth
Senate seem to think that adjudication on what
is blasphemous, obscene or libellous is as easy as
flicking a fly off the nose. That admittedly
eminent and Conservative jurist, Sir James
Stephen, of the English Bench, laid it down,
page 105 of “ Digest of the Criminal Law,” that a
man may, with perfect decency and complete good
faith, maintain doctrines as to marriage, the rela-
tion of the sexes, etc., which would be regarded as
“highly immoral by most people,” and yet com-
mit no crime. The Drake Postal Bill will take
the word of some policeman or Customs official
that a book or newspaper of the description
alluded to does commit a crime, and unless
author and publisher are able and willing to fight
a tedious, expensive, uphill battle in pursuit of a
level-headed Judge like Stephen, with but small
chance of finding one, they will have to let their
property go to the post-office fire. Let the Com-
monwealth Parliament apply a test case to
discover where its Bill presses. For instance,
those reputable London publishers, the Mac-
millans, have issued an edition of Bentham’s
“Principles of Morals and Legislation,” in which
many grave topics are discussed, among them
what are known as the “Self-regarding vices.”
Will any policeman, any Customs official, any
clerk or small lawyer let that book through under
the present Bill ?
“Northerner ” gets very near the solution of the
colored-labor problem in Q. sugar industry. The
Bulletin knows it, but is not allowed to tell until
the discoverer has exhausted the possibilities of
the Federal Cabinet:
Dear Bulletin.— The Q. Kanaka labor advocates still
talk about the inability and unwillingness of white men
to tackle sugar-growing field work even at fair wages,
and yet they won’t back their opinions by asking the
Federal Parliament to legislate so that it will be possible
to pay white-labor rates to field hands. This can be done
in two ways; either by the adoption of State sugar-
refining for the benefit of those central or private mill-
owners who would pay an extra price for cane grown
entirely by white labor ; or else by the direct payment
by the State, of a bonus per ton on all cane grown by white
labor. The money for the latter scheme could be raised
by a land-tax on the lands of those who employed or
encouraged alien labor for sugar production. This would
give a direct monetary inducement to use white labor,
and if after, say, two years’ trial the supply of white men
was “insufficient,” “unreliable,” or “unsuitable,” the
pro-aliens would then have just grounds for their opinions
and the public would be with them in any measures
likely to safeguard sugar-planters’ interests, even to the
extent of a restricted alien immigration scheme.
There are scores of white cane-farmers in Q. who would
prefer to use white labor, but who are compelled to em-
ploy the cheap nigger owing to the low prices ruling for
cane. Of course a cane-farmer expressing these views
would be persecuted by his less enlightened neighbors,
and by the mill-owners or other sugar magnates. The
Queenslander is booming black labor by publishing long
accounts of interviews with representative men in the
sugar districts, and this is the way the reported scoops
in his information : He goes to a sugar estate after due
