notice to the manager, who entertains him, shows him
round the estate, and introduces him to some of the
white (?) tenant farmers or the employees. The latter are
questioned (generally in the presence of the manager or
other safe pro-alien)'as to the effects of abolishing black
labor. Of course the reply is in favor of retaining the
nigger, or else the reporter goes to men who have changed
their opinions, and have found out that white men are
“ unreliable ” (at colored-labor rates). These opinions are
then paraded as the unanimous verdict of the sugar
industry.
Years" ago, at a Ministerial banquet, a Q. politician -
now dead—said that when Queensland had a numerous
and “ carefully-instructed ” class of cane fanners they
would be a greater political bulwark against the assaults
on black labor than the large-estate system of his day.
These were prophetic words, and present-day managers
of the sugar industry have evidently had the spirit of
that prophecy in view, because they have systematically
weeded out all those farmers holding “white Australia”
views. So, was it any wonder that the Queenslander
man was able to get so much “ evidence” (?) in favor of a
“ piebald Australia.”
Nevertheless, there are many fanners of the “white”
variety who are obliged to keep quiet, and if legislation
helps them by increasing the price of their cane, they
will be the salvation of the sugar industry on a white
labor basis.

Sydney Lyceum blossomed out last Satur-
day in a new drama called “Serving the King,”
in which the characters are employed mainly in
serving writs on one another, or serving each
other out, and merely talk of serving the King.
Also they talk about serving Edward when
it wasn’t Edward, but Victoria; so that
misleading title falls to the ground a second time.
Otherwise it is the story of a Colonel’s daughter
and her secret marriage to a soldier known as
Harold Ainsworth. Harold has a very nice
mother, but he isn’t spoken to by his father
because latter has got it into his head that Harold
is really the son of a certain small and boyish -
looking burglar. There is no obvious reason
for the old man having this idea ; it just occurred
to him one day as a very possible contingency,
and not having any other ideas at the moment he
let it stay and roost in his brain, and cackle there.
Anyway, the burglar turns up in an unusually
large vestry just before the hero and heroine
appear for their marriage, and, having murdered
the clergyman, disposes of him somewhere about
the wings. Various people stroll into the vestry
after this and remark to each other that they
“heard cries,” or that “something must have hap-
pened; ” but they forget about it between times tul
the villain comes out of the wardrobe, where he
has hidden himself, and denounces the hero for
murdering the clergyman. Whereupon the bur-
glar, who has put in time by marrying Harold and
his best girl, makes his appearance in the deceased
pastor’s surplice. All are so pleased to find that
the sky-pilot wasn’t murdered that they never
think of looking about to find out who was.
The hero goes off for his honeymoon, to be ar-
rested six weeks later, when the absent-minded
congregation has begun to notice that someone is
missing.
• * • *
The hero is not so much concerned about
his probable execution as about the fact that he
isn’t as legally married to the heroine as would be
advisable. Other people also observe cheerfully
that her life must now be considered blasted, and
later on her father-in-law obligingly looks up a new
man to make an honest woman of her, never seem-
ing to think that the original man would do just
as well. Meanwhile, the hero is found guilty and
sentenced to a lunatic asylum, where the maniacs
claw monotonously at the bars and gibber with-
out any variety in their gibber. He escapes on
the day of his incarceration and goes home to find
his wife with a rather sudden baby. She is
scorned by the whole village because the person
that married her turned out to be a burglar and
not a clergyman, and it is understood that the
small boy boo-hoos through her window at odd
hours and calls her “Miss Villiers.” Later,
things are cleared up by the burglar’s con-
fession that he murdered the clergyman ;
he also lifts the ton weight of shame off
the heroine by the announcement that he was
once a clergyman before he burgled. Mean-
while it has occurred to the hero’s father in a
casual way that the hero is his own son, and so
some more shame is lifted off. It isn’t clear
whether or not the hero’s rehabilitated mother
dies in the second last act, when she takes fare-
well of her son among the maniacs. Anyway, she
doesn’t reappear in the last act. Her husband
merely exonerates her in a calm way by remarking
that he now recognises Harold as his own son.
* • * •
A good deal of really bright humor is caused by
Miss Dora Mostyn and by Bert Bailey, who dis-
guises himself as a Highlander with an insufficiency
of provocation and an overabundance of kilt.
Miss Eugenie Duggan dresses rather badly till
her shame comes upon her, when she gets more
style; but all through she lets her voice suddenly
mount an octave higher for the last two words of
every sentence. There is also a good Jew sketch
by Stirling Whyte.
** * *
The great point about Espinasse’s one-act
playlet, “Ivy Leaf,” which preceded “In the
Soup” of late at Sydney Palace, is the strong
endeavor that it makes to be pathetic. And if
Hawtrey, who takes the part of the man on whom
the halo of tearfulness mainly rests, were not
mainly a comedian, and a very good one too, a
wave of pathos might pass over the footlights,
despite the fact that there isn’t really any in the
piece—though there are various other things quite
as good. The hero of this curtain-raiser is a Dr.
Garnet, stout and aged 55, who is in love with his
ward, aged 20. There never is any pathos about
a stout man in love, and when he is baldish into
the bargain, the sympathetic tear is still more apt
to drop in a dry state. Hawtrey does not seem
able to assume the right shade of sadness, and
when his face droops as the nice girl tells him of
her other mash, it is the droop of a man who sees
his new umbrella fall through a grating or his last
whisky drunk by somebody else—not that of a
lover with a crushed heart. The heart of the
medical man, however, cannot be deemed broken
to any extent —even if Hawtrey were able
to assume the proper broken aspect—because
when another nice girl breathes love-vows on the
back of his neck he receives them in a friendly
spit it. It is understood that when she takes
courage and proposes still more definitely, he will
probably yield to his fate. The real pathetic
point in the drama is possibly supposed to lie in
the fact that the doctor will not declare himself
the son of the adopted daughter’s father, as he
will therefore be legally entitled to the coin. If
the middle-aged medical man were played by a
really sorrowful actor, the play would be ex-
cellent in one way; and if he were made wholly
humorous, and not only humorous in mere spots,
it would be excellent in another way. As it is,
it is excellent in a third way, which isn’t either of
the other two ways that it might be excellent in.
* *■ * *
Brough’s “Second in Command” is doing
respectable but not very exciting business at
Sydney Royal. It isn’t a happy play. It is
artistically constructed, and to those who love art
for art’s sake—who can appreciate the workman-
ship apart from the cheerlessness of the materials
—it appeals strongly. It is a good story, and the
plot is a dreadful complication brought about by
perfectly possible circumstances. The heroine,
who is engaged to the man she doesn’t love, and
also to the man she loves, does it with the best
intentions, and quite innocently. They both
abandon her from the very best motives, and her
brother, who brings about the trouble, has the best
motives also. The story is a clever essay on the
utter foolishness of having any but the worst
motives. But its cleverness is possibly not a very
popular kind of cleverness.
# * * -Me
At Sydney Tivoli the Latonas, musical
tramp and slavey respectively, were to have con-
cluded a highly successful season on Wednesday
night, but the donkey, and the selections on the
one-stringed instrument, and the “ Holy City ”
played on the trombone were so popular that the
harmonious dead-beats may be found in the same
old place for some time yet. The Dartos have
really taken hold of the community—the old push
that used to congregate at Chowder, in the day's
before it became Clifton Gardens, now revolves its
donah in the Dartos style, and there is no getting
away from that evidence. There is also a dog-
show just now at the House of the Dispiriting
Curtain.
** * *
The new features of John Fuller’s Empire
are largely feminine and pleasant to gaze upon.
“The Dashing Elmores” have shapely limbs, which
they twinkle to great advantage. Other recentfirst-
appearancers are Miss Julie Cleveland, in a blue
dress, with a clear soprano ; and Miss Edith
Burke, with several dresses and an imitation of
Marie Lloyd. John Leon sings “On the Road
to Mandalay’’—Kipling’s jingle of old pagodas,
the chunk of paddles, flying fishes, and a hurried
sunrise. It isn’t a literary' audience at the Em-
Eire, but it seems to like its Leon when he
.ipples. For the rest, Carney bounds around,
and Yalda does lightning sketches, and several
other people do fast or slow things of varying
importance.
** * *
A “Casino Girl ” boom is steadily setting
in at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. Miss Carrie
Moore’s knee is alluded to nightly at that house
amid great applause, and her Toy duet with the
young doctor who sought for the fair owner of the
knee aforesaid over most of the world is much
appreciated. Mrs. Edouin Bryer also deserves
mention as an operatic Mrs. Malaprop, while
Lauri keeps on informing the public generally
that there are only a hundred girls in the world
for him. There are quite a number of men in
Australia who can truthfully say that they are
quite as exclusive.
** * *
“The Power and the Glory,” with its
large, bright scenery, goes off the Sydney Crite-
rion stage this week, and “ Dangerous Women ”
is billed to go on. No evidence is yet available as
to whether the hazardous ladies referred to are
the wives that wait up for their own husbands at
night with the poker, or the wives who wait up
for other people’s husbands with a low-cut dress,
and a necklace, and the smile that leadeth to in-
solvency. However, this matter will be cleared
up on Saturday.
** * *
Bertram, the Court Magician, opens at
the Palace on Saturday under the management of
Robert Brough. The Bulletin has already said
very kind things, mingled with words of fatherly
reproof, about Bertram during his Melbourne
season, and it hopes that he has taken the latter
to heart. He certainly took the former to heart,
for he reprinted them in his advt.
** » *
The climax of “ Cavalleria Rusticana ” at
Melb. Majesty’sis unaccountably weak, considering
that the story has been twice played in Australia
—as an opera and afterwards as a drama—and the
original stage business is familiar to all old theatre-
goers. The present Italian production seems to
“cut ” the climax in order to give theprima-donna
the centre of the stage. Chorus people rush on
and state that Turiddu has been killea behind the
scenes, whereupon Santuzza falls flat, and the
situation falls flat also. The effect of the one
fainting figure is not to be compared with the
original picture. Turiddu, of yore, used to
stagger into view of the audience, and die on one
side of the stage, with Santuzza, the girl he had
treated so shabbily, weeping on his chest. Mean-
while the teamster, the slayer of Turiddu, had
rushed on, followed by gendarmes who arrested
him for the gory deed. And the giddy wife—this
was the educational touch of nature in the picture
—showed herself much more concerned about the
arrest of her husband than interested in the fate
of her illicit sweetheart. It was on the husband
that she depended for support. Besides, he had
beaten the other fellow in fair He was the
hero of the moment, and an uninjured hero-
husband is better than a dying or dead “mash”
anyday. The climax of “Cavalleria” at the
Princess was a powerful tableau. At Her
Majesty’s it is a bit of ineffective “fat” for the
leading lady.
## # *
Puccini’s somewhat comic opera, “La
Boheme,” is so very new and original that the
first Saturday night applause at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) gave no idea of its immediate commercial
prospects in Australia. Present scribe found
“ La Boheme” an enchanting entertainment from
the latter end of the first act right up to the
impressive climax. The music seems to be a light
network of sweet, elusive strains, harmonising
with a sketchy libretto. One hears the harp
talking prettily amid the gentle murmur of the
orchestration, whilst the melodies come and
go —as Marshall Hall might say. It is
a frivolous, dreamy, pantomimic sentimental
tragic opera bright, silly, sad, and beauti-
ful. But to admire the work effusively one
must accept the French story as Puccini puts it
to Italian music, without complaining that the
gaiety of light-wine-drinking countries has very
little humor in the English sense cf the word.
“La Boheme” is often “comic,” but never cold-
bloodedly humorous and satirical. Call it rather
the same sort of thing in opera as “ Robert
Macaire ”is in dumb pantomime. Macaire con-
cluded with the weird acrobatic decease of Paul
Martinetti. The end of “La Boheme ”is the
passing lienee of the heroine, a painful, Camille-
like death, attended by lovely musical effects.
» • • *
The most conspicuous faults of the first
performance ought to be remedied without delay.
The opera has four short acts, and the waits were
wearisomely long on Saturday. To give theatre-
goers hereabouts sucli gaps of silence and periods
of plush-curtain is to court failure. The wigs of
the four male bohemians are also an absurd error.
The management may be earnestly advised to
persuade the poet, painter, musician, and philoso-
pher to adapt their bohemian disguises to the
taste of a mixed public, which hasn’t read the
French story whereon Puccini’s opera is founded,
and doesn’t understand that these organ-grinder
types of genius are supposed to be living 70 years
ago, when hungry genius possibly did put its
greasy locks into curl-papers at night.
• * * *
“La Boheme” characters in any other
wigs and clothes would sing as sweetly, and their
merriment would appear less puerile to foreign
eyes. The four comrades work, frivol, and starve
in an attic in the Quartier Latin, at Paris. The
poet (he’s the tenor, naturally) forms an easy
attachment to a consumptive Mimi, and intro-
duces her to his mates outside a cafe, where they
all dine together at a table in the street —a chilly
Parisian place for dining on a Xmas Eve, any-
how. Another girl comes along and resumes
giddy relations with the musician (baritone), and
this second act introduces a crowded spectacle
and a big burst of chorus, the only considerable
chorus in the opera.
* • * *
The impecunious geniuses quarrel with
their girls in the 3rd act. Act 4, in the garret
again, ranges from forced hilarity to simple
pathos. Bright girl Musette brings sickly Mimi
to expire on the penniless poet’s little bed. This
is the best-acted section of the opera. The men’s
sadness is more convincing than their attempts to
be funny. Musette’s solicitude is artistically ex-
pressed, and the philosopher who is about to pawn
his overcoat to buy something for the dying girl
sings a droll adieu to the garment ere he consigns
it to the vwnte depiete “spout.”
* * * *
Signora Bassich, a dark, demure-looking
lady, with melancholy eyes, made her first ap-
pearance on Saturday as Mimi aforesaid, and
gave a fine death scene. A clever actress, and a
charming singer. Her soprano is very light, but
strong enough in most places for the dainty music
of the part. Signorina Vaccari, the other new
lady (the Nedda of “ Pagliacci ”), shone as Mus-
ette. Dani, Bozzoli, Lombardi (the Pharoah of
“ A'ida”), and Tondini sang their attic characters
pleasingly, and, when they have altered their
heads of hair, they should give general satisfaction.
The staging was good. And again this scribe
feels tempted to remark that “La Boheme” is a
delightful opera, if considered in a sympathetic
spirit. Next Saturday (20th) brings “Un Ballo
in Maschera,” which will be the last production
of the present season.
«* * *
At the "Bijou (Sat., July 13th.) the Dam-
piers visited “ East Lynne ” upon a smallish
audience. Alleged new version of Mrs. Henry
Wood’s chestnut was endured with dry eyes, for
though “ East Lynne,” thanks to its modern comic
atrocities, is a sadder show than of yore, the
anguish it awakens is rather too deep for tears.
Handsome, portly Lily Dampier depicts that
sullen aristocrat, Lady Isabel, as an unintentional
infanticide. No consumptive child could survive
the course of wild endearments perpetuated by
Madame Vine, so the invalid dies of mother’s love.
Blanche Clements’ pugilistic burlesque of the re-
spectable Miss Carlyle is not convincing, but her
complaints against her aristocratic sister-in-law’s
bad housekeeping are borne out by the smudged
state of the best mirror and the harsh clang of the
bicycle-bell, used to summon flunkeys to her lady-
ship’s drawing-room. Alfred Rolfe infuses a dash
of bank-clerkly skittishness into Archibald Car-
lyle’s appearance, not quite what one expects in
the heavy husband. Edmund Holloway, Ford
and Buller help to fill the bill, and Little Willie is
as human as can be expected, considering he’s
hampered by consumption, long curls, a step-
mother, a family skeleton and an eerie French
governess in blue goggles. On Friday night Dam-
pier sets up “Macbeth,” with himself for the
bare-legged usurper, and Miss Lily as the woman
who walks in darkness.
«* * *
“ A Million of Money ” appears to be
showing a handsome profit at Melbourne Royal.
Bland Holt’s revival is a large, gaudy spectacle,
with five-and-twenty restless gee-gees employed
on the and a cheerful prevalence of
coin among the characters. The only two people
complaining of poverty are a racecourse gipsy in a
picturesque frock, and a degraded husband who
never wants for the price of a drink. This unde-
sirable connection rids the drama, of his presence
by dropping dead in the third act, leaving
his defiant widow to marry a youthful
capitalist, after she has blighted «Walter
Baker’s life for the time being. Nobody goes
looking for work in “ A Million of Money.”
Baker is restored to wealth at the finish through
a mining transaction, which turned up trumps
whilst he was wrecked at sea; and Miss Ross
weeps comfortably under the shelter of her father’s
vicarage during Walter’s absence. Several ladies
of easy virtue continue in easy circumstances, as
far as is known; also the designing Majah is a
villain of substance when he lifts his shiny hat for
the last time. Even the future of Brown, as Dick
Bounder, gives no serious cause for anxiety. Brown
on one occasion loses £3OO to Bland Holt by lay-
ing odds to that amount against White Stockings,
but he meets the difficulty by asking Bland
to settle-up, whereupon the defrauded backer
refers to Brown as a mystery. Later on, Brown
gets engaged to Mrs. Holt, until the lady changes
her mind, and drives him back to evil courses.
Talking of courses, the race for the Derby now
begins in Little Bourke-st., as of yore. The
eccentric starting-gate, stage-managed by two
clowns on stilts, is but a haunting memory of an
innovation that was tried on the audience and
found wanting.
** * *
At the Princess’s, where Premier Barton
and sixteen other people had thed.c. to themselves
last Saturday night, a “funny society-drama,”
entitled “Jim Belmont,” was announced for
Tuesday of this week. To speak quite candidly,
the little world of Melbourne doesn’t much care
whether “Jim Belmont” or “Nurse” is taking
place at the Princess’s for the next night or two.
** * *
The Vic. Operatic Society will have the
use of Musgrove’s theatre for two months, prior
to the arrival of Arnold and Co. On Saturday,
the V.O.S. produces “ Girofle-Girofla,” which
opera was satisfactorily staged by the same man-
agement at the Bijou, a while ago. Miss Ida
Osborne, Minnie Waugh, and other well-known
singers are preparing for the fray, the professional
orchestra will number 24, with Hanstein in the
conductor’s chair, and GO voices can be depended
upon to strengthen the reputation of this great
chorus singing continent.
** * *
Marie Lloyd and the unhappy sacred bear,
also Hurley, Rosie Aquinaldo, Madame Lucretia
(the lady whose dancing offers a good deal of
atonement for her tiresome songs) and numerous
other people are keeping business together at
Rickards’ Opera House. On Saturday next the
periodical Bain will occur again, together with a
new “midget comique ” of the boy persuasion.
The supply of small children for Rickards’s show
is unlimited, and as they already amount to a
thriving industry the application of Peacock’s
Factories Act seems to be called for.

Even this doesn’t express one-half the badness of
the situation, for during the same period £12,606,432
has been borrowed for wholly non - productive
works and casual repairs, which should be paid
out of revenue, and which were charged to

been a deficit every year. Also there is going to be
another deficit this year to a dead certainty.

Maoriland, during the same period, borrowed £4,935,300-
for works other than the usually - productive under-
takings of railways, trains, telegraphs, water, and*
sewerage; but much of this was for the repurchase of
land, or to provide capital for its State Land Bank, or
to raise funds in aid of the Bank of New Zealand. And on
the other hand it paid for a lot of its productive works-
(railways, &c.) out of its surpluses. Altogether there is a
great difference in the two styles of financing. Yet
the only secret is that, over a dozen years ago, Maoriland
decided to levy enough taxation to pay its way and leave
something over for public works, while Australia kept on-
shirking its troubles till now they have reachedi
stupendous proportions.

The Australasian Insurance and Banking
Record of 19th October, 1900, writes regarding the
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society, Limited : “ The
Society can justifiably point to the adequacy of its re-
serves, which are of a magnitude and a character to
command the fullest confidence.”*
* * * »
The Austral Otis Engineering Company have
now delivered one of the water-ends in connection with
their contract with the Melbourne and Metropolitan
Board of Works. The second water-end will be delivered
within a week or two. These water-ends are a very
massive piece of work and reflect credit on the makers.
They are made of a special mixture of iron, which was
subjected to severe tests before being put into the work
and gave highly satisfactory results. Each water-end
weighs about 70 tons.*

All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on up-
to-date lines include a number of Luhrig vanners. The
South and Junction companies each have about a dozen,
no slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Yanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Ooolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Yanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

, Ap a rt from accumulated deficits, this is how
the six States of the Commonwealth have been trying to
Keep up a fictitious appearance of prosperity by using
loan funds to cover non-productive expenditure which
snouirt have been charged to revenue (Coghlan’s “ Seven
Uolomes ' or 1892, page 281, and same work for 1899-
1999, page 823) r
In the last twenty complete financial years there
have been six years of deficit in Maoriland ; nine years in
Victoria, S.A., Westralia, and Tasmania ; ten in Queens-
land ; and thirteen in N.S. Wales. There are some beau-
tiful features about the finances of this great and
prosperous Commonwealth—especially when it is con-
sidered that the revenue has been very greatly assisted
by the proceeds of land sales, that the expenditure has
been relieved by paying an enormous amount of ordinary
charges out of loans ; that there has been very little per-
manent military expenditure, and that the greatest part
of the S’African war expenditure is not included in the
twenty-year period.
* * * •
The Maoriland Government has introduced a
Bill for the establishment of a State Fire Insurance De-
partment, which may be extended to do marine business
also. The capital is to be £250,000 raised by the issue of 3J
per cent, debentures, with power to increase if necessary.
Profits are to be devoted to the repayment of the capital,
so that the State may ultimately have its Insurance De-
partment free of debt and not burdened with any interest
liabilities ; to the establishment of a reserve fund ; and to
the payment of bonuses to customers. It is expected
that a system of compulsory insurance will follow,
premiums being collected in the form of rates. The
Bulletin hereby bets a small sum that the Maoriland
State Fire Insurance Office, if it really starts, will be a big
success.
• • • •
Victoria’s State Land Bank is doing such a
large business that the £lOO,OOO raised last February is
exhausted, and another £lOO,OOO loan at 3 per cent, is
being issued. It is fixed for ten years, and the minimum
is £96. If it sells at a shade over the minimum, this will
be exactly the same as borrowing at 3£ per cent, right
out, and it is an open question if it wouldn’t be better to
do it that way. Certainly it would have been better to
have fixed the loan for 20 or 30 years instead of 10. The
money would have been obtained at a cheaper rate, and
as the State Land Bank has come to stay, it will want it
for a great deal longer than that.
» * • •
On 31st December, 1900, Westralia’s public debt
was £12,227,763, or £67 17s. 7d. per inhabitant. The
Leake Government is now putting through a Loan Bill for
£3,500,000 in order to finish the great mass of works left
on its hands by the Bigjohn Government. When it gets
that money—if it does get it—Westralia will put an end
to the competition of Queensland and South Australia for
the billet of the most indebted State on earth; it will
stand alone and unrivalled. It is proposed to raise the
moiey in London, but seeing that British consols are
bel jw £92 (lower than they have been for 26 years) it is
difficult to see what chance Westralia has got. The one
bright spot is that the year’s revenue is £3,078,033 —the
biggest on record, and £202,637 more than previous year.
But last year, with the smaller revenue, showed a surplus
o £1.'9,721. This time a deficit seems certain. Con-
tingents and dukes are costly.
* • 9 *
Sydney Telegraph, on Hordern’s fire : “When
the loss is distributed among companies having many
millions of capital, receiving millions of premiums
annually, its effect is greatly mitigated. A State insur-
ance department such as some are enamoured of, and
such as is proposed in Maoriland, would have been badly
hit and thrown the loss on the whole of the State.”
The Telegraph actually seems to forget that a State Fire
Insurance Department would pay its losses out of its pre-
miums just as the private companies do. If it did all the
fire-insurance business of the State, it would get all the
premiums of the State, and it could stand all the losses.
A whole occasional loss such as Hordern’s wouldn’t hit a
concern of that size any harder than most of the local
private co.’s are hit by their loss of a thousand or so.

The Bank of North Queensland has had a
reasonably good half-year to 30th June, 1901. Starting
from the date when the B.N.Q. had its great cleaning-
up, at which period the bank started fair, with £lOO,OOO
paid-up capital, things have gone as follows :
Recovered Less 2J per
from Debts Total cent. Divi- Total

June, 1900.. 2557 901 3458 1313 8,154
Dec., 1900.. 2368 1539 3907 1312 10,749

The three half-years specified above were the only ones
in which mention was made of any sums recovered from
the bad debts previously written-off out of capital and
reserves. Assuming that there were no such recoveries
during the other half-years, the actual profits are rather
better than they were at December, 1900, and not quite
so good as at December, 1899, or June, 1900. They repre-
sent £4 7s. 6tl. per cent, per annum on the shareholders’
funds as at the beginning of the half-year (capital,
£lOO,OOO ; reserves, £10,749) which is well up to the aver-
age nowadays. And the past year has been a lamentably
bad one in Queensland. Under the circumstances, the
B.N.Q. has done rather well.
The business has remained practically stationary
as compared with the figures at 31st December, 1900,
though it is larger in most respects than it was at June,
1900. Cash and State securities are £120,492, equal to
over 36 per cent, on all liabilities to the public, which is
an unusually solid reserve. The Bank of North Queens-
land holds £10,500 of Government stock, while its re-
serve fund stands at £11,250, so apparently it has no
fixed rule about keeping its reserve fund all separately
invested in State securities. It would be much better if
it had such a rule. The reserve which is represented by
Government securities—not necessarily Queensland
securities—is the most findable kind in the day of emer-
gency. In 1898 quite a lot of banks had only thousands
of dry unsaleable acres to show for their reserves, and
these weren’t by any means a portable second line of
defence.

The once-large St. James’s Building Society
(Melbourne) is getting near the end of its liquidation, and
there isn’t much left of it nowadays. The depositors re-
ceived 3s. in the £ during the last 12 months, making,
with previous payments, 15s. in the £ altogether, and the
amount due them on 31st March was (with interest)
£12,446. Apparently they may be paid in full, though
that is quite uncertain. In the process of selling oft
the properties in a weak market to meet the depositors’
claims most of the paid-up capital (£215,515) has dis-
appeared. This is the record of the last two half-years ;
bustness Toss

Nominally, therefore, £32,136 remains intact of the capital

But the paper-value of the assets fades so lamentably in
the process of realisation (those sold during last year
alone realised £17,326 less than the values set against
them) that the shareholders may find nothing left for
them after all. Building societies named after saints ap-
pear to be unluckj'. There are only two of them in Aus-
tralia, and neither St. James nor St. Joseph has been pay-
ing dividends of late.

“ B. J.T.” : In reply to your query as to position
of Queensland Deposit Bank. That institution suspended
in 1892, and agreed to repay deposits by instalments with
4J per cent, interest. Up to middle of 1897 it repaid
£104,381 out of £178,369 due, leaving £73,988 still owing.
In 1897, it further re-arranged things. At that time there
was an accumulated deficit of £19,595, which was written
off out of capital. The paper value of the co.’s land was
also written down by £31,000, and advances were written
down by £5195. Altogether, £58,790, or much more than
half the capital, disappeared. The depositors agreed to
accept 3 per cent, interest instead of 4.} per cent., and
to take the balance due them by instalments spread over
20 years (up to 1917). When the co. wrote off its losses in
1897 it left itself, apparently, with a reserve (Interest
Suspense Account) of £3794. Later proceedings have
been somewhat as follows :
Profits on Property
Loss on Sales, repurchase of Net
Business. Deposits, and old Bad Reserves.
Debts recovered.
Sept., 1897.. _ ..£3794
„ 1898.. £2402 £3297 .. 4689
„ 1899.. 748 „ 1522 .. 5463
Profit.
„ 1900.. 193 .. 2452 .. 8108
The earnings for 1900 were only £3 more than for 1899,
but the saving in expenses and the reduction of the in-
terest bill through repayment of deposits made a material
improvement. The amount (how much not separately
stated) made by repurchase of depreciated deposits is, o'f
course, not earnings in the ordinary sense of the term,
and the profit on sales of properties at more than their
written-down value, and the bad debts recovered, are
merely part of the old lost capital coming back. Appar-
ently, however, the Queensland Deposit Bank is now able
to earn enough to pay the humble 3 per cent, interest on
deposits, and over and above that the earnings
are equal to 9s. 2d. per cent, per annum on
the shareholders’ funds as at the beginning of
the year. It is so very little as to suggest that the
assets want a good deal more writing down to bring them
to actual present values, but probably the profits made
on the repurchase of deposits and the other earnings will
gradually fill the gap. When once a co. can do something
more than pay its way its worst troubles are over.

Lake George, N.S.W.: Re-construction scheme
approved by the Court. Proposed new co., 189,000 shares
issued at 17s. 6d. Old co.’s debts about £2OOO. Policy of
new co., to turn to account the large body of gold-bearing

* * * *
Great Central Freehold (N.S.W.) : Stated that
£90,000 is to be spent in developing and equipping this
mine, which embraces a large area of copper-bearing
country in Mt. Hope district. Proposed now to erect a
concentrating plant capable of treating 1500 to 2000 tons
of ore weekly, also to erect a blast-furnace, £50,000 being
set aside for equipment. If all that has been said by the
directors about the size and copper value of the numerous
ore deposits in the co.’s ground is correct, it will be a big-
property, but the need of railway communication will
always be seriously felt. It has to face 85 miles of road
carriage to Condobolin, and it is easy to understand that
a copper mine that would be a div.-payer with the rail-
way might always want calls to keep it going without.
The crowd is developing property at a bad time from a
market point of view, for although it is in a different
State the shadow of Chillagoe will be cast over it until it
asserts its own value by the size of its copper consign-
ments and the number of its divs.
* * » *
Chillagoe Railway and Mines: At last the
public has had some official admissions of the unsatisfac-
tory features in connection with this great undertaking,
in respect to which the writer has made many allusions
and given many warnings. Stated that, while Stewart’s
report on the quantities and value of ore actually in sight
at Mungana is practically confirmed, such appears to be
far from being so in the case of the properties remaining
in possession of the Chillagoe Co. A careful system of
measurement and sampling indicates a serious dis-
crepancy between the figures supplied by the previous
management and those now presented to the board,
and steps have been taken to clear this matter up
beyond all doubt. An independent examination is
to take place, and Stewart is coming from China
to set forth his side of the matter. Admitted, too,
that too much money has been expended in sinking away
from the lodes, and upon a number of small lodes that
might as well have been left to some future time. Too
little lias been spent in sinking upon the ore, to prove its
continuity. No concentration of effort has taken place on
the most important outcrops of ore-bodies, which present
the same appearance as they did years ago, so that they
remain practically unproven, and it has been decided to
spend £lOOO a month in this direction. It all comes
back to the fact that the Chillagoe mines never were
what they were cracked up to be, and that, had it not
been for the strength and names of the Broken Hill and
Mt. Lyell combination which took the properties in hand,
they never would have reached such a market value, and
the public would not have to suffer the present hideous
disappointment. Ten years ago the same group was
examined on behalf of another crowd who knew their
business, but had no names to conjure with. Mines are
not made by men’s names. Chillagoe shares have been
over £2 ; to-day they are 125., and sooner or later, when
shareholders realise the present financial position, they
will probably fall to half their present price. Recon-
struction or another debenture-issue is inevitable. For
the last three .years Tub Bulletin has stood alone in its
warnings in reference to Chillagoe.
• * * *
Mt. Garnet Freehold : “No 1 furnace has been
blown in and is running satisfactorily.” Shareholders
have been largely blown out: shares now under 305., not
nearly half the highest figure reached and less than half
the price they were ere smelting started.

Lake View Consols : June production, 14,1040 z.
from 9166 tons—about 500 oz. above last month’s return,
and the biggest for more than a year. Shares £B, and
have been down to £7l7s. 6d., the lowest they have reached
for many months, and a lower figure than when the mine

* * • »
Brownhill scraped together 51630 z. last month.
Doubtful whether it will be able to get another return of
this magnitude. The tailings, from which 700 to 800 oz.
a month have been got, are finished. Most of the sul-
phide ore is coming from near the boundary of the
Northern Associated. An application was lately made
to work the ore on the boundary’ from the Northern
Associated levels, so that as much ore as possible should
be taken out without danger of encroachment. It was
refused. Remarkable that now this mine has almost ex-
hausted all the ore reserves it accumulated during the
nine months taken for the completion of the Diehl plant,
has completely exhausted the tens of thousands of tons
of tailings accumulated from the old plant, has “ gouged ”
out all its known rich shutes, and is now scraping for ore
against its neighbor’s boundary, shares are higher in Lon-
don than they were when this work began. There is hut
one explanation, that they have been so largely oversold,
and shareholders have been so hoodwinked, thatlittlegam-
blers in them will be squeezed out at a loss, while big
“ bears ” who can weather the storm will probably have the
pleasure of covering at less than half present price, and
even then the shares will be more than double their real
value.
• • • •
Great Boulder: June production, 12,2960z5.
from 7614 tons—an average of loz. 12dwts. 6grs. per ton,
a little higher than usual. Value of gold unstated,
although it has been going down for a long time, and is
now not more than £3 4s. 6d. per oz. taking it all round.
Hence probably the rumor that the gold-ores of the field
are gradually changing into silver—a nonsensical state-
ment to make, for, as the Boulder manager has declared,
the silver content in the deepest sulphide level is no
higher than it was in the oxidised ores near the surface.
The lode at the 700 ft. in the Golden Horseshoe is less
than 20ft. from the G. Boulder boundary, but it might as
well be ten times the distance. The Great Boulder has
prospected for that lode so much with a diamond-drill at
that and deeper levels as to prove that it will
never have the pleasure of giving the wanderer
a welcome home again. The Boulder has been singularly
unfortunate in losing shutes of rich ore, due to the way it
was pegged out, despite little W. G. Brookman’s 'big
assurance that he had spent a great deal of time and
brain to allot each lease so that it would have the lode
properly within its boundaries. W.G.B. was largely
applauded for the statement, and when it takes a long
time to prove a mine a duffer, or a man a fool, it does not
much matter. The Ivanhoe lodes, too, every one of them
get poor just a few feet from the Boulder boundary at
the north end of the Ivanhoe lease. There is a probability
of the Boulder getting some of the Ivanhoe middle lode
below the 400 ft. level, as the shute is dipping northwards
and is already 60ft. in the Boulder ground. The lode on
the Boulder western boundary, opposite the main shaft
and now known as the main lode of the Horseshoe, and
from which the great bulk of its gold is got, went into
the Horseshoe and became much wider and richer than it
ever was in the Boulder ground. The Boulder also lost a
rich shute at the south end of its ground. It pitched
into the Main Reef mine, and it is from this rich shute
that that mine has produced its gold and paid its diva.
* * * «
Great Bing-all: Go3Goz. for June. Crushed
3673 tons for 47110 z ; 28 tons of concentrates for 3230 z •
and IOOOoz. from 20S0 tons of tailings. Shares, £9 10s ’
and likely to go higher. This seems the best mine in
W.A., from an investor’s point of view, according to all
the accounts writer has received. It is simply an old-
fashioned quartz-reef, averaging 17ft. thick and about
Boz. value in the lower levels, and needs nothing but an
up-to-date stamp mill and an ordinary cyanide plant to
work it. Not so interesting, from a metallurgist’s point
of view, as the Kalgoorlie mines, but much more satisfac-
tory from the view of a shareholder. For long after the
mine was taken overand its value had been established in
the lower levels shares were standing at from £2 to £3.
The failure of the Day Dawn, as it was once called, and of
other mines on the field, scared people away, and it was
not until value had been proved by numerous crushings
and permanency established by opening up lower levels
that shares began to move. They were not long going to
£6, where they remained for months. They then in a
few weeks jumped to over £9, where they are likely to
stay till another lift has been opened up. Meanwhile a
bigger plant is going up, and should next lift prove as
good as the last these shares are bound to see a big rise.
But writer had better stop, or he will be regarded as a
tipster with shares to unload.

rights of an adjoining lease have been bought.

“Vestal”: Re “She Cat’s” remarks in
Bulletin about Melb. Women’s Hospital trouble,
it strikes present writer that a return to the
system of address in vogue up to 100 years ago
would be the best way out of the difficulty,
because it was then the custom to call all adult
women Mistress (Mrs.), whether matrimonially-
yoked or not. Thus Mrs. Smith’s daughters,
when they got out of the schoolroom, would be
Mrs. Deborah Smith and Mrs. Jane Smith, and
when Mrs. Smith (senior) died, the eldest daughter
would have as much right to style herself “Mrs.
Smith ”as her brother’s wife. Curious to note
how the terms Miss and Mrs. have changed values.
About the times referred-to, the former appella-
tion was the dubious one, a nobleman’s mistress
being referred to as Lord So-and-So’s Miss, while
the rigidly-unmarried girl was entitled to be a
Mistress. The Melb. Hospital Committee asserts
that it cannot honestly label a woman married
when she isn’t; so it seems that, after all, it is
not woman who is hard on woman,” but merely
“English as she is spoke” nowadays.

“Samaritan” : Cocks is a quaint little
Sydneyite, a true religionist, who for many years
has given all his spare time and all his worldly
substance to establishing and maintaining his
home for cripples On its behalf he holds
forth at Sydney street corners and in the Domain
on all possible occasions. One of the old man’s
charges is a granddaughter of the Duke of Kent
by his first though unofficial wife, and she has re-
cently been in Sydney hospital for an operation.
The lady is in very poor circumstances, and Cocks,
with other friends, wrote on her behalf to the
Duchess of York, sending portraits of the Duke
of Kent and his unofficial wife, and other family
heirlooms, as evidence of the true, though mor-
ganatic relationship. The Duchess replied,
through Secretary Wallington, to Cocks’ letter,
expressing sorrow for the “sad condi-
tion of Mrs. but “the Duchess is
not quite able to see how the Royal family
can be held responsible.” (Writer quotes from
memory, and has omitted Cocks’ quaint and
sarcastic interpolations; the old man read the

England. To think of it ! ” Toe thought nearly

quite. He can still find a word of scorn to hurl at

Bulletin sincerely hopes that the unworldly old
man will say nothing likely to get him into
Darlinghurst for high treason or “foul libel” or
whatever his offence may be named.

Ararat (Vic.) Borough Council decided to
give a welcome home to returned soldiers, but
were divided as to whether they should have
drink or ladies present —it being admitted that
both were impossible. Drink, of course, carried
the day.

Even the poor pretence of patriotism is
dropped now ! An ex-Vic. Bushman complains
loudly in Melb. Aoe of his rejection for one of
those 1400 railway billets now offering, and goes
on—
When I arrived home I remained three months on the
invalid list and was then discharged, to get a living as
best I could. Being a working man, I had to return to a
hard bush life, with scant pay ; but I worked on, buoyed
up with the hope of Govt, employment at the first oppor-
tunity. Although physically and mentally fit for the ,
position I was rejected—and now I am a member of the
unemployed.
Well, he got a trip to S’Africa, on better wages
than he could make at home, was invalided on
full pay till quite recovered, and then was actually
left to get a living as best he could—just as almost
everybody else has to do. Does the returned
eohentiugenter expect to be an able-bodied pauper
all the rest of his days ? It really seems so. The
cohentingent business is bringing its own antidote.

and co. to a practical monopoly of Govt, billets

* * * *
The fire at Anthony Hordern’s mammoth
shops, last week, provided the biggest sensation
in Sydney for years ; and, sad to say, five lives
were lost. The fire was discovered between 8 and
9 a.m., and vast clouds of smoke rolled upward
from the Haymarket end of the city and drifted
heavily south and east. Then the news went
flying far and fast. Every street was filled with
a crowd rushing to see the burning of Hordern’s.
Cabs began to pour along, and later, the ladies
of the plutocracy drove up in their carriages and
leaned back and watched with opera-glasses.
There was smoke, smoke, smoke everywhere,
and showers of sparks and embers. Coats were
having holes burned in them. A chiffon-trimmed
hat burst suddenly into fire, and the distracted
woman tore it off. Someone gave her a handker-
chief, which she wrapped round her head —then
the crowd swallowed her up. On it rushed through
mud and slush, past smellful houses where Chinese
clustered and jabbered, till it surged round a
corner and caught a glimpse of the grey towering
building whose top was lost in rolling clouds of
smoke, through which red tongues of flame flashed
spasmodically. The embers were falling like rain.
The crowd had stopped—no one knew why ; but
it felt instinctively that there must be no pushing.
When someone started to shove, a dozen voices
threatened grimly to roll a tram-car over him.
Then there was a backward movement, and sud-
denly a score of voices howled that the big gaso-
meter, close by, had caught fire and would blow
up. Next moment the crowd was streaming back
up Campbell-street, carrying before it the" other
crowd that was rushing down to see the sight.
Seen from Belmore Park, the spectacle was
nothing compared to an ordinary bush fire. The
chimney and tower streamed with smoke, and the
whole huge block of masonry was a crater, but
there was wonderfully little flame to be seen. The
vast windowless wall on which the name ap-
peared was turning black, and cracks were begin-
ning to make their appearance. Explosions and
crashes shook the air as the great flooded floors
went thundering down, and sent up fresh bursts
of flame and clouds of sparks and burning frag-
ments, that floated away to Oxford-street and
Woolloomooloo. Gipps-street .was filled with
the smoke of the steam fire-engines ; but now and
again it fissured into vistas through which waved
the puny streams of water. It looked like trying
to quench a haystack with an ear-syringe. Fur-
ther down, towards George-street, could be seen
dim shadows with bright brass helmets, mounting
ladders and pointing tiny streams of water through
windows. Only the bright helmets were real—all
the rest were shadows, outlines, intangible forms
that trembled a moment between the walls of roll-
ing smoke and then disappeared in it.
The man had a catch in his voice, and his eyes
were misty: “They say there are five deaths,
but his was terrible to see : You see that parapet
there, cracked with the heat! Well, that’s where
he was when I saw him first. The smoke was
rising from the roof in little streams like the steam
rises from a tiny hot spring, but all those windows
below there were filled with fire. He waved his
hands, and, bending down, shouted something to
those below. They sent up a cheer, and shouted
to him that he was seen, and some people went
forward with a tarpaulin. He stood up and
seemed to wait silently. Then smoke blew
all round him, and I couldn’t see him
for some time, but there seemed to
be someone else beside him. The fire
poured out of those windows in one great sheet,
and the boom of a falling floor echoed above
everything. The flame swept upward and curled
right over the parapet where he stood crouching
behind the ornamentation, just barely visible.
Then smoke rolled round him and it was all
blotted out. People sobbed, and some fainted.
The smoke rolled past and the man reappeared.
A cry of joy went up and men rushed forward
with a tarpaulin, but the firemen shouted to him
to hold fast till the ladders came. They came at
a gallop and swung through the crowd like a flash,
and in another minute, amid deafening cheers,
ladders were run up against the wall, with a fire-
man climbing nimbly upwards. And then—the
ladders were too short. The flame sank down;
had the ladders been long enough there was a
clear moment in which the poor fellow could have
got past. He bent forward, with his hands clasped,
and looked down the sheer 100 feet of empty
space that lay between him and life. Even tnen
a line, could it have been got to him, might have
saved him, though the parapet must have been
almost hot enough to have
scorched a rope through. A
fireman was shouting something
to him about holding on a
little longer, when another
floor fell, and once more
a sheet of flame surged
out of tlie windows
and hid him and the
parapet in a red veil. Then a louder crash
than ever was heard ; clouds of smoke and
torrents of flame sprang skyward, for the roof had
fallen in, and the man stood on the naked wall on
one side of which was the fire while on the other
was empty space. The flame soared up above
him and his clothes caught fire. He flung his
blazing coat from him, and another red tongue
licked round him. He reeled a moment and cast
his burning hat away, and then in his torment he
stood straight up and sprang outward—turned
over, and then flashed down —on to the roadway.
He fell all huddled up, like a bundle of clothes,
and ”
The man’s name was Henry Clegg ; he was a
packing-hand who had apparently delayed too
long, trying to fight the flames. Four other men
were killed ; two who had seemingly been near
Clegg, and two who were caught by the fire while
busy in the basement keeping up the supply of
water for the hose. “ Christs of the Age of
Trade! ”
The total loss is estimated at £500,000 ; insur-
ance, £360,000.
Hordern’s fire directs attention to what seems
to be the only trustworthy provision for escape
from a burning building—iron staircases on the
outside. On every building, at the side where
there are fewest windows, there should be con-
structed from the roof to the ground an iron stair-
case with a landing and a door at each floor.
That door should be fastened by bolts only, and a
municipal inspector could examine the bolts
regularly to see that they are well-oiled and work-
ing easily. Had the Palace Emporium had such
a means of escape (an example of which can be
seen on Messrs. Holdsworth and Macpherson’s
building in George-st, Sydney) at least three
lives would have been saved, and other
people would have escaped injuries received
through descending by ropes. Fire Supt.
Webb has stated, in connection with the Hay-
market tragedy, that the Brigade has no
reasonable chance to save life in buildings more
than 90ft. high, and that people who construct such
edifices, and the companies which insure them,
must take the responsibility for loss of life.
Therefore, on these buildings it is especially
necessary that the landlords should be compelled
to construct such staircases as is suggested. The
staircase need not necessarily come to the ground,
but it should" come within a few feet of it, to a
point where the ordinary fire appliances can re-
move people expeditiously and without difficulty.
Sydney Sunday Times came out with an
amazing picture of the unfortunate Clegg. The
horrible thing represents the poor doomed man in
one of the most beautiful of A. Hordern and Son’s
up-to-date suits—39s. Gd., 2 A per edit, off for the
winter sale —standing behind the parapet with
one hand resting gracefully on it and the other at
his temple saluting the crowd in approved
military style.

The sensational little scullery maid of
Auckland Grand Hotel broke up badly in the
end, as might have been expected. She stuck for
two weeks to her story about the three strange
men who had a habit of glaring in at the win-
dow, and who then took to breaking-in casually
at night, and telling her in deep hoarse whispers
that they were going to burn the premises and all
tbe people therein, and who poured combustibles
over the house, and compelled her to carry
messages to the boss-incendiary while they were
lighting the match ; and about the six men who,
after the fire, followed her in the streets with a
simultaneous goose-step, and threatened to dis-
courage her with an axe if she informed on them.
The police always had doubts about this yarn, for
Auckland isn’t a very large city, and if Jessie
was pursued up and down the streets by
six miscreants the matter would have been
noticeable. Jessie states quite affably now
that there was nothing at all in the story, and she
has specified the sixpenny novelette that she
copied it out of. She still says that she met three
people in the houao that night, but they were
former employees, and it is inferred that they had
dropped in harmlessly to see the housemaids that
they used to be mashed upon. The police, how-
ever, “ believe that the girl is still keeping some-
thing back, ” so it would probably be best to search
her box at once, and see if she has another novel-
ette concealed about her. Probably she will ex-
plain later on that her first lie was quite true, but
she was followed by 14 masked men with battering-
rams, and terrified into retracting. If Jessie had
started the usual tale of “assault,” some man
would probably be doing seven years by now.
That is an easy kind of lie, in which there are no
witnesses, or apparatus, or complications ; the girl
merely says the man was there, and the man
says he wasn’t, and the fool jury, despite the
stupendous mass of woman perjury that is con-
stantly in evidence, believes the girl. Jessie
Minns unfortunately tried her hand at an
elaborate lie with six men in it, and conspiracies,
and bottles of combustibles, and midnight irrup-
tions, and she got left. But she was quite as
truthful as the average woman who brings accusa-
tions against men.
Most sensational narratives of the J essie Minns
type are stolen out of books. If an elaborate
index of current literature was kept at some
central place—say Sydney or Melbourne—in which
the plot of every new novel, novelette or news-
paper serial was classified, it would greatly
simplify the work of the police. It would only be
necessary to index the striking crimes, names and
mysteries in each work. For instance, when
Jessie’s tale was wired to Australia, the central
bureau would have looked up its index for
“Incendiarism inspired by Motivesof Vengeance,”
and by referring to the books in which that
episode appeared it would have been able to wire
back “Jessie’s story taken bodily from ‘Flash
Fred, the Miner,’ ”or some such work. Failing
that, the box and premises of a surprising witness
like Jessie should always be searched and her
dog-eared literature overhauled. In nine cases
out of ten the book will be found on the premises.
The typical Jessie is a creature of neglected
stockings and short-lived emotions, and her tale is
generally extracted from yesterday’s ghost book—
not the ghost-book of last week or last year.
Further light on the methods of the casual
Australian girl who brings criminal charges
against men was forthcoming at Cobden (Vic.)
police-court on June 28, when a respectable farmer
was charged, on informations sworn by two of
his daughters (aged 13 and 19), with having crimi-
nally assaulted them on specified dates. The
wife, who said she had had 11 children in all,
expressed implicit confidence in her husband, who
had been “both mother and father” to the
children when she had been away nursing. The
younger girl (Emily), she regretted, was untruth-
ful, while Anna had a violent temper, and she
added, naively, “ I have no reason to believe
Anna is immoral, but I know she goes to dances.”
When Miss Emily was cross-examined she ad-
mitted that the charge was trumped up, her sister
Anna, after a quarrel with her father, having
induced her to make up the whole story, to the
truth of which both had sworn before the police.
Charges promptly dismissed, but, of course, the
inventive young damsels go unpunished for their
revolting perjury.

“Zoo ” to The Bulletin : —
When in London I used to look askance at the street-
sweepers army—grimy, sodden-ragged, semi-human am-
phibia who dredged from dawn to dark with yard-brooms
through quagmires of slush. But during the last couple
ol weeks I have been impressed with the need of such in
oydney, the streets of which at present take the cake for
abject slushiness. New York rejoices in a machine like a
tumbril, with an open paddle-wheel at one end. This
perambulates the city five times a day in wet weather,
and carries off the mud bodily. The contrivance may
possibly reach Australia by the time it has been super-
seded in its native land by something better.

* * * *
The collapse of the Australian Economic
Association was not the calamity Mr. Richard
leece the other night deplored. It began, had its
middle, and ended, as a mutual-admiration body,
with the late Dr. Garran on the mantelpiece.
1 here was neither blood in its veins, nor nerves in
its body. It died principally because it should
never have been born.

The Victorian honorary magistracy repre-
sents nothing but cupidity and stupidity, and
continues to howl about the Factories Act. The
other day a Williamstown butcher was called
up for paying 6s. a week to a young man who,
under the Act, was entitled to 30s. “How long
has the man been in the trade?” asked one J.P.
t x> W0 years >” defendant. At which the
J.P. seemed half-smothered with righteous indig-
nation and pious disgust. “ It’s a villainous Act! ”
he. cried, horrified at the idea of an employer
being compelled to pay a grown man who had
been two years at the trade 30s. when he could
get him for 6s. If Premier Peacock had half-a-
i chara ? ter he would ring off a few of these
globular magistrates who show their unblushing
partiality by vicious abuse of the laws they are
called upon to administer. Generally when the
J .P. squeals it is because he finds the law com-
pelling him to tread on his own corns.

Writes “ Ignis ” :
Dear Bulletin, —Would you allow me, through your
columns, to remind the N.S.W. public of the existence of
the local Cremation Society—offices, Otis Chambers, 82
Iving-st., Sydney. Annual subscription—one shilling.
Briefly put—the advantages of cremation are that it pre-
vents possibility of pollution of water or air, obviates the
necessity for large and costly cemeteries, removes the
chance of being buried alive, is less expensive than earth-
burial, and interferes with no religious rite. As regards
the possible destruction of the traces of poisoning, the
society advocates legislation to make incumbent a search-
ing inquiry in every case of death, “ with a view of deter-
mining- the necessity, or otherwise, for a coroner’s inquest
before interment or cremation."

Astonishing the agility with which the
Law gets round a difficult corner when there’s
money beyond ! Morris Sleeman, of Melbourne,
lately handed his son Tobias £l5O to bank for him.
lobias banked it, but in his own name, and then
proceeded to prodigalise. So Morris took steps
to recover his good coin. He couldn’t draw it out
and start fresh because Tobias refused to sign the
necessary cheque, and he couldn’t sue Tobias, be-
cause Tobias was a minor and the Law didn’t
consider him capable of being sued, or signing
cheques or having an account at all. In fact, the
jLaw reckoned that, as a commercial man, Tobias
didn t exist. But it thoughtfully provided a way
out (with costs). Application, it said, must be
made to a Judge to appoint a guardian for Tobias,
and then Morris Sleeman might sue the guardian.
And, of course, he has to pay said guardian, and
the guardian s lawyer, and his own lawyer.

According to a witness who gave evidence
last week before the travelling Victorian Board,
the flock that the Sydney man’s nice soft bed is
stuffed with mostly comes from the Moore Park
tip. The dirty rags are gathered there by dirty
men and women, and, without any process of
washing or disinfection, are rushed into one end
of a machine, and come out as flock at the other
end. * ‘ Haven’t you any Board of Health here ? ”
gasped the Commission, turning pale as it thought
of the bed it had slept on the previous night.
“Yes,” was the reply, “but dynamite wouldn’t
shift them.” Wherein the witness did the Board
a wrong. If the See Government will pass the
necessary Act of Indemnity, and O’Sullivan will
put a sum on the Estimates to supply the
dynamite and the fuse, and somebody will hold
the Board steady, The Bulletin will guarantee
that no flock will come from Moore Park tip in
future.

About two escaped criminals :
By the escape of prisoners Sparks and O’Connor from
Pentridge prison, the possibility is opened of some fine ad-
venturous bushranging. O’Connor is an ordinarv footpad
of the suburban-garrotter type, but Sparks is a man of
very different mould from the average criminal. He is a
sturdy, good-looking, well-educated fellow of 27, and has
the brains to conceive and the daring to carry out
schemes for the redistribution of wealth, which would
simply “lose” nine-tenths of the choicest ruffians in
Pentridge. Both he and his comrade, Negus, with whom
he was convicted, are men of force of character unusual in
their class, and the way they carried out their robbery is
an object-lesson in daring crime. Unlike so many of
their class, they did not regard the job as finished with the
mere stealing of the swag. Masked and mounted, they
bailed up a Rutherglen mine manager and compelled him
to hand over £l2OO, intended for the payment of wages.
Then they galloped off across a paddock into the bush.
The intervening wire fences to the number of a dozen
had all been cut before hand, so that the robbers had a
straight run to the spot where they crossed the Murray
into N.S.W. Abandoning their horses at the river, they
crossed in a punt and mounted two more horses tethered
in readiness in the scrub, took short cuts through numer-
ous fences by more wire-cutting and, 20 miles from
the border, picked up another relay. Turning round
they crossed back into Vic. at Cobharn, and tooklrain to
Melbourne, where they were subsequently arrested
“from information received.” When they re-entered
Victoria, this pair carried no money, and, as it was not
recovered, it is plain that they must have planted it
during their ride. Unless “ information received ” has
unearthed it meanwhile, Sparks will probably have
secured that £l2OO by now. If it is gone, the man who
got it had better keep out of Sparks’s way, and so had
other people who carry money and dislike bullets.
The escape of Sparkes and o‘Connor recalls the dis-
appearance of recicLiviste Pierre Douar from same prison.
He was a splendid mechanic and is supposed to have
made a very fine steel file, or saw, and cut through the
bars of his cell window, hiding the traces of his night’s
work by patching the bars with black putty. Outside, he
worked his way through the barriers with a skeleton key,
followed the tracks taken by the present escapees, and
clambered over the low wall facing the Merri Creek.
While hiding in Fitzroy Gardens, clothed in a suit he had
stolen from a house in Coburg, he met a criminal whom
he knew, and they arranged that they should take a room
together at the Melbourne Coffee Palace, and with
skeleton-keys “go through ” the rooms in the early
morning. The accomplice went to Bourke-street and
took the room, but as he came out detective Macmanamy
arrested him for stealing two watches from Kenny’s
baths, and to save himself, he offered to give up Douar.
The offer was accepted, and as soon as Douar was safe iii
the room the other man went down and gave
Ward and Macmanamy the number of the room and
cleared. The detectives went up, knocked at the door
and when it was opened, rushed in, knocked down and
handcuffed the occupant. The accomplice, however
was next day arrested for theft, taken to the city
lock-up and lodged in a cell, when he found his
only companion was Douar 1 The recidivist# sprang upon
his treacherous mate like a panther, and but for the
timely arrival of the watchhouse-keepers would very soon
have finished the man. A week after, Douar strangled
himself in his cell with his trousers-strap. The treacher-
ous mate led a dog’s life in Pentridge, and had to be re-
moved to a country gaol.

Why does not some energetic member
of N.S.W. Parliament move for the production
of that long-suppressed report on Norfolk
Island, on the condition of the islanders under
Col. Spalding, adding to his motion a desire for
full information as to their condition at present
under Magistrate King? N.S.W. Parliament is
responsible for the welfare of these people, but
successive Governments practically suppress all
essential information.

Dear Bulletin,— What a pity the money
squandered over the York razzle-dazzle wasn’t
used td start a fund for building a fever hospital
for Sydney ! Here is a chance for the O’Sullivan.
At present, the children of his pet hobby—the
working-man—have to be bundled-off 10 miles to
Little Bay as soon as scarlet fever develops in the
household ; and the working-man aforesaid grum-
bles at fate and things medical, instead of insisting
upon a demoralised Government erecting needful
health-institutions in place of emblazoning itself
with flags and fireworks—at the working-man’s
expense. —M. B.

OMARESQUE.
Omar of old Iran,
Well unto thee ’twas known,
Even poetic Man
Lives not on Love alone.
Thou wouldst in desert bide,
Deeming the desert fair,
If thou hadst by thy side
The Loaf—the Jug—and Her.
Though the Beloved She
Gome not—l shall not pine,
If Allah leave with me
The Loaf, the Jug of Wine.
V.J.D.

A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, J uly 15, 1001.
My Dear Arint.—
It has been a fiery week. That disastrous blaze
on Brickfield-hill has really painted the town red.
And everyone you meet was there, and saw it after
his own fashion. A wet, grey sky, spanned from
east to west by a low-lying rainbow, was the back-
ground of the awful picture as I saw it, an hour
or so after it had been on exhibition. A towering
brick square, its windows filled with flame, and
from its roof great pines of fire growing sky ward.
I thought of Heine’s conceit about the huge pine
(or was it a cedar?) that he would dip into the
crater of Etna, and with that red pen write his
girl’s name across the sky. Strange frescoes
wrought by the gaoled fire appeared on the walls.
Violet prisms, yellow-ochre stars, and long, jagged
white tracings. They grew and changed colors
and forms, and all the time there were men burn-
ing behind those frescoes. And all the time the
hose looked like scent-squirts sending up their
streams into the fire that jumped out of the win-
dows, as if to welcome what could do it no harm.
But it was that “bow of promise” in the sky
that gave the satire to the picture. The promise
that there shall not again be too much water.
And all the time there were men burning.
Madam Janson, en route to M.L. that after-
noon, was at the fire and spake certain things re
the inefficiency of our fire-appliances compared
with those of N. York. Madam saw a ballet-girl
burnt to death on the stage once at Stockholm,
and the awful memory of it chases her yet. It
was in “Robert the Devil” opera—the Nuns’
Dance—you remember, they are nuns outside,
ballet-girls beneath. The girl’s drapery took
fire, all her fellow-dancers tied from her, and
before rugs or rescue could be found the girl was
dying.
But even the Brickfield-hill tragedy had its
humours. An old woman of about 60, with a face
that a “Rocks” pugilist would have disdained,
clothed in muddy rags and a remnant of hat,
wished to pass a barricade and a handsome police-
man. She entreated and expostulated in vain;
then in disgust: “Ah, if I had me feathers on,
and me flowers, and a long-tailed gown to howld
out o’ the sthreet, and a touch o’ paint and a bit
o’ white, ye’d let me pass quick enough.”
Miss Minnie Hordern, who, with some of her
kin, watched the fire in the morning, in the after-
noon went bravely through her part in the Pope-
Einuey wedding. But she showed traces of the
shock she must have felt. Miss Bertie Fiunev,
youngest daugater of warehouseman Finney, of
Brisbane, married Mr. Norman Pope, a son of
the partner of Farmer and Co., and Miss Minnie
Hordern was bridesmaid. The bride has been
noted as Brisbane’s most elegant dresser. Her
presents were on view in the dressing-room of the
Australia hotel, where the wedding breakfast was
also held.
Twenty-three-year-old genius Gerardy is filling
the Town Hall with entousiastic crowds and the
music of his ’cello. The world’s leading ’cellist is
a good-looking lad, who really has not the big
mouth that his portraits give him. Whilst he
plays he snakes his front locks over his forehead,
like Madame Darto. He comes here ahead of a
cyclone of European praise, which proclaims
“ there is none like him—no, not one,” so Sydney
will scarcely say, in effect, “ our own ’cellists ha\ e
much longer tails.” When Gerardy was 11 years
old he appeared in trios with Paderewski and
Ysaye. He has a trunk full of diplomas, and, as
Russia, Germany and France have decorated him,
he must have a good show of medals and orders.
Yet he is so modest, he wears none of them. The
instrument he plays is a btradivarius, and cost
£2OOO. In private he is very melancholy, and
pines constantly for his best girl, who is
beautiful and lives in America. But then Gerardy
is oidy twenty-two.
Miss Olive Kingston, the soprano who sings at
the Gerardy Concerts, is a Marchesi pupil, bhe is
a tall, slender, oval-faced olive-complexioned girl
(it’s not often a girl grows up to fit her name so
acculately) with great ruffles of dark hair round
her head and ears and throat. Her first public
appearance was at Melb. Town Hall—she is a
M-jib. girl—with Gerardy, where she got as good
a reception as she had here on the great ’cellist’s
opening night. The pink silk gown coveied with
silver embroidery which Miss Kingston sometimes
wears on the platform is a Paris frock. It is a
glittering gown but accentuates the sombre
coloring of her face and of the great web of hair in
which she wears no ornament. She ought to carry
the effect of glitter right up to her top knot, then
the audience wouldn’t be worried by a too obvious
bright bo ly and nebulous head.
And now it has struck people that the Barton
box was not manufactured for its recipient. That
is proved by the three feathers on the lid. The
Duke had no right to the badge, and the man who
once had the right now sports an Imperial Crown.
Hence it appears that the Box and other feathered
gifts are some of Albert Edward’s old stock, given
to his son to be worked off on the Australian tour.
Still the old principle—anything’s good enough
for the Colonies !
Prince Alfred Hospital is going to raise funds
by means of a Bruce auction. You give some
article which you don’t recly need —be it a grand
piano, or a tin of kippered herrings, a cottage, or
a bundle of unpublished poems. When a great
number of people have given something they
don't yearn to keep, a big auction thereof will be
Held in Sept, for the charity. All the people
who give something they don’t need will presum-
ably be at the auction to buy the thing some-
one else doe n’t need. The instinct to get some-
thing in return for your cash lies deep as original
sin in the human heart.
Mr Abraham Cohen, late of Tamworth, the
father-in-law of baronet Samuel, has just died
in London ; and it is presumed that the baronetcy,
which was poor, will now be fairly endowed. The
veteran N.S.W. Agent-General, father of the
present baronet, was twice m irried, first to Miss
Levien of Geelong, an aunt of Mrs. Felix Levien
and of Mrs. Forbes Angus, and later to Miss
Isaacs of Auckland. His first wife left several
children. Louis, the son who would have got the
title had he lived, was married to a sister of the
Miss Fowler who is engaged to Dr. Ralph
Worrail, of Sydney. Old Saul Samuel’s widow
lives in London with her one offspring, Randolph.
Whatever the veteran had he left to them.
The present baronet was a stock-broker here when
he married Miss Rae Cohen, of Tamworth. They
were on their way from Australia to visit her
father when he died.
Mr. “ Willie ” Beattie, the best of the singing
clerks in “Florodora,” leaves for London this
week. He takes with him (along with a host of
good wishes) a three-act drama of the time of
Marie Antoinette. “Madame Millefleurs” it is
named. Local good judges who have read it §>ro-
phesy its acceptance in London.
Miss Iva Muriel King, who was married, a few
weeks ago, to Mr. R. A. Mackenzie, of Glades-
ville, has, for an Australian, a long, long pedigree.
Governor King, w T ho ruled in the days of the
System, was her direct forbear. She is a grand-
daughter of the Hon. Philip King, M.L.C. Miss
Annie Dulhuuty, of Bathurst, lately married to
Mr. George Sutherland, of Gowan Station, has
also a snap of “descent,” being a great-grand-
daughter of Col. Gibbs, who was Collector of
Customs in the time of Gov. Gipps.
Mrs. Spencer Brunton and Mrs. Boeson have
called a meeting of friends of Mrs. Fred. Aronson
for this (Thursday) afternoon, at 3 o’clock, at the
Hotel Australia, to make arrangements for giving
her a “ farewell ” and address. Mrs. Aronson,
who, with her husband and two children, is leaving
Sydney to settle in Melbourne, was for some years
editress of the Woman’s Department of Sydney
Mail.

dance at Leigh House on Thursday, 25th July.
Dancing from Bto 1, and tickets ss. Hon. Secs,
are Messrs. J. A. Preshaw and C. A. Moore.
