From Brisbane I hear that at the Yacht Club
ball southern girls were well to the fore. No one
has had a letter from Lady Lainington yet, and
lucky will be she who first finds favor ; or will the
discreet ex-Governoress write to all and post on
the same day ?
A friend who trapsed around Duking at Rotorua
writes me that the Duchess was attended by
Sophia, the well-known old Maori guide, but the
other guide, young, good-looking Maggie Papa-
kura, annexed the Duke, who brightened up im-
mensely as she ogled and flirted with him. “Her
hair was beautifully dressed with huia feathers, a
fine mat was around her tall, straight figure,
leaving her shoulders bare. She carried a green-
stone mere in her hand, but the tout en-
semble was spoilt by Maggie sporting a
pair of fashionable high-leg buttoned kid
boots. Honors were divided between Maggie and
Sophia. Maggie had the Duke, the boots and
youth on her side; Sophia, old and wizened, but
interesting as the heroine of the dreadful eruption
of Tarawera years ago, had been surreptitiously
taught the’real Court curtsey before the Duchess
arrived that day, by a lady who was staying at
the Geyser Hotel (of which institution Sophia is
one of the features), and Sophia made the curtsey
to the Duchess, and looked tomahawks at Maggie,
who knew it not, and felt that, despite the kid
boots, she had ‘gone one better’ than her young
rival.
“ We all stood round while the Wairoa Geyser,
the big gun of the place, was soaped, to make it
play. Two bars of soap were Hung down its boil-
ing throat. It objected to the emetic, and in two
minutes a magnificent column of boiling water
and steam sprang a hundred feet into the air,
making us all—Dukes and Duchesses, Ministers,
journalists, and private secretaries—scurry to a
safe distance. Pohutu, the other big geyser, had
been playing splendidly before the Royal Party
arrived, but, in spite of a dose of soap, it refused
to perform, and sulked all the time we were
there.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Lord Hopetoun has broken down, as it was
expected that he would. The responsibility of
looking after poor little George, and the fears
concerning the terrible things that might happen,
have smashed his nervous system—only tem-
porarily, let us hope. Nobody is allowed to
see him at Government House except Premier
Barton. A passing glimpse of the poor
man in the garden at Government House shows
him frail, drooping, hollow-cheeked, dull-eyed
he has just shaken off a Royal nightmare. But
the Countess —her troubles about worries ! She
should have been the man of the family; in-
deed, she is. Her Sexellency, the Governess-
General, ought to be the dashing lady’s title.
“ The vice regal l op without ti e Ruby, just now
(so Col. Campbell told inquirers) laid up with
influenza, and the lesser lustre of sister Leslie,
who is away North at present, was wanting in
interest. The hostess of Rupertsvvood was missed,
too, from the gathering; the rest of the Clarkes
were there in force, especially the Dowager who
danced like a juvenile. Her white satin gown
was a marvel of the sartorial art; it had grand
layings-on of black lace cobwebs, and walls of lilac
on each shoulder, diamonds everywhere. A Lon-
don lady was the most conspicuous object at that
official hop. Site is quite six feet tall, with thin
features, and small piercing eyes; she wore a
superb gown of moonlight spangled stuff, and pro-
menaded the room with majestic strides, her train
flowing out yards behind her, a tall lorgnette
raised to those singular eyes of hers. One has
seen the strut in the fashionable show-rooms of
Worth or some other big dress-architect. Another
Londoner, Mrs. Nigel Balfour, a guest of
S’Rupert’s wife, was as dainty as a bit of Dresden
in shell-pink silk with black lace fixings. Miss
Duret, of the beautiful scarlet mouth and hour-
glass figure, showed her native taste in choosing
shrimp-pink satin severely made. Mrs. Percy
Chirnside flourished the diamonds of the night.
Across her black satin bodice, she wore a thick
constellation of big diamond stars —a window-full
of the best of her stock.
“ The wife of a military officer wore a tricky
creation in black and white, and had a colossal
mounting of millinery shavings on her prema-
turely grey head.
“ Out of a bunch of debuting girls, Miss Nora
Power (daughter of the sporting Herbert) had
strong claims to prettiness. As had also Miss
Elaine Molesworth (daughter of the sporting
Judge), and a daughter of Alfred Deakin—who
ventured into the world about which her parents
have cared so little. Col. Campbell pioneered a
‘coming-out’ set, and gave a military partner
to each member of the Lily-of-the- Valley series.
“At Flemington on Wednesday, despite the
wet, gloom and cold, Sassiety turned out in
droves. A new pen had appeared, like a thief
in the night, beside the old one, and blue-and-
sulphur decorations specified its object. There
the Gov.-General’s party and a fraction of the
Duke’s suite sat in state, and in the other one—the
old, shabby, over-shadowed one—the local vice-
regalities quietly and in a very humble spirit took
their gruel. ‘ For a little while,’ as the Scripture
saith, ‘you shall see me, and again, in a little
while, you shall not see me,’ and thus it is with
the small Excellency.
“ The Countess of Hopetoun, positively bright
in manner, and amiable in chat, sat with the
magnificent Viscount Crichton (of the Family
Herald figure, flaxen moustache, and single-eye-
pane), the Duke of Roxburghe, and others less
considerable, or accompanied them in thin high-
heeled shoes through all the slush of the betting
Bedlam to the bird-cage. An Eton costume of
serviceable grey and mountings of stitched white
bands, and a toque of tartan in violet tones, with
a thick border of the flower of all modesty, was
the brightest rig-out Melbourne has yet seen the
Countess clothed in. Both Mrs. Derek Keppel and
Mrs. Philson wore black; and Mrs. Corbet, the
only other woman of the party, was large, lacy,
rollicking in black and festive blue.
“The magnificent Viscount was putting in a
good time, getting home on favorites, and punch-
ing the Pnysician-extraordinary of the party in
the ribs. Sometimes the lengthy Adonis skipped
down the Terrace, sometimes hooked the heavy
Duke of Roxburghe’s arm and stepped out like a
show Guardsman, and whatever way he went
proved a fellow of fooling and youthful larks.
This dispelled some of the romantic fire the
Viscount’s dignified and imposing presence at the
State ceremonies had lighted in undraped bosoms.
The Viscount, off the Royal chain, takes the air
of freedom like an overgrown terrier.
“On Wednesday ‘Gor Save’ was the signal
for the Gov. General’s contingent to set off. They
took their time, too, in leaving. After them, the
Maddens moved away, hundreds assembling at the
end of the Terrace to see the style of things as
they are done now. Pilot Croker saw the three
carriages of the big party put off first, and then
returned to do the honors for the smaller craft.
Corinthian Croker’s lawyer wit never deserts
him.
“Poor ‘Pagliacci,’ all passion and pathos, fell
upon miserable weather in Melbourne, and in con-
sequence, suffered further chill from public
neglect. It would take a woman all her time to
drag fireside smoke-oh man to Italian Opera these
nights, and just now the average she, conscious
of chapped lips and ehilblained ears, condemns
frivolity, basks in the bonny, blithe blink o’ ‘her
ain fireside,’ and poses self-righteously as aeon-
firmed home-comfort.
“T’other night when Lady Hopetoun, Mrs.
Derek Keppel, a Duke and a Viscount, sampled
‘Pagliacci’ they began the evening in the right-
hand box. Half-a-minute in that cave of winds
and the Governor-Generaless was gently massaging
her nose-tip, while her lady-friend hugged herself
in her wraps. Lord What’s-his-name turned up
his collar, and the other title-wearer chased
an usher to say that satiated with sport
(after their day at Flemington) they were
in no mood for a draught-tournament.
Then a rescue-party piloted the shakers to the
opposite box—a bad spot for seeing, and seemingly
none, too snug, for the Duke (Roxburghe) con-
tinued to bail-up attendants with complaints
against intrusive breezes.
“Lady Hopetoun, in flung-open black pelisse
and trailing skirt, was impressive walking round
the d.c., but black, absorbing as it does much of
the wearer’s color, makes the dark Countess look
worn and sombre.
“Coiffure novelties get little support from
Britain’s best, who differ in this respect from
Australia’s Naicest. Lady Hopetoun’s black hair,
arranged exactly as it was eight years ago, coils
simply at the back, just where the head starts
down-hill. Her fringe is quiet-going, her side-
tresses only slightly waved and scarcely puffed-
out at all.
“ The funny-man of Lady Hopetoun’s opera-
party laughed at lots of things, amongst others at
Marcomini for looking so fat as Lola, the Caval-
leria coquette. Not that Lola’s plumpness is out
of the picture. The former belle of the village is
now a young matron, a teamster’s wife with little
to do, apparently, but flirt and fatten; besides,
Lola is meant to be very winsome and fascinating,
so according to Bulletin creed, she’s sure to be
stoutish.
“ Lady Hopetoun still clings to her old custom
of anxiously fluttering the leaves of her pro-
gramme in search of clues to the conundrum of
Italian opera. The other night one of her guests
came to the rescue, but as he started to read
through his single eye-glass from the very be-
ginning of the book, the titled slow-coach was
always miles behind current stage-situations.
“Hard luck that 4 Pagliacci ’ and ‘Cavalleria*
were handicapped by the fog-fiend, for the short,
sharp sentiment and music, alternately fierce and
frivolous, of these modem Italians, just about hits
Australian taste, given favorable conditions.
Said strenuous operas, less stilted and stagey than
compatriot predecessors, are not so figurative and
metaphysical as German musical allegory. Re
Wagner, was there ever such a tea-and-bun
‘atmosphere’ for moral tragedy as that Tann-
hauser hall of song ?
“Harrie Ireland, bad angel of Bland Holt’s
current production, gets into a close-clinging all-
black evening gown, which shows up her tall,
slight figure as own cousin to a whipping-post.
Quite a relief when the wearer veils her narrow-
ness with a long cloak of white brocade, flounced
with black lace. Later, this stage syren looks
plumper and, consequently, more dangerous in
fluffy amber. Yellow goes well with the Ireland’s
ruddy hair, the blend suggesting autumn-tinted
foliage. f
“The robbery of tenor Larizza’s diamond rings
and tilings from his bedroom seems to be num-
bered with the felonies that fade away into silence
after making one appearance in print. Signor
Larizza, whose titular prefix, Cornmendatore,
sounds more nautical than musical, lodges in
Drummond-street, Carlton, and has a valet person
to prepare liis meats and maccaronis for him. The
other Italian Opera people are mostly quartered
at Drummond-street boarding-houses, where they
enjoy the use of the kitchen for their own private
cookery purposes. Buxom Signora Benedetto is
blessed with a husband —a pleasant, little fair
man. Signorina Marcomini is said to be a genuine
spinster, and a much younger spinster than she
looks on the stage. Neither Larizza nor the other
tenor Dani can speak two sentences of English,
but the Cornmendatore, at any rate, has been
heard to say ‘ I lofe you ! ’ to a pretty girl over
a garden gate. With that limited knowledge of
the language a foreign tenor can get along nicely.
“If a recent letter published in the Argus
states all the facts of the case it is evident that
Archbishop Carr has not undertaken to start a
real Foundling Hospital for Victoria. The letter
aforesaid, written by the hon. sec. of a Melbourne
‘home’ for convalescent spinster mothers, com-
plains that the Foundling Hospital will be closed
to the ‘sinner’ whose unlawful infant is a second
offence (or upwards) against the law of Mrs.
Grundy. But it is hard to believe that the R. C.
Archbishop thinks of shutting the new hospital’s
gates against the unmarried parent of a number 2,
3or 4 baby. Even the Women’s Hospital gives
bed and board to the ‘hardened minx,’ though the
Dianas of the committee say, in a snortful tone,
that the unwed mother of a frequent family is
‘ coming it pretty strong.’
“Queen Victoria Hospital, the one that is run
by women for women, managed to get £1450 out
of the York grovel. Subscript ons for the
‘ Duchess’s Welcome,’ or whatever they called it,

“An attractive feature ahead is the Melb. Dis-
trict Nursing Society Ball, to happen in Prahran
Town Hall, Wednesday, Aug. 7. A fashionable
committee, which includes Mrs. Simon Fraser and
Colonel Campbell, is working hard to make the
function a success. Their Excellencies, Major
and Minor, with their respective followings, are
booked to attend.
“The Melbourne University medical students
made ‘a night of it’ last Wednesday. They
attended the performance of the comedy ‘ Nurse,’
and afterwards painted the town red. They made
so mucli noise in the theatre that the rest of the
audience could hear nothing, and Miss Maynard,
from the stage, had to appeal ineffectually for
order. Numbers of them were semi-drunk, and
many of them thought it manly to pretend to be.
Writer saw four fights in the streets, and one at
the railway station.
“ Saturday’s show at the Albert-street galleries
was quite a sociable and chatty afternoon for
artists and their appreciative friends. Painters
and sitters prowled around arm-in-arm, studying
the result of their mutual labor with grinning
satisfaction. Sassiety was either at Flemington,
or nursing its influenza in Toorak mansions.
There could be no sales, however, for it would
have been a gross slander on most of the visitors to
credit them with a stray sixpence to buy anything.
There wasn’t a costly rag in the room, though
Mrs. Tom Roberts looked happy and successful in a
cherry-red pongee, and Mrs. McCubbin brewed tea
in a cheerful velvet art gown, also red.
Yours affectionately,
AKENHI.
The latest device for swindling the gentle
consumer is a German-invented contrivance by
which churned milk can be pressed into butter, so
as to increase the weight by about 25 per cent.,
without altering the appearance of the article.
Sundry London providers have been smartly fined
for selling butter thus “faked.”

The twenty-third anniversary of the death
of John Dunmore Lang, political, religious and
social tribune, is at hand. He died in Aug., 1878.
He was one of the fathers of Federation, but
wanted Federation to go hand-in-hand with Sepa-
ration from England.

well-known as a Bulletin b:«G), now edits the
Q. Labor journal, and will also probably “have a
dash ” shortly.

William Small, of Ryde, xs.o.W., is one
of the oldest natives of the State. He is 94 years
of age, and has lived in the same locality all his
life, and for 60 years in thq house he now oecu-
pies. His 10 children but one are settled at Ryde.
On one occasion he went outside Sydney Heads,
but that has been the limit of his travels.

Chief Justice S. J. Way, Bart., caused or
allowed to be floated across Rundle-st., Adelaide,
a banner bearing on each side this modest and
characteristic inscription : “Chief Justice Way,
on behalf of his fellow-citizens and himself, bids
their Royal Highnesses a Loyal and Hearty
Welcome.” But why “his fellow-citizens ?
Also, why mention them before “Himself” ?

The announcement that Gov.-Gen. Hope-
toun is engaged in a three weeks’ effort to get
back the health which he lost on his last voyage
to Australia, recalls the story concerning the
birth of his fragile but plucky lordship. Hope-
toun, it is said, came into the world by one of the
most successful Caesarean operations that surgical
history records. In an ordinary way of speaking
he was never born at all.
The “ my learned friend ” gag, as prac-
tised by the lawyers of the Eederal Parliament,
has reached the “sickenin’” stage. Now, re the
supposed “ learned ness ” of the lawyer. A few
years ago some 50 or 60 candidates went up for a
police-magistrateship exam, in -Sydney. These
were nearly all lawyers, with a few C’s. P.S. and
two school-teachers. Out of the 12 who passed
only two were lawyers —the two dominies being
placed 4th and sth.
A certain democrat plays up the old game
in Melbourne He tells all and sundry that he
has no dress-suit, but he gets “there’’all the same.
He even managed to shake the Duke’s hand, tell-
ing while doing so that he had not a dress-suit.
He also introduced his daughters to the Duke,
and likely repeated that he had not a dress suit.
When the philosopher in rags reproached Plato
for his decent attire, Plato replied, “I see your
vanity through the holes in your coat.”

“ Firenza ” : When Bill
the Smelter (late W. H. Os-
mand, Vie. M.L.C.), was in
Florence some years ago with a
party of Australian shipmates,
he had a bad quarter of an
hour. The Italians use the
leaves of some wild plant as
garnishing for oysters in the
shell. The party was late for
dinner at the hotel one even-
ing, and the oysters were gone.
Bill said the leaves referred to
were put on the table as salad,
that he knew all about Italian
salads, and that he intended to
hive some. His friends had
doubts. Bill questioned one of
the waiters, who didn’t know
in the least what the question
was, but bowed out of natural
politeness. Bill helped himself
freely, and had made great
progress, when another waiter
rushed up and snatched his
plate way, .saying the contents
were poison. The consequences
were disastrous ; they need not
be particularised. There was a
waiter who had suddenly dis-
appeared from the room, and
there was a man with a very
pale face remaining in it who
was thirsting for that waiter’s
blood.
One Hamilton, of Tiratu
(M.L.), claims that 30 years ago
lie bought from the Maoris all
the land from Takapu to Mana-
watu Gorge—about 500,000 acres
—for 2s. per acre ;he produces
the deeds and insists that
the •'deal was bona-fide. His
anxiety to gain possession of his estate is quick-
ened by the fact that the land is all now closely
settled and of great value. It includes several
flourishing townships, in which land is selling'at
£3O per foot. Hamilton’s claim recalls a greater
one made long ago by an American whaler named
Webster, who declared that he bought all Maori-
land from its “ original possessors.” Webster’s
pretensions were, however, never taken seriously,
though he several times attempted to enforce
them.
That brainy and industrious lawyer, Louis
Horwitz, of Hamilton (Vic.), is a sticker. He
stood for Portland electorate in Vic. Legislative
Assembly at general election last year and was
beaten by Ewan Cameron (one of the 17 “traitors”).
Next wooed the Wannon seat at elections for
House of Reps. Samuel Winter Cooke was
elected by over 1000 majority; Horwitz and
Cussen dead-heated for second place. Now he has
just returned from unsuccessfully contesting S. W.
Cooke’s vacated seat in Leg. Council. The
electors of Western Vic. have spent a lot ot time
lately in crossing “ Louis Horwitz” off ballot-
papers.
Chief Justice Way and Senator Symon, of
the shiny bald head, are by no means “ cousins ”
now. The coolness is not paraded, but it is
there all the time. Symon originally had a
small practice in the south-east, but Way,
recognising his ability, brought him up to
Adelaide and took him into partnership.
They worked up a splendid business, and
then Symon started on a trip to Eng-
land. He had no sooner got aboard
the steamer than Way snavelled the post
of Chief Justice. As it is contrary to the
etiquette of the profession for a lawyer
to sell his practice when elevated to the
bench, it looked as if the fine business
of Way and Symon would go to the dogs.
Way, however, wired to Symon at
Ceylon to return at once to Adelaide.
He did, and it is claimed by Way’s
friends that therefore Symon owes all
he has to the “ little Chief.” Symon
thinks otherwise, though exactly how
Symon thinks isn’t really known. Any-
how, a rare and beautiful feud exists
between the pair.

“it wasn’t the best thing our Lord did !”
The Bulletin’s London correspondent:—
Some pars from antipods abroad. A tripping “booky’
is knocking the mildew off Englishers he meets by his
contemptuous comparisons of things here and “ out in
Australia.” A Bond-st. clothes-chopper so shrivelled up
under Barney Allen’s compliments when returning three
rough-hewn suits “made to measure” that his collar
fell round his ankles and hobbled him. Donald
M‘Donald wouldn’t give a Cape Leeuwin shell-fish
for four Londons and two Windsor Castles. On the
other hand, Louise Mack is absorbed with London’s mys-
terious fascination and struck a payable lead with a pro-
minent magazine a few days after sighting the white
cliffs.
Australians and quasi-Australians in Lon-
don : Last mail reports Phil May recovering from
pleurisy; Patchett Martin seriously ill with
dropsy. For the other side: Florence Schmidt,
concerting at a good salary, has great hopes of
Covent Garden and Grand Opera; Louise Mack
is beginning work with the best prospects; Syd-
ney painter Joe Wolinski, whose “After Life’s
Fitful Fever ” was purchased for N.S. W. Gallery,
has found a patron for his music as well as for his
art, and is in high feather.

It was said before Glebe (N.S.VV.) Court,
when Harry M‘Connell proceeded against R. E.
Wilkins for challenging him to a duel, that such a
prosecution was the first in 98 years. Not correct.
In 1844 Henry M‘Dermott, a Sydney alderman,
challenged Robert Lowe (the late Lord Sher-
brooke) to a duel in Sydney, whereon Lowe had
the alderman promptly bound over. In the Bur-
wood case, tried at Glebe court, it was stated that
Wilkins, with his second, actually went to the
proposed meeting-place, but his opponent did not
turn up.
Thomas Farnell, who,died the other day
at Ryde (N.S.W.), wasbrotherof the late N.S.W.
Premier, James Squire Farnell. The Farnells,
going back a generation farther, have seen the
beginning and end of many families and Govern-
ments in N.S.W. When J. S. Farnell first
became Minister of the Crown he was surrounded
by a Cabinet of robust men. All these are dead
except one—R. P. Abbott. All who were Minis-
ters when he entered Parliament are dead. The
Cabinet over which he presided as Premier, how-
ever, holds several of its faces still to the sun—
Supreme Court Justice Cohen, ex-Justice Foster,
J. F. Burns and W. H. Suttor. Of these Cohen
and Burns are still workers, but out of political
harness.
About murdered missionary Chalmers :
The Chalmers massacre has, of- course, been published
in various ways all over the civilised world, and probably
is just about the best advt. that missionaryism has got
for years. “ The blood of the martyrs is the seed of the
church” —the subscriptions flow in when it flows out.
Various personal considerations connected with “ Ta-
in.ite’s” exit are being much preached upon just now.
Just before last Xmas Mrs. Chalmers took ill and died.
Out of her own private monej' she bought a new whale-
boat and presented it to her husband. Granted that it
was her last bequest. Granted that the veteran mission-
ary himself onlj r used that boat once —on the occasion
when his head was cut off by cannibals. Granted that
his young coadjutor died with him. Granted that his
pet dog was shot by a Govt, officer in mistake for a dingo
at the Mission head-quarters. What does it all come to?
The man’s time had come ; that’s all. Old man Chalmers
was a Man as well as a missionary. There was no cant
about him. He would take his whisky with you and tell
a spicy yarn as well ; but if ever he was called upon to
do a service for a human being (colored or white, it was
all the same to him) he was there, and then the Christian
showed beyond the man.

Deceased Melb. lawyer M‘Kean had once
the best law-library in Victoria, though he was
not an erudite lawyer himself. He attended all ■
local law-literary-sales, and generally secured the
choicest books in t'.ie catalogue. The late attorney
M‘Gregor, of the firm of McGregor, Ramsay, and
Brahe, was also a collector, and after his death
M‘Kean bought the bulk of his valuable library.
A dead-beat strolled into Melbourne Insol-
vency Court one day and, addressing a reporter
as “ Dear chappie ! ” requested the loan of a pen.
He was so insinuating in manner that he was soon
supplied with writing materials. “ What’s .the
Judge’s Christian name, old mail?” he asked.
They told him, and he wrote this little note :
“Dear Hickman, can you lend me five shil-
lings?”—folded it daintily and forwarded it
to his Honor by the court messenger. When
Hickman Molesworth J,, read the letter, he
looked puzzled to begin with, then gradu-
ally grew red and angry and fixed his eye
threateningly on the reporters. The messen-
ger, summoned to his Honor’s side for a whispered
confab, designated the real writer, at whom his
Honor gazed ponderingly for some time as if to
make certain his correspondent wasn’t a rich
relation in disguise or sich. When he had con-
vinced himself that there was nothing of the angel
unawares in the stranger’s appearance, the Judge
smiled genially, but slowly shook his head. Poor
Dead-Beat got up, bowed politely and left the
court.
“ Been There ” writes about C. Hudson,
lately selected as general-manager of the Tas-
manian railways:—
Tasmania’s new railway-boss was for many years in
M.L. railway service as auditor, (list, manager, and finally
assistant general-manager. His promotion was rapid,
partly through influence, partly through ability. He has
had Seddon’s right ear for many fruit seasons—in fact, he
has had it so much that it began to be doubtful which of
them the ear belonged to. Hudson is rather under
medium height, wears red, closely-trimmed heard and
moustache, and has perched on a somewhat tilted nose a
pair of gold-rimmed specs. His appearance is aggressive,
and his manner likewise. The present M.L. Railway
Classification Act is largely his work. It is a most un-
satisfactory measure (for the officers particularly) as
Hudson managed to insert so many “ hedging ” regula-
tions that the management can justify any appointment
it wishes to make, in face of the printed list (D 3) showing
division, sub-class, grade, status, salary, order of pro-
motion, &e., of each officer and wages man, which has to
be placed before Parliament with the railway estimates.
The management (through Hudson’s efforts) had reserved
to itself the right to ignore or vary the order of pro-
motion to practically any extent. Maoriland railway staff
has already adjourned bodily to the nearest pub to drink
fervently to the prayer that his vine and fig tree may take
permanent root in Tasmania. Hudson is » smart man all
the same.
N.S.W. general elections were responsible
for this masterly exposition of policy—from the
inevitable alderman: —
To the Electors. —Doubtless some of you will be won-
dering what has become of me—Old Gilliver. If so, the
fact is, after addressing a few meetings, I find myself in
my old friend Dr. Traill’s hands for repairs. Trouble—a
real beauty of a cold. He says it’s imperative I keep in-
doors at nighttime. The alternative, a ride to Rookwood.
That being so, I have no alternative other than address
you through the medium of the press. First, I would ask
you to support me as a solid man of the world, who is not
seeking Parliament as a means of livelihood. 1 claim to
be the only candidate in sympathy and touch with the
electors. Some candidates talk a lot of tarra-diddle to
you during election time. Do they know you when they
get there ?
I must appeal to you to accept or reject me on your
knowledge of me during the past, in my capacity as one
of the largest employers of labor in the State.
I again wish to say I am a Liberal, and if elected it will
afford me the greatest pleasure in assisting to oust the
party now in power, for usurping behind the backs of the
people the powers of a Czar, and scattering broadcast the
contents of a more than well filled Treasury chest.
See ad. to follow. Meanwhile, jours truly,
Wm. Gilliver, Mayor of Rankstown.
And yet letters addressed to Gilliver, M.L. A., at
Parliament House won’t find him ! He still
sneezes as a private citizen at the old address.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.—ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
A distinctly aboriginal incident helped to
brighten the visit of the Yorks to Rotorua (M.L.).
An old chief stood in front of his tribe elaborately
got up—bare.to the waist, hair dressed in warlike
style with liuia feathers, a kind of flax kilt about
his waist with a drapery of red cloth under it, and
a battle-axe held upright in his hand. Facing the
royalties and the assembled white public he
looked eminently respectable in his draping,
but when he turned his back to the stand a yell
of laughter shook the welkin. The dusky one,
as regards the rear portion of. his anatomy, was
in “ the altogether.” The seat of the mighty (he
weighed about 17st.) was dressed only in a tattoed
inscription. It was a beautiful inscription, and
the great man wasn’t going to hide his family
crest because of any foolish Pakeha prejudice in
favor of clothes all round. The weighty old
warrior was so calmly unconscious of anything
wrong that it was impossible to be really shocked,
so, after a preliminary blush, the great audience
studied the memoranda on the back part of its
fellow-subject with some interest.
* » * *
The Maoris who gathered at Rotorua “ to
see the Duke ” included some hundreds of chiefs
and all these travelled first-class on the railways.
One well-dressed Maori said to the station-master
at Greymouth, “ I go to Rotorua to see te Tuke.”
The s.m. said that was all right; Parata (the
Maori M.H.R.) had left a ticket for him.
“First-class?” asked the Maori. “No, second,”
was the reply. “Oh, too long to sit on te hard
seat,” said the old fellow, “all te rangitira go
first-class. I no go ! ” and he left the station
bursting with indignation.
MAKING IT SOUND LARGE.
BUSH PUBLICAN (to tourist): “ Wot’ll y’ave—
Ram, lamb, sheep, or mutton?”
The Mt. Egmont (M.L.) Mountain House
is some 3000 ft. above saa-level. It is, of course,
abandoned in winter, but the piano, dragged up
there with immense labor, is left behind. During
the first two seasons it was found that the intense
cold of winter snapped the strings. Now, when
the house is closed at the end of summer, the
piano is wrapped in blankets and soldered into its
original zinc case. This keeps the strings from
breaking, though, of course, by next season the
weapon badly wants tuning.

Love-making in the bush. Scene, the
dining-room of the “ Travellers’ Rest.” Dave and
Cis discovered, the former sitting at one end and
the latter at the other end of the long table.
Room in partial darkness—one solitary smoky
kerosene-lamp in the middle of the table.
Audience peering in from the billiard-room, bar,
kite l en, passage and other places. Dave (peering
round the lamp): “ D’yer luv me, Cis ? ” Cis
(bashfully): “Yaaaiiis!” Dave: “’Ave yer got
a’art, Cis?” Cis: “Yaaaiiis!” Dave: “Give it
to me then, Cis.” Cis : “ Oh, 1 couldn’t ! ”
Dave : “ But yer got mine, Cis, an’ yer wouldn’t
’ave yer Dave walk up the street without a ’art?
would yer now ? Stryte ? ” Cis (defiantly) :
“ Storp gittin’ at me. If yer luvs me woy don’t
yer mug me ? ” Simultaneous bursts of laughter
from the billiard-room, bar, kitchen, passage and
other places. Lamp put out suddenly. Total
darkness.

“Alpar”: Recently went out ’possum-
shcofing. Shot one, and my two dogs worried it
be) ond recognition. I sat on a log with my gun
(at full-cock) beside me, across the log. After the
banquet the dogs started to fight, and the smaller
one, getting the worst of it, jumped up beside me
for protection, got his foot fast in the trigger-
guard, and discharged the gun, the charge shatter-
ing the other dog’s head. Believe it is the only
case on record of dog shooting dog. Not a “fairy”
either.
• * * •
“ P.X.” : Yes, the Afghan is getting on.
Every camel-train used to have attached a couple
of riding-camels, used by the boss in looking out
for water and feed, and so on. But lately several
of the trains have been equipped with a bicycle
or two; and the riding-beast is turned out to
grass. Of course a bicycle has for a long while
been a serviceable part of many an out-back
teamster’s gear.

“ Malle ” : A recent electioneering ex-
perience. Reached a wayside pub. where I was
advertised to speak at 6 p.m., having to be 10
miles away at 8.30. There were only two men
and the landlord waiting for me, but I decided
that by spouting I might make three missionaries
who would sing my praises through the locality.
The landlord “had to look after the bar, but
would pop in now and again.” One half of the
remaining audience moved the other moiety into
the Chair, and the chairman introduced me with
great solemnity in a 20-minutes speech. I then
spoke for half-an-hour, was accorded a unanimous
vote of confidence, and we adjourned for drinks.
As I was about to drive away, I was warmly
thanked for my effort, “and,” said one, “we’d
both vote for you, mister, only neither of us has
got a right.” I didn’t say anything !

“Abdul” : River Darling is now lower
than at any time since the eighties. You can
not only walk across dry-shod, but can walk
dry shod down tlie bed for 200 and 300yds. at a
stretch, both above and below Louth. For long
stretches it is as dry, if not as dusty, as the
Bourke-Wilcannia road —no longer a river, but
just a chain of waterholes. A slight rise reached
Bourke recently, but the weir blocked it there.

Frequent sudden deaths of cows in the
mining district round Maldon (Vic.), attributed
to cyanide poisoning, led one man to prospect the
stomach of his late lamented heifer. He found
47 pieces of stone (some 3in. xfiu.), 11 pieces glass,
9 pieces tin (someiin. xljic.), and 13 pieces broken
crockery. Had the animal lived a little longer, it
would presently have laid a tin of condensed milk
every morning.

The aboriginals of the
Lower Murray and its tribu-
taries possessed once a race of
professors of languages—men
possessing a remarkable'know-
ledge of the many different
dialects spoken by the numer-
ous tribes in the locality.
They were messengers or
itiuerant human newspapers
from tribe to tribe, each one
delivering his news to one, in an-
other tribe, of the same status or
calling as lnmself. T. ese news-
carriers travelled great distances and
bore certain marks or insignia that pro-
tected them from molestation. The local
linguist would, on receiving the news, relate it to
the assembled tribe, while the traveller was feasted
on whatever the camp afforded.
“ Wer.” : In a late Queenslander there
is a photo, of two leeches, taken from the udder
of a cow, and Stock Inspector C. D. Whitty gives
a precise account of the matter, presumably under
the impression that he has discovered something
new. As a matter of fact, leeches are .commonly
thus found on cows depastured on marshy lands on
the Richmond, N.S.W. On Spring Hill estate,
near Casino, where the cows mostly feed up to their
backs in water in the old racecourse swamp, I
have squirted dead and live leeches out of cows’
teats by the dozen. A little blood mostly comes
with them, not directly from the cow, but forced
from the leech in the tight squeeze getting out.
“6xB” : On the Lachlan (N.S.W.)
Father Drought doesn’t seem to have played up so
severely as we’ve been led to believe. Warroo
station on that river has just finished marking
19,000 or more lambs; an average of 86 per cent.
This is seldom surpassed even in the very best of
seasons. But up Cobar way things are very
different. Booroomugga, which used to shear
fully 60,000 sheep, will, this season, have hardly
3000 to put through. By the way, the A.W.
Union intends demanding the reinstatement of
its own Union agreement this year, so a strike
is pretty sure to ensue. Considering the deplor-
able state of the far-West where the battle will
begin,- the shearers should have a walk-over.

“Trotter”: Trekked recently through
the pastoral country between Cloncurrv and
Hughenddn (Q.), and found that although there is
now plenty grass and herbage everywhere, there
is no stock to eat it—in fact it will be years before
this country sees prosperity again. The stations
have mostly now no “hands” but a few blacks,
and there will this year be hardly any shearing.
Nelia Ponds station, which had 29,000 sheep, has
now only 900. Hamilton Downs, 93,000, now
15,000 ; Maxwelton, 110,000, now 40,000, and so
on. One pastoralist came up from the South
three years ago and started with 50,000 sheep;
now he can only muster 5000.

“ Hagr.un ” : Re horse-sense and vindic-
tiveness. Knew a horse at a tan-yard whose
business was to trot around a ring and keep a
bark-crushing machine moving. Bowler had been
at the game for years, and when knock-off time
was nearing, the old moke used to be on tlie alert
for the word to stop. Sometimes he would pull-
up if anybody approached and only said a word to
the boy driver. Then the boy would curse and
rattle stones on the poor brute’s ribs. “Yer
cow!” he used to say—“l’ll give yer stop \”
One day the boy’s shirt-sleeve caught in the cogs,
and his arm was being drawn into the machine.
He called frantically for Bowler to “whoa,” but
the old fellow turned a deaf ear and bowled on.
It was the pelting arm that was amputated.
Again : At a N.S.W. coastal town an old pub-
lican owned a horse named Jacob. On Sundays,
sailors used to hire “ Jake ” out at a bob an hour,
but I never heard of one keeping him out for five
minutes. The smallest boy in town had only to
shout, “ Up, Jacob ! ” and the neddy would at
once cast himself upwards in the atmosphere and
leave his man in the street, or anywhere. Then
he would return to the stable and be hired again.
* * *
There are still some wild patches left in
Australian life. A few weeks ago a trouble oc-
curred at Coen township (N.Q.), that strikingly
revealed our barbaric fringe. A Coen aboriginal
coveted a gin of the Archer tribe, and put out of
the way two Archer bucks who obstructed him.
On his attempting to stealthily carry off the gin
next day he was detected by some of the Archer
tribe, who pursued him and brought him down,
transfixed with four spears, in the middle of Coen
township. Then civilisation, in the shape of the
local police, arose, secured four of the offending
Archers, and fettered them (hand to leg) to a con-
venient tree. Next morning, the Archers had
levanted and .a black tracker who had evidently
released them, had disappeared also. Then the
chase began again; several more Archers were
secured by the “foorce,” and the Coen public is
awaiting developments.

A steep yarn of Drought:—
Dear Bulletin.— How far can
travelling cattle sniff water ?
Once when travelling
with a mob of Queens-
land scrubbers we had
been out three days
when we found the
tanks en route dried-
up, so my mate sug-
gested another track
where he knew of
“certain” hotels.
Wheeled off into the
scrub, taking a m' re
westerly direction,
and had m u c h
trouble that night
in keeping t h e
thirsty animals to-
gether. Next
morning struck
the new track,
crawled onward
until midday,
but still no water.
The bullocks
were now des-
. a. _ 1
perate, an 1
charged, bellowed,
moaned and struck
at one another furiously.
Thirst, dust, flies and
heat had maddened
them, and shortly after,
wards in one seething,
uncontrollable mass they
suddenly wheeled and went
at a terrific scramble in a
south-easterly line. Three
miles ahead, and a sight
beyond description ! A tank
about 40yds. square filled right
up with dying and dead cattle.
The weakest were trampled to
the bottom and smotheied or
drowned in the slime ; others,
torn and smashed, gouged at
each other in a hideous manner.
We rescued a few, but these also
hied almost imme-
diately. Out ot a
mob of 500 we didn’t
save one ! Parsed
the same tank on our
r< turn a few weeks
later, and at a mile’s
distance (could get
no closer) we distin-
guished a hillock,
30ft. high, of swol-
len carcases, the
whole being covered
with a black shroud
—crows ! —H.G.
“ Trotter ” : Queensland’s Continuous Go-
vernment proroses putting filters in public.schools.
I’ve got no filter. Had been l? 4 hours without
water in drought-stricken West, Queensland when
I struck a water-hole in which a man was washing
his socks. On expostulating with him, he re-
plied : “ Well, that’s nothing; when I came here
a cove was washing hisself in it.” Was camped
on a water hole where one Adams, a fossieker,
resided in a streak-of-bark humpy. We missed
him one day, and on going to the hut discovered
this note on the greasy slab-bench: “You'll find
my body in the bottom of the dam.” We did, too.
Have tal e 1 sheep out of a bog and drunk ti e
water ; and have been 48 hours looking for a bog
with even something worse than sheep in it, with
the full intention of drinking out of same when
found. Water has a fascination for intending
suicides here just by reason of its scarcity. I
sometimes suspect that every water-hole here has
somebody in it.

“T.G.F.”: “ Yarrum ” gives Temora
the “bun” for being the best-pubbed town
in Rabbit Land. Dubbo, I always under-
stood, was the best pub-supporting town in this
hemisphere. The licensed drink shops, to within
a mile of the town boundary, number 36. There
is little or no Sunday trade, but the “ two-up ”
schools have weekly classes in the scrub near the
gravel pits. At least this was the case when I was
there.

Sailors are proverbial growlers over
“ tucker,” but they can’t beat shearers. Cook
after cook had to “chuck” the job at Bogga-
baroo Station from fear of being battered by men
on the board who, to hear them talk, would have
been a match for Tom Sabers at his best. The
last “chef” had just got his cheque and cast the
dust behind him—thanking his stars that lie had
got away “whole” —when a weary-looking man
came along, threw down his swag, and asked for
work. The overseer took a bird’s-eye view of him
and suggested “ rouseabout,” but the stranger
Scorned the idea, and said he was a cook. He did
not look like one. His nose, probably originally
aquiline, had been badly flattened out and turned
to one side, and his face bore traces of many cuts,
which, he explained, had been caused by a tree
falling on him when a child. He signed on as
Wm. Peace, was duly installed as cook, and all
went well for a couple of days. Then a heavy
thunderstorm made the sheep too wet to shear,
a id some of the shearers rode over to the Pine
Ridge Hotel, with the result that next day, their
palates being out of order, a deputation, headed
by the bully of the shed, took a junk of “plum
duff” over to the cook and wanted to know what
t le blanky so-on sort of stuff he called that. The
cook calmly inspected the delicate morsel, saw
that there was an “intermission of five minutes
(as the theatre bills say) between the raisins, and
knew at once that the rest had been extracted in
order to raise a “ btirnoy.” He quietly suggested
to the deputation to “get,” and there was in-
stantly a wild rush at Wm. Peace, who stepped on
one side and landed the leader of the deputation
a staggering straight left on the nose, followed
promptly by a right swing to the jaw that ended
the first act then and there. Wm. Peace, in the
language of the barber’s-shop, then cheerfully re-
marked “Next, please !” But there wasn’t any
next, and there were no more complaints of the
“ menu ” during the rest of the shearing. When
the shed was “cut out” the owner handed the
cook his cheque and treated him to a couple of
glasses of whisky—which induced Mr. Peace to
confess that the marks on his face were really due
to contact with the “dukes” of numerous
antagonists of much greater fistic capabilities than
a shearing-shed was likely to produce.

Windsor (N.S.W.) Benevolent Asyluin
boasts the champion derelict of the district. His
age ranges at anything from 80 to 100. Ever
since his incarceration he has' been in the habit
of climbing out of the institution o’ nights for the
purpose of keeping up a custom of, say, 60 years
standing— i.e., three fingers of rum, gratis. A
few months ago, while on the way home one
night, he varied matters by essaying a short cut
through a neighboring garden. Consequence was
he fell down a disused cellar and broke his leg.
The limb was amputated, and the old gentleman
survives on crutches. A few weeks ago he was
caught trying to climb out of the institution on
the same old lay. Since then the authorities
provide him with the wherewithal to observe the
aforesaid custom.

“ Trotter ” : Out-from Normanton, in the
Gulf country, I visited recently five stations in
succession. In every case the manager was a
bachelor, and on some stations of from 300 to 500
sq. miles lie’s the only white man on the place.
Niggers do all the station work, while the dusky
damsels do the housework, &c. The cook at
the first place was a Malay ; at the second
a Chow ; at the third a Jap ; and at the
other two, Chows. Rouseabouts in this
country average 4s. or 5s. a week all the
year round, and two-thirds of their time
it hey are looking for work. Wages are
next to nothing, considering the con-
ditions under which the men live.
Manager gets £150 a year ; bookkeeper
(also boundary-rider), £75; overseer,
£75; stockman, £60. And you pay
20 per cent, more than “inside” for
the common necessaries of life—
potatoes, 35s. a cwt., 6s. for a pump
kin, Is. a loaf, 10s. a shirt, and Is.
a nip. On some stations, as soon
as the sun goes down they draw a
mosquito curtain all round the
table, placing heavy weights on it
to keep it down. In a few minutes
this is black with grasshoppers,
flying bugs, moths, beetles, sand
flies, ants and mosquitoes. God
help 1 he man who travels this coun-
try without a net! My visit was
in winter (May)—what must the
summer be like? At the break
of day the flies make an appear-
ance, and you knock them off
you in handfuls. Horses and
cattle suffer frightfully from sore
eyes ; so do the majority of people,
especially children. The white
ant literally devours everything
bar iron. A pair of boots which I
incautiously .left by the rooP of a
tree for a day and a night, I dis
covered in the form of an ant-heap and
nothing left but the nails, eyes and part of
the heels.
“Esnii”: Was watching a man working
sheep with his two dogs—one an old hand, the
other a pup just beginning his education. Pup
worked wild and man got off his horse to belt
him. At the puy/s first yelp the old dog made a
rush at the man and bit him savagely in the calf of
the leg. Then took a severe hiding himself with-
out a murmur. Man told me it is not at all
infrequent for an old dog to interfere if you are
giving a pup a hiding.
9 * 9 *
Alex. Gordon (Aboriginal Protector ap-
pointed last year by Q. Govt, for Boulia district)
complains bitterly of the evils of the Combo sys-
tem among the blacks. The Combo system
implies the maintaining of black women on a
station by a white man. The half-caste children
born are usually knocked on the head by the
mother. When a gin has given birth to a child
she is usually sent back to the camp and a fresh
one installed in her stead. No one on the stations
sees much harm in these practices. The native
women are the legitimate perquisite of the white
man.
Two samples of piebaldism in
North Queensland. A sugar-planter
has a half-caste illegitimate son,
whose mother is an aboriginal gin.
This young man recently married
his half-caste cousin, whose mother
is a Kanaka woman who is now
“kept” indiscriminately by Chows
and other aliens. Another case is :
An aboriginal-European half-caste
girl had a child to her white uncle,
and lives with her white relations as
one of the family, which, by the way,
is rather “well-off.” Now, what
sort of citizens will the progeny of
these specimens of humanity be? If
this breed is not stopped, the half-
castes will be a menace to civilisation
in that they have the franchise as
well as the right to cut ivages-rates.

“ L.” : In reply to Bright (8.,
18/5/1). The cause of the dying-
away of boree and myall is not
change of climate brought about by
ringbarking.. The boree and myall
are, comparatively, short-lived trees ;
and sheep are very fond of the
young plants. Consequently, on
sheep statioi s, young trees, to take
the place of those that have run
their course, are not allowed to
grow. In many horse-paddocks in
boree country this fact is fully exem-
plified ; there hundreds of young
trees may be seen growing up as fresh
as daisies. This class of vegetation
is quite easily grown in suitable
soil.
“R R.”: “Ita,” in Bulletin (4/5/’01),
seeks information on the one-sided growth of trees
round a clearing. The explanation is simple.
Light is life to all vegetation. Plant a potato in
a cellar, and five feet away a tiny hole to
admit light, and that potato-stalk will assuredly
find its way to that hole, and, if large enough,
through it. Grow a plant near a wall. All the
foliage will lean away from it and face the direc-
tion in which there is most light. Turn the plant
round, and, given a little time, the foliage will
reverse itself. It is the same with trees. When
a clearing is made on one side of a tree it imme-
diately begins to put on foliage in that direction,
and in time leans that way.

From Pretoria :
“The women continue to maintain an irreconcilable
attitude. In spite of prohibition they continue to wear
the Transvaal colours. One was had up before Lord
Roberts for so doing ; she drew a revolver, pointed it at
him, and asked if she required a permit for that also. The
noble Lord was much discomposed, and called for help.
On the occasion of a distribution of the Victoria Cross we
were told that Afrikander ladies would pin the medals on
the heroes’ breasts ; but when it came to the point we
did not see any ladies at the ceremony.”
The N.S.W. Freetrade press anl poli-
t'cians are angry with Melb. Age and others for
s lying that the failure of the Sydney Foreign-trade
party at the recent polls was a defeat of the cause
of Freetrade. The N.S.W. “Liberals” unani-
mously declare that the recent contest had noth-
ing at'all to do with the fiscal issue. Yet at the
beginning, Lee, Garland, and the Telegraph
not only prevented any coalition of ti e
“ Liberals ’’ with the “ Protectionists,” but
drew an appalling picture of the dangers
ahead of the holy cause of Freetrade if N.S.W.
did not return a majority of that faith at this
election. The “Liberals” went so far as to
appeal for a Freetrade majority in the probable
event of a vacancy in the State’s representation
in the Federal Senate, when the local Legislature
elects the new Senator. Truly enough the electors
did decline to make the contest a struggle on the
fiscal issue. But, if the Liberals had won ?
What price the good old Banner of Freetrade
then !

sive from Eraeusres (no less) A. Phipson, 151
Strand, London, Eng. :
It is always surprising to me that a paper so radical
and anti-humbug as yours should exhibit a predilection for
French words which not one person in a hundred can pro-
nounce with even approximate correctness. Whether you
expect your readers to sound them as if they were English,
to invent some quasi-foreign pronunciation for, or to skip
altogether, such words as nijneron, employ 6, absinthe,
repertoire, I don’t know, but, at any rate, it seems a pity
that you should put them to the trouble of any of these
alternatives when the good English words vintner, em-
ployee, absinth, repertory are at hand. Then why write
kharki for khaki any more than Chicargo for Chicago ?
I am glad to notice that you write mama instead of the
affected and indecent mamma. I trust you will use your
influence to have the expressive and appropriate name
“ Brightland ” adopted instead of the meaningless and
prolix N.S.W.
In reply, The Bulletin has an idea of writing
something like this :
214 George Street, Sydney, N.S.W.,
21st June, 1901.
Mr. Mick Phipson,
151 Strand, Fogtown, Eng.
Sir. —ln replying to your post-card of May 17, we have
addressed you as Mr. Mick Phipson. “ Mick ” is shorter
and more expressive than “ Evacustes.” Why do you
exhibit such a predilection for the foreign “ Evacustes,”
which not one person in a hundred can spell with even
approximate correctness ? The “A.” is a useless excres-
cence.
We use vigneron or vine-grower instead of vintner,
because these mean one thing and vintner another. A
“ vintner” is a wine-seller. Look at your dictionary.
Besides, why do you yearn after the foreign (Latin)
vintner ?
In spelling “ kharki ” we follow Kipling, who is pre-
sumed to know something about it.
We agree that employee and repertory are preferable to
employe and repertoire, though employee is only a
mongrel corruption of the French word ; if the French
spelt “ absinthe ” absinth we would also. At present it’s
their drink and their word, which we have borrowed,
but not yet incorporated, into English. We have just
the same reason for not writing “absinth ” that you have
for not writing “ troubl.”
We trust you will use your influence to have the expres-
sive name “ Fogtown ” adopted instead of the meaning-
less London.
“ The Devil’s hunting ground is the
border-land between Sense and Spirit.”— Patmore.
“ Chinese War Loot. Before disposing of
loot it is advisable to have it valued by an expert.
Mr. Larkin, 104 New Bond-street, values and
buys Oriental art specialities.” Advt. in Morning
Post, London. No sentiment about this; but
plain devil and no dissembling. By the way, the
loot smuggled from China now begins to make
very public appearances indeed in Sydney.
Died in the Balkans : William Clarke, a
London journalist, who has done much towards
shaping Radical thought during the last 20 years.
He was one of that brilliant band, the Fabian
essayists, and is the first of them to die. Of the
others, Mrs. Besant is with the Socialists no
longer, and Sydney Olivier is a big Colonial Office
official in Jamaica. The others—Bernard Shaw,
Sydney Webb, Hubert Bland, and Graham Wallas
—still beat the Fabian drum. Clarke changed his
views re Socialism in later years, and became a re-
viewer on the Spectator. The articles on Ameri-

ELDEST (log.): “ Johnny, you go and find her
clothes for her. I’m SICK of shepherding yous
kids. Where’s maw ?”
JOHNNY: “ Gone sailing with Mr. Mosscrop.”
ELDEST: “An’ where's dad?”
JOHNNY: “ ’E’s sittin' over there watching the
bathers. ”
ELDEST: “ Well, yous kids ain’t wanted, so yous
just go home.”
can politics and on economic subjects were nearly
always his. A firm-jawed, cold-eyed, reddish-
bearded man, he was a fine scholar and a good
friend. “ Send me all the cuttings you can about
Australian economic and social affairs,” he said to
the writer, some years ago: “ I want to master
Australia.” A few years ago, he was coming out
to study Australia on the spot, as he had partially
studied America—but illness prevented.
CONSIDER THE LILIES.—(FROM A CLUB BALCONY.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I looked out on an idle crew
Of women gotvned in ev’ry hue,
And muttered, leaning on the sill,
“ The Lilies paint the moral still!”
And then it seemed a ghost I spied,
And Solomon stood at my side /
He pointed where in sportive zeal
A girl flashed by upon her wheel.
And thus he answered with a grin,
“Oh, yes, these toil not, BUT they ‘spin/’”
BERNARD ESPISASSE.

A CLERICAL PROTEST.
A deputation, representing the local Minis-
terial Association and Christian Citizens’ Asso-
ciation, waited on the Prahran Council at its
1 meeting last evening urging that body to take
■action in putting down Sunday-trading in fruit,/
confectionery, ice-cream, &c. A number of{
fruit-sellers also petitioned the council to a
similar effeot.
The Rev. J. Nicholson (Wesleyan) said
several instances of Sabbath-breaking had
| come under his personal knowledge. The
other Sunday evening he saw a coffee-shop
'open m Chapel-street in which there was a deal 1
!of hilarity and a little disorder going on. It
was desirable that the Sunday law should be
strictly enforced for the benefit of the young
people, who were tempted to spend the pence
given them to take to church for mis-
sion&ry purposes in the lolly-shops." This|

The Health of Women
is all-important. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow. How essential, then, that
good health be hers. Good health, which alone
brings happiness and vigorous posterity. The
mechanism of >voman is finer than that of man.
She is naturally higher strung, and when the dual
functions of her life are taken into consideration,
it is little wonder that the delicate machinery
breaks down under the burden laid upon it.
The majority of women need an aid which
should strengthen them for the stress and strain
they daily meet. Such an aid is found in
Warner’s Safe Cure, a simple vegetable prepara-
tion, which has secured the highest endorsement
among, women in all walks of life in all parts of
the civilised world.
113 Edward-street,
East Brunswick, Vic.,
April 30th, 1901.
Dear Sirs, —
I have pleasure in giving evidence as to the
efficacy of Warner’s Safe Cure.
For many years I suffered from kidney and
liver complaint. Frequently I was quite pros-
trated and unable to do my household work, my
whole system being affected, especially my head.
The doctor who treated me did me no good. I
was advised to try Warner’s Safe Cure and
Warner’s Safe Pills. After taking a few bottles
of the medicines I felt quite a different woman,
•and am now in the enjoyment of as good health as
ever I had in my life.
Yours truly,
(Mrs.) E. NELSON.
Messrs. H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd.
“Camrock,” El wood-street,
North Brighton, Vic.,
29th April, 1901.
Sirs, —
I have found Warner’s Safe Cure to be a most
wonderful medicine for giving health and strength
when suffering from that languid and tired feel-
ing, which most women are subject to, and which
makes one unfit to attend to household duties or
to any other business. In my case Warner’s Safe
Cure had an immediate and lasting beneficial
effect.
I strongly recommend Warner’s Safe Cure to
any ladies who may suffer in like manner, feeling
sure that they too will derive great benefit from
taking a course of this medicine.
Yours truly,
(Mrs.) E. BADE.
Messrs. H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd.*

writer for the last ten years. Price half-a-crown,
ost-free from this office.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
Ho notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
Ho liability is undertaken in any case to contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.
H.N. : Why make such a row about N.S.W.
Electoral Dept. “ failing to send me a renewal of my
elector’s right whereby I have been deprived of my vote
at two elections ? ” You don’t need a new elector’s right
for every election Quiz: The whole British part of
the island has exports of about six million dollars —
steadily increasing. A railway is being constructed.
There are healthy mountain regions but they are very
inaccessible. A rich agricultural and mining country,
still largely jungle and containing about 500,000 nigs. A
hardened and clever nigger-driver with capital might
make a lot of money there ; not much show for any other
kind of man—except, perhaps, a hardened missionary

But we are not the Boers’ keeper. We only admire them
as men who hold aloft the torch of Liberty even
after it has burnt so low that it sears the fingers. (2) :
Then how is it that so many Boers have of late been
returned by the British officials as killed in actions in
which the British ha\ e been victorious, and so few com-
paratively set down as wounded ? Wounded should, in
proportion to killed, be as at least five to one ! What is
the inference?... .0 A. A. : Scores of public reading-rooms
all over Australasia were like yours—they discontinued
subscribing to The Bulletin because of ‘-’that attitude,”

members can’t do without the paper. The Bulletin
merely sniggers—it doesn’t want their subscriptions. It
knows perfectly well that The Bulletin habit is as
powerful and enduring as the drink haiiit or the morphia

THE FUNNIEST STORY IN THE WORLD
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HEARD it only the
other day,
Told hy a clergyman,
The funniest story in
all the world
Since ever the world
began.
We were sitting snug in a
railway train,
Smoking and talking
free—
There was Jim the Doctor,
and Parson Bill,
And the Barrister and
Me.
The Barrister told us a
funny yarn
That made us laugh
our fill;
But the funniest story in all the world
Was told by Parson Bill.
Laugh! we laughed till the window-pane
Shook like an aspen tree,
And even the mirthless railway train
Rocked on its rails with glee.
They say ’tis sad to see strong men weep,
But, since I was a boy,
I have never seen three men shed such tears
Of undiluted joy.
Jim, the Doctor was paralysed,
I was curled in a fit,
And Joe the Barrister laughed until
I thought his sides would split.
Parson Bill, with his deep grave eyes,
Gazed sternly all the while—
The funniest story in all the world
He told without a smile.
I never thought to laugh so much
Upon this earthly ball;
The people who have not heard that yarn,
They have not lived at all.
Wives with their household troubles crazed,
And widows plunged in woe,
Would hilt their petticoats and dance
If they that yarn could know.
Can I tell it ? Of course I cam
But, alas ! this may not be—
’-Twas told to us by a clergyman
In strictest secrecy.
CREEVE ROE.
The Murdered Poor in N.S.W. Asylums.
When that Good Man, J. N. Brunker, was
N.S.W. Chief Secretary, the Public Service Board
proposed a very important and salutary reform ill
the administration of the department which cares
for N.S.W. aged poor. According to the Board’s
report for 1599, it found a majority of inmates
of Liverpool and Parramatta Asylums were suf-
fering from cancer and lung-trouble, or were too
infirm to leave their wards. It proposed that
the control of these and other State institutions of
a like character should be put in the hands of a
medical man, and that the Asylums should no
longer be regarded as poor-houses, but as hos-
pitals for the aged. The Board also proposed
that each asylum should be under the control of a
doctor, and have a staff of trained nurses attached
to it, and that vacancies in the staff should there-
after be filled by wardsmen acquainted with hos-
pital work, and not by political deadbeats as was
then the case.
* « * *
The humane intentions of the Board would have
commended themselves to anyone, and ought to
have been especially grateful to the Good Man
whose piety was so great that he grew frantic at
the sale of a bag of bananas on the Sabbath. It
was also a reform easy of accomplishment, as there
was at that time a vacancy at the head of the
Charities Dept., and gross abuses had been found
in the previous administration. But apparently
it was somebody’s interest to block reform, and
the Board’s recommendations slumbered peace-
fully in the pigeon-holes of the Chief-Secretary’s
office. The person chosen by the Board for the
task of reforming the administration of the
Asylums, and transforming the institutions into
hospitals, was probably either Dr. Williamson,
of Gladesville, or Dr. Paton, Chief Medical Officer
for Sydney, both good men and capable of carry-
ing out the reform proposed. It would seem,
however, that the particular doctor chosen, who-
ever he was, had failed to bow low enough before
the potentate iq whose keeping Good Man
Brunker had placed his conscience ; and the poor
old victims of cancer, the consumptives, the
maimed and the blind, were condemned, as
before, to the brutality or inefficiency of ignorant
male warders, and were deprived of proper atten-
tion.
** 4 * *
The Lyne Government came in with great pro-
mise of social good to be accomplished and bene-
fits to be showered down on the toilers. And the
Public Service Board further delivered itself of
the following in May of last year :
For the most part, these old people (in the asylums)
are in infirm health, and probably 1300 out of about 3900
are really hospital patients ; while many others, though
actually not sick m the ordinary acceptation of the
term, are yet too infirm to quit their dormitories. . . .
At present only eight out of some 20 attendants have had
hospital training, while quite a large proportion of the
unskilled attendants joined when too old to adapt them-
selves to the requirements of the service,
The Board might have added that much of the
work at the Asylums is done, not by the paid
warders, but by the inmates themselves, and is
therefore even less efficient than that of unskilled
warders. Then the Lyne Govt, took the job in
hand. Its chief and only care certainly was to
see to the well-being of its old friends, whose
shining virtues had so long adorned the com-
munity. First came poor old Waller, for whom
a gilded haven was found at the Harbour Trust.
Then came all the deserving poor of the Daily
Telegraph. Unfortunately, having provided for
these and a few others equally unfortunate, the
Lyne Govt, felt too exhausted to continue further,
and, while Father Oarey and his chickens were being
satisfied, the poor wretches at Parramatta, at
Newington, and at Liverpool, were allowed to die
off in lingering agony, unalleviated by the skilled
attention and medical comfort which civilized
people give even to prisoners of war. But, oh
bow different, if the old men had votes!
* * * *
While the recommendations in regard to the
old people were still in the minds of the Ministers
the awkward matter of the Little Bay Hospital
inquiry began to attract notice. This hospital is
essentially a place for the treatment of the sick
poor. Scandalous abuses were unearthed, and the
Public Service Board, unable to allow the Hospital
to remain under the Health Department, sug-
gested, this time to Premier See, that it
should be placed under the same manage-
ment as the charitable institutions, and that a
medical head should be appointed to control these
institutions. The Board, in fact, revived their
earlier recommendations with additions. It is a
matter of public notoriety that patients are kept
on at Little Bay after they are convalescent,
because, being paupers, they cannot be discharged
lest they should have to sleep in the parks. Red
tape forbids that they should be sent direct to an
asylum, and when the unfortuates are at length
cast out of the hospital, they are left to drift
about until they relapse or get an order for asy-
lum admission. Meanwhile their presence at
Little Bay prevents other patients being accom-
modated, and thus double mischief is wrought.
In view of the unspeakable scandals at Little Bay
a new administration was necesssary, and the
Charities Department was probably the right
place under which to put the hospital, but such a
change more than ever necessitated the medical
control of the charities. But whoever it was that
was able to stifle Good Man Brunker’s Conscience
was able to do as much for Premier See’s, and the
proposal was vetoed by the Government.
• • • »
Failing to carry its point, the Public Service
Board weakly gave way, and completely reversed
their frequently-urged recommendations. It
took back its nomination of a medical man, and
appointed an official named Hanson, reputed to be
an excellent auditor, but with no effective qualifi-
cation to administer the charitable hospitals of
the State. The folly of the Board’s action will
appear the more glaring when it is remembered
that under the Old Age Pensions Act many of the
able-bodied inmates will be removed from the
asylums, leaving behind mainly those who cannot
shift for themselves.
* * • *
While the farce is being played, the harvest of
death is being reaped. At Liverpool Asylum one-
fourth of the inmates die every year, and at Rook-
wood and Parramatta one-sixth. The heart that
bled for Willman’s sad fate cannot be expected
to bleed for the five or six hundred ancients who
are yearly counted out. at Liverpool and Parra-
matta, where their survivors are crushed together
like trucked sheep. So the plea of the poor and
the outcast is unheard, and their bodies are daily
. made a sacrifice to feed the pompous pride of cal-
lous officialdom.
Northern People and Liquor.
“ Waddy” makes a free speech to The Bulletin
on Northern liquor and things :
Queensland laws are of the elastic sort. We complain
further south of two laws : one for the rich and one for
the poor. Further north in Q. they complain of two laws
also, one for the north and one for the south. Take
the Chinese question. It has been told me by the
officers of three steamers going north that at Cooktown
every time a Chinese steamer comes in a cargo of Chinks
is landed. The Universal Great Australian Tired Feeling
gets very bad the further north you get, and presumably
the customs officers are painfully afflicted with it. Each
steamer coming south carries a number of non-English-
speaking Ch.namen, and if you question an officer of the
steamer he will give you Cooktown as their shipping port.
A shrug of the shoulders is his answer to most of your
other questions.
At one shipping port of N.Q. I have known a case to
slip from a ship’s sling and burst open in front of a cus-
toms officer, showing the contents to be boards branded
with some well-known whisky name all ready to go toge-
ther. Branded corks, capsules, labels and bottles are
imported—all made in Germany. What class of whisky
is put into these bottles and labelled with these
imported labels is known only to the Lord and the
whisky merchant. If these merchants stopped there per-
haps it would not matter so much —for the coastal con-
sumers of this bad whisky are usually satisfied when
they are as drunk as 40 cats —but they go further, and
sell to the out-back proprietors of N.Q. drinking-hel s
some of those branded corks, capsules, and labels. And
the out-back drinker isn’t satisfied unless he is as drunk
as 85 cats, and pretty large cats at that, so the whisky
that goes with the bogus cork speedily kills him. Out-
back whisky is usually manufactured on the premises,
from some recipe supplied for a consideration by the
agent. Anyone can get a license in North Queensland,
as far as the police are concerned, and for any kind
of premises—they will license a sheet of bark up
there. One P.M. of the North informed me that he
granted licenses to smellful houses because his direct
instructions from head-quarters in Brisbane were that,
unless some private objection was offered, all licenses
applied for were to be granted in the interests of State
revenue. So in small villages of 60 adult inhabitants you
may see four “ hotels,” consisting of a bar, a dining-room,
a couple of bedrooms (probably all with earthen floors), and
a murderous-looking landlord in charge. I don’t know
where they grow some of those landlords.
It has also occurred to me that “ the interests of State
revenue ” may account for some of the liquor dens of
Sydney suburbs, to say nothing of the out-back abomina-
tions of N.S.W.
The licensing law’ of N.Q. (they have the same law for
all Q., but in the north it is administered differently) is
admitted by the authorities to be rotten, but the Three
Years’Licensing Commission summed up most of its re-
commendations by saying, in’effect, “ The people are used
to things as they are.”
One of my N.Q. publican friends one day inherited
some whisky from a retiring publican, together with a lot
of branded corks, capsules, etc. My friend was new at
the trade and in the whisky he had struck something
curious—he said it had a “ funny feel,” and asked me to
look at it. A modicum of yellowish-brown' liquor was
poured into a tumbler. “ Feel it,” said Mr. Pub. I felt
it. “Quite cold, ain’t it?” said he. “Quite cold U’ I
said, Mr. Pub poured some water in. “ Feel it now,” he
said. I felt it. The temperature had risen from about
70 to 120 or so. I didn’t examine it closely, nor did I
taste it. I was afraid it might rush out of the glass and
attack me. I can’t think it was sulphuric acid that
caused the heat. I know rectified spirit gets warm on
adding water, but the stuff was too hot. Two nips of it
knocked over one fellow in 25 minutes.
The Customs question, so far as the tainted North is
concerned, is one which can only be cured in one way.
Remove every official north of the tropic of Capricorn
who has been there more than two years, but don’t leave
the new men there for more than two years. The whole
of N.Q. is rotten through and through, the climate has
got the officials and population in its enervating grasp,
and laziness and corruption are supreme in the land.
The business people of N.Q. are so used to dealing with
Japs, Chinkies, and South Sea islanders that, for the life
of them, they cannot be prompt or business-like in their
dealings with a white man from South. Those of the
North don’t notice it.
Intercourse between such a mixed population brings
about some curious results. Here we see the white man
rapidly degenerating through kow'-towing to the inferior
races. A wealthy Chinaman in the North is a power in
the land, and a wealthy white man is so scarce that—
welf, N.Q. gets down and wallows when it sees him. The
hybrid population that is growing up offers some interest-
ing studies of the. effect of racial intermixture. Will the
ultimate end of this mixture produce a new race like the
lazy, treacherous Guatemalans or Cubans? In and around
Cairns you see every possible combination of half-caste—
the most evil that of a Chinaman and a black gin.
When their son marries the daughter of a Syrian and his
Kanaka girl, I don’t know what will happen next.

The monster public deputation in favor of
Co-Operative coupons (introduced by Mr. Duncan
G-illies, M.L. A.) established an Australian record ;
over 500 people attended. The speakers were
limited by Mr. Peacock to live, but they rep-
resented an enormous number of the public. Mr.
Nelson (representing 2000 workers in the railway
service) said : “This coupon system has taught us
thrift and brought pleasure to a great many
homes.” Mr. Davis (President Brickmakers’
Association) said that “ The public realised that
it is the only system by which they can get dis-
count on all small purchases.”*
