POLITICAL
POINTS
Of the original N.S.W. Labor party of ’9l
only two have held on to their seats and remained
Laborites throughout—M‘Gowen and Cann.
Edden, though now a Laborite, was independent
for a time ; and Andy Kelly had a spell at the bar.
A N.S.W. State forecast that does not sin
against probabilities is that the Arbitration Bill
will again be thrown out by the Council, whereon
Govt, will go to the country on that Bill, a Re-
duction of Members Bill, and Reform of the
Council.
Men in the street praise S.M. Herald
because in the recent State elections it was not so
big a fool as Sydney Daily Telegraph. But the
difference between being a fool and abstaining
from being a fool is very often a mere matter of
heat of blood.
The Kanakas in Mongrelia, according to
the latest official count (end of June), numbered
9330. Bundaberg owns 2274, Mackay 1775, and
the Herbert River 1183. Altogether 209 are
debited to Brisbane, where, of course, they’re
sugar-growing.
This is something to their credit, and
perhans here and there to the credit of their
organisations—Not one Labor member who sat
in last N.S.W. Parliament and ran again was
defeated. That is true of no other party —not
even of poor Terry’s Independent party of one.
Apropos of the great railway strike in
Westralia, it is interesting to note that W.A. has
an Arbitration Act to meet such difficulties. It
was one of the measures passed by Bigjohn to
placate the progressive party, and few people
noticed until now that it was quite useless for the
single reason that the Board which was to arbi-
trate had never been appointed. Eternally
keeping your eye on Bigjohn is the price of
. iberty.
TOO DANGEROUS.
FREDDY: “But, I say, Chawley, what’s come of that fancy [?]ais
coat you had on yesterday?”
CHAWLEY : “ Oh! had to leave it off. Fwightened the hosses.”
In the fund raised in India recently for
the purpose of erecting a memorial hall to the late
Queen Victoria in Calcutta, the seven largest
donations (from greasy Indian princes) total
£216,529. How can one possibly reconcile this
with the numerous requests last year for subscrip-
tions to assist in relieving the Indian famine ?
The Queensland deficit of £550,000 is
making the local Civil Service tremble. Already
officials are putting out feelers and trying the tone
of the Ministerial mind. And the Ministers
themselves don’t know what to do, for the general
election comes next year, and an onslaught on the
Service just now would mean calamity in 1902.
Labor-leader Browne (Q.)says that Leahy,
the present Q. Works Minister, represents
about 200 men, half-a-million sheep and 7,000,000
rabbits. Browne might have added that Leahy’s
Govt, also represent a dozen years of shameful
misgovern ment, a score of strangled reforms, the
biggest debt (per capita) on earth, and half-a-
million deficit.
WHICH DEVIL ?
“ You know us, gentlemen ; you know what we have
done.” —Premier See at the Empire Hotel.
It seems to us, as politics go,
In spite of what proverbs tell—
Better the devil you do not know
Than the devil you know too well.
The mere fact that one newspaper brutally
attacks a certain politician personally every week
is no reason why the Commonwealth Parliament
should establish a tyranny by Act of Parliament
in the Postal Bill. If that politician and his
friends have a grievance let them take action for
libel; if they don’t choose to do so, then they
must be held to have no grievance.
Senator Glassey’s proposal that mail sub-
sidies should be paid only to white-labour steam-
ship lines moved Senator Dobson to explain that
Lascars were employed, not for the sake of cheap-
ness, but only because of the drunkenness, disor-
derliuess and unreliability of the British sailor.
Now, if a Labor member had uttered such a blast-
ing libel on the vaunted British sailor !
Federal Postmaster-General Drake’s Bris-
bane organ, Progress, affects a bad policy in
eternally rejoicing at Labor calamities in Queens-
land, and eternally patting the rotten Continuous
Govt, on the back! From a recent issue “ Mr.
Philp has a golden opportunity of rallying the
Democracy of this country to his standard and
becoming the Seddon or Ballanee of Queensland.”
Fancy Philp as a Ballanee !
Rumored that the Westralian members
propose to vote solidly against the taking over by
the Federal Govt, of the Northern Territory, lest
the expense attached to it, and the further cost
of completing the overland line to Port Darwin
which must needs follow, should interfere with
the prospects of their own transcontinental rail-
way. If they do so, a lot of members will get
level by voting against the Westralian transcon-
tinental line, and the Gropers may cut their own
throats. The Commonwealth is rich enough, if
its affairs are well administered, to take over the
Territory and to build both railways; they are all
essential works, and all works of great ultimate
profit. But the taking over of the Territory is
ten times more urgent than the W.A. railway,
and if one work must stand over for a while, there
is no doubt at all as to whph should stand.
Dear BULLETIN. —
The headquarters of the
pearling industry of Tor-
res Straits and its sur-
rounding 5000 sq. miles
of sea are at Thursday
Island. The wages paid
to the colored employees
of this industry alone
average £50,000 a year,
and that money is distri-
buted amongst some 2200
men—Japanese, Manilas
Kanakas and Australian
aborigines. Now, to des-
troy this industry would
mean the ruin of many,
and a reduction of about
£30,000 per an. in the
Federal Customs tariff
from Thursday Island
alone. Take away the
colored labor, the divers,
the tenders, and the
nigger crews, and there
is no white labor to re-
place them. The whole
industry must cease for
a time. But the various
States have industrial
schools and reforma-
tories scattered every-
where, and in most of
these institutions at
least one trade is taught.
Why not include diving
amongst these trades ?
So, within a few years,
white divers would be
forthcoming to super-
sede the Japs and Manila
men, who now have a
practical monopoly of
the business.— W.

strengthening of S. Aus.
Leg. Council, King-
ston’s democratic idea of
having a convention to
frame a revised State
Constitution will most
likely be dropped.
Holder, during his

same disease, only more so. And if, having noth-
ing much else to do, Parliament should drop Jen-
kins, the chances are that the Premiership will
go to Duncan, a fine Proputty representative in
the Council. It is an awful thing to contemplate
S.A. coming to this, but it looks quite possible.

Premier Leake declares that when he
offered voluntary arbitration to the W.A. railway
strikers (the compulsory Arbitration Act being
laid up for repairs for reasons connected with late
Premier Bigjohn) they accepted cheerfully with
the proviso that neither party shovM be bound by
the decision of the Board. The negotiation then
snapped off, carrot-fashion. It would be inter-
esting to know if there is any other side to that
story. If there is no other side, then the W.A.
railway strikers are a remarkable crowd.
The proposal that N.S.W. Government,
which now owns 29 public-houses in the resumed
area of Bydney, should try the experiment of
running the drink traffic there as a State Mono-
poly, has caused multitudes of good dry men to
rise up and protest against the State deriving
revenue from the “accursed thing.” The trouble
is that N.B.W. already derives £1,000,000 a year
revenue from the accursed thing, and it would
need nearly 2s. in the £ income-tax and 3d. in the
£ land-tax to make up the loss if it swore off.
“B.”: Thenuml >erof candidates who offered
themselves at N.B.W general elections, just over,
proves that the deposit system should never have
been abolished. Numbers came forward just for
a business advt., and nobody’s cheese or bacon
advt. should be pasted on the Constitution in this
fashion. If a lot of electors petition someone to
allow himself to be nominated, and the candidate
cannot pay the deposit, the electors will pay it
for him if their invitation is not a hoax. At
Braidwood three candidates got six votes among
them.
Owing to the cable between Vic. and Tas-
mania being private property, the little sister of
the Commonwealth is denied equal press rates
with the other States. Inter-State press messages
are now carried, when dealing with proceedings in
the Federal Houses, at Victorian rates ; i.e., a
paper at the Gulf of Carpentaria or Perth will pay
no more per 100 words than a paper at Bendigo.
But Tasmania is out of the running because of her
privately-owned cable. An object lesson in the
national curse of privately-owned means of com
munication.
Consider Britain’s wonderful luck in the
past ! It was a stroke of luck that made France
and Spain, instead of joining to drive the English
out of Canada and the West Indies, waste their
strength on the siege of Gibraltar, defended by an
English cavalry General—Elliot—-who, by another
queer chance, was also the best defensive engineer
in Europe. It was another stroke of luck that
ended the Crimean War before the Indian Mutiny
broke out, and another that prevented the late
Chinese shindy breeding a European war while
S’Africa was a blaze.
What an edifying and suggestive spectacle
is that of the Victorian Chief-Justice (in his capa-
city of Acting-Governor) colloguing with the Vic.
Premier on a political subject (the Findley case),
which may yet come, and might easily have come
ere now, before the Supreme Court presided over
by the Chief Justice-Lieut.-Governor aforesaid!
When are these two utterly incompatible offices
to be finally divorced in order that such shameful
scandals may be avoided? What does Chief-
Justice Waxcellency Madden think of himself on
calmly reviewing the Findley situation ?
Th© recent
issue of a new
Victorian duty-
stamp which
can’t be used for
postal purposes,
and a postage-
stamp w h i c h
can’t be used for
receipting bills, also the
calling in of the previous
penny stamps which were
available for both pur-
poses, are matters for
question in Vic. Assem-
bly. What in the name
of Federation was the depart-
mental excuse for ordering new stamp-
dies for a dying provincial-system ? The Federal
P.O.'will in due course issue a first and—it is to
be hoped—final set of all-Australian stamps.
With these in view, the recent introduction of
new stamps for a new rule looks to have been
done for the benefit of somebody else than the
general public.
The Federal Property Acquisition Bill
provides that the Commonwealth shall have power
to compulsorily resume land in the Federal Terri-
tory ; that the compensation to be paid shall be
based on the value of the land as at the 1st Jan.
preceding the resumption, and shall not include
any added value or prospective value created by
the expenditure of Federal money in creating a
capital, AND THAT THE COMMONWEALTH SHALL BE
SOLE GROUND LANDLORD OF THE FEDERAL TERRI-
TORY FOR ever. To all of which The Bulletin
says a deafening Amen.
Sydney Morning Herai.d cordially ap-
proves, in a recent leading article, of the proposal
that the British Govt, should cancel the agree-
ment under which the Netherlands Railway Co.
(largely owned in Holland and Germany) exists
and has its Transvaal line. It supports t l is act
on the ground of public policy. The same prece-
dent may come in useful some day with a view to
cancelling the concessions of the S.A. Co., the
Australian Agricultural Co., the V.D.L. Co., the
Hampton Plains (W.A.) Co., the Melbourne,
Perth, Brisbane, and Adelaide Tramway Co.’s,
the Northern Territory Land Co., &c. And then
even the Herald will say things.
Sydney D. T.’s Cape correspondent, for-
wards a column of Jingo pleading for (1) dis-
franchisement of the British electors of Dutch
descent in Cape Colony ; and (2) abolition of the
Dutch language in schools where a vast majority
of the children are of Dutch descent. And the
war, says the Rhodesian press, was made because
Transvaal Britishers—aliens in the community—
were disfranchised; and because English was

READ WHAT . . .
VITADATIO
... IS DOING.
A Victory from Junee.
30 YEARS A SUFFERER.
VITADATIO CURED HIM.
Mr. S. A. PARMER, Sydney,
Dear Sir, —It is now close on 30 year* since I had the
first attack of a cold, which has troubled me continuously’
At first the cough was only slight, and, as most people
thoughtlessly do, I did not take any particular care of
mvself, thinking the cough would soon leave me. 1 was
then about list, in weight; but 1 began to get much
worse, and, in spite of medical attendance, I soon found
the cold had such a severe hold on me that nothing
apparently did me any good. For years I tried many of
the advertised remedies, but they all failed to cure me.
The cough increased, and before 1 could lay down in bed
at night I would be seized with a fit of coughing, which
frequently lasted for an hour, during which time I would
expectorate a great quantity of phlegm. 1 lost flesh
rapidly, and my clothes were simply hanging on me like
hags. ’ However, a short time ago, I commenced taking
“ Webber’s Vitadatio,” and, after taking the second
bottle, I began to improve, and now, after taking six
large bottles of Vitadatio, I feel splendid, and have re-
gained my loss in weight. I am 14st. again. I cannot
express my gratitude for the recovery of my long-lost
health. 1 intend taking a few more bottles, so as to make
the cure thorough and complete, and I am sending you
this testimonial in the hope that other sufferers may be
induced to give your wonderful medicine a trial.—Your*
gratefully, (Signed) W. B. LYONS.
Junee, late of Albury,
February 20th, 1900.
For further particulars.
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

In ace of the recent wholesale alienation
of C. own lands in certain Q. districts, this Bris-
bane Courier extract is worth reading :
Nothing will so quickly re-act on Brisbane as the
cutting-up of large estates on the Downs ; and, since
Parliament has endorsed the principle of cutting-up tat
lands within easy access of centres of population and
making them suitable for settlement, the Govt, will pro-
bably look in this direction for relief.
More Seaforth-estate jobs in prospect T
Indications are that King Ned is to be
called King of “Greater Britain,” or “Sovereign
Lord ” of various specified places, and that there
is to be in bis title nothing about “ Protector of
the Australian Commonwealth.”
However the various independent miscel-
laneous and unattached members of the new
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly may sort themselves out,
it is pretty certain that the self-styled “ Liberal ”
party will not succeed in obtaining a term of
power without a long course of political log-rolling
and intrigue. How the stronger men of the party
must curse their stupidity in allowing Sydney
Telegraph and G. H. Reid to bluff them into
declining the portfolios offered by See, O’Sullivan
and co. a month or two back ! But it looked such
a rosy prospect, such a sure thing ! Spurn the
offer with scorn, storm the country with the
Freetrade cry, sweep the polls and establish a
Ministry under “us” —the Freetrade and Liberal
Association. Ha, ha !
The scale of tilings American ! The last
two years have seen blizzards and cyclones which
destroyed hundreds of lives. Last year’s huge
N.Y. dock-fire did the same. And now Carnegie
sells out for £30,000,000 ; and same cable tells that
a dam has burst, wrecking 11 villages, leaving
14,000 people homeless, and causing the death of
between 500 and 1000. Again, we have the
striking of a pier by lightning, causing the death
of 11 youngsters who were fishing therefrom ; and
in one day we learn that because somebody said
“ Strike !” 40,000 men “go out”; and that So-
and-so has casually given a trifle of a million or so
to something or other. In New York at present
the heat is causing scores of deaths daily ; 200
police are on the sick-list; the hospitals are
crowded with patients; vehicular traffic is sus-
pended, and firemen line the streets hosing such
poor beasts as are out. “ Millionaires walk the
streets in their shirt-sleeves, and workmen go
about in gymnastic costume.” Truly wonderful
are the happenings in the “chosen spot of God’s
green earth.”
Senator Glassey’s motion that the Com-
monwealth should only pay mail subsidies to
3teamship lines employing white labor exclusively,
aroused much silly vituperation about “presum-
ing to dictate to companies located 10,000 miles
away as to whom they should employ,” and
“ wanting to punish people because of their
color,” and so on. The Democratic party doesn’t
object to anybody’s color, and it is quite willing
to see black men employed— if they are employed
at white men's wages. It only objects because the
black man is employed at low wages, and is used
as a means to lever down the white man’s pay.
And nobody proposed to dictate to the P. and O.
Co. as to its employees—it may have even nigger
captains and nigger directors if it likes. It was
only proposed that the Commonwealth should
have the same liberty to decide whom it should
employ, and that it should not employ Lascars in
its ocean mail service. Glassey’s proposal was
lost by 17 to 9, most of the Freetraders voting
solidly for the oheap nigger.
Labouchere’s Truth and a large party in
London contend that Milner is ineligible for a
British peerage as he was born a German subject,
and there is a statute of William and Mary divest-
ing the King of the power to create a naturalised
subject a peer. Milner, having been given his
peerage, will be known as the Lager peer.
Dear Bulletin. —It is hard for common
persons like myself to realise the enormous lengths
to which royal condescension can go when it tries
hard, but . the recent cable message that the King
would give his assent to the Civil List personally
in recognition of the promptitude with which
Parliament parted-up came in the nature of an
eye-opener. Suppose I was employed in your
office, and you paid my salary earlier than usual,
and I marked my approval of your promptitude
by personally signifying my acceptance of the
“beans,” would my condescension break you
up ? —J.H.

Pity the sorrows of the trader on the
western (N.S.W.) rivers and the country those
occasional rivers serve ! While other N.S.W.
merchants have been filling their warehouses in
order to dodge the coming duties, these waybaok
unfortunates, the rivers being unnavigable, see
the weeks slide and their ordered goods still on
the other side of the border.
“ S.S.”: The Bulletin, in recently point-
ing out that brains are, under modern conditions,
a third factor in the production of wealth, and
one that must be considered equally with labor
and capital, should have dropped a line or two to
cripple the fond illusion of Fatman that this is
some further justification of his appropriation of
the lion’s share of the product The Argus long
ago raised joyous acclaim over this theory in the
comfortable belief that it reduced to absurdity the
demands of labor for not only a share of the
necessaries, but a proportion of the luxuries.
Really brains in production are as much the bond-
servant of Capital as Labor is, presuming you
mean not merely the cunning to acquire and
manipulate certain properties; but the genius to
devise and invent machinery, and the power to
conceive and execute great schemes to facilitate
production. Capital employs Brains as it employs
Labor, but identifies itself with the third factor in
argument.
One of the worst and clumsiest follies of
Victoria’s House of 48 Deranged Landlords has
come home to roost. Some time ago Vic. Assembly
passed an Act providing for the official examina-
tion and branding of all meat sold in Melbourne
and suburbs, with power for any outside district
to come under the Act if so inclined. 11
was simply a law for the suppression of diseased
meat, but the Upper House, which largely repre-
sents the country districts, couldn’t see why the
country grower shouldn’t sell his diseased meat.
So it thought it was doing a clever thing when
it insisted on striking out the clause providing
that the Act might be extended to other places
besides Melbourne. Result is that no meat
slaughtered outside Melbourne can be officially
inspected and branded as good. Without that
brand it can’t go to Melbourne, and that big
market is cut off. The country freezing and
chilling works and slaughter-houses are closing
up, and the country dead-meat trade is about as
dead as its own meat. It is a beautiful commen-
tary on the alleged brains of the House of 48 Rich
Dodderers that it couldn’t see what it was doing
in a little matter like this. .

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Grand National Steeple this year, as last,
proved a fluting companion event to the Hurdle Race,
Freedom, at Oat. 111 b.—the same weight that Shoddy car-
ried over tha smaller sticks—beat a good one in Bethnal
(list. 191 b.) in much the same way as Shoddy had beaten
Pirate King. The third place in each event fell to a fairly
weighted horse from the Miller stable. The time for
the Steeple was 7seq. better than Rosebud’s previous re-
cord, whereas the Hurdle Race time was merely equal to
previous record, viz., the smin. 52Jsec., scored by Aqua-
rius last year.
So the V.R.O. handicapper has to mark, learn
and inwardly digest the fact that the Grand Nat.
“ double ” last year was secured by a pair of 9-stoners, in
record times, and this year it fell to a pair 9st. 11-ers,
also in record times. The average jumping gelding,
nowadays, is much faster than the field of a dozen years
ago, but the system of handicapping is not j'et adapted
to modern conditions. The heavyweighted and middle-
weighted good performers feel the burden more than they
felt it when the pace was slower. They deserve a better
chance of winning these rich stakes. The interests of the
owners of nags that show good “form” in minor events
are the interests of sport, and the interests of sport are
what punters expect to see protected bv the boss Aus-
tralian Racing Club. On a rough calculation, the “ lOst.
or under” division provides not more than one-third of
the field for aG. N. Hurdle or Steeple. I l '- ought to be
2 to 1 against a lightweight winning.
Freedom is a good, game chestnut. His legs
showed signs of damage sustained during the long jour-
ney, but he was gay and festive when he came to be
rubbed down after the race. Bellamy (who trained for
the Millers before he started on his own) had got Freedom
very fit, and was sanguine of pulling off the prize if
Laceby didn’t stand up. Laceby duly fell. The son of
K.ng Tom was meeting Freedom on 41bs. better terms
thin when he beat him in a canter over 19 furlongs a few
davs previously, but this time his weak fore-leir failed
at the first jump. His jockey, in brand-new jacket and
cap, escaped unhurt. The hor3e was led off the course
with his broken limb swinging, and a bullet from the
vet. terminated his earthly pilgrimage. Backers of
Laceby knew all about the bad leg, and were practically
gambling on the chance of its remaining good for one
more race.
The Tasmanian horse, Bay Eagle, who looked a
commoner beside most of them, ran in close company
with Freedom for 2£ miles : but Pirate, the hope of Bal-
larat, was a great disappointment. He seem 3 to have
lost some of his pace whilst beingcured of his impetuosity.
Perhaps Irish Stew was the only unfortunate (bar
Laceby) that might have won had he stood up. The
Stew had a large following of men who “knew some-
thing,” and he rushed into the lead after going two miles.
Then he put his backers in a stew by falling at the next
fence.
» * * *
Freedom, winner of V.R.C.G.N. ’Chase, carried
a good deal of coin, though good odds were going against
him at flag-fall. His owner is chairman of Bendigo J.C.,
and is a sport right through. Writer looks to see this
lepper supporting much bigger imposts than 9.11, and
finishing in front with them. Second nag put up a great
go, all things weighed. Conceding close upon 3st. to the
winner, he ran under anything but favorable conditions.
A long early morning bolt on Mordialloc road, and conse-
quent injury to his legs, rendered the substitution of
swimming for galloping necessary.
* * * •
“ Gun ” : Of 11 G.N. Hurdles since ’9l the
favorite has only won twice, first when Apsiey scored in
’94, and again four years subsequently, when Pat (5 to 1
against), with J. E. Brewer up, squelched 19 others. This
fact notwithstanding, punters rushed 9 to 2 a il 4 to 1
about Carbinier. Haven’t seen it mentioned anywhere
that the jumping of all 22 starters was very high-class.
• * • •
Running of V.R.C. Grand National (Saturday)
calls to mind that several top-notch leppers humping heavy
loads have got away with that event. Redleap (13.3) had
the weightiest, then follow Sussex (13.1), Dungan (12 9)
Ruby and Floater (12.7). Domino (12.10) finished second
to Mysore. Denmark (12.11), Lady Ashton (12.5), Mernder
(12.2), Saxon (12.0), Busaco (12.10) and Dondi (12.2) were
thirds in their years.
* * • «
Indications are that, all being well, Sagacity
will (if he doesn’t go to India in the interval) start very
nearly first favorite foi- oming Caulfield Cup. Last week
he closed on Braybrook Handicap (Urn.) easily
(carrying 9.10) in 2.35 h With a 31b. penalty, already'
incurred. Sagacity will only hump 8.10 for the first of the
two big Cups. _Up to lately he has been labelled a pro-
nounced unreliable. Previous to annexing Braybrook
Handicap, all that stood to his credit were A.J.C. Spring
Maiden Stakes and V.A.T.C. St. Kilda Cup.
* * * »
The brilliant form shown by Sagacity at recent
V.R.O. meeting was ascribed to the fact that
they have found out how to ride ’im.” A waiting race
terminating in an irresistible sprint is Sagacity’s “ dart ”
it seems, and judging from what the horse did at Flern-
mgton, there can be no better way of handling him—
when the opposition is weak. But when it comes to a
Caulfield Cup race the policy of a long wait and a short
sprint is more difficult to work. Sometimes the lj-mile
course is covered in 2min. 36sec., the pace being so warm
that the animals which “ wait ” simply get left
until it is too late to catch the tiring leaders
with a final sprint. Take the case of La
Carabine. The mare used to be a slow beginner, i.e., she
ran a waiting race on her own. When the field was big,
and they were all travelling full speed from the start, La
Carabine couldn’t get into a position to win the Caulfield
Cup (1899) with the nice weight of 7.5 on her back. The
mare put in a brilliant run at the finish, but the effort
came too late. To make sure of winning a Caulfield Cup
a horse must be among the first six at the turn for home,
and to make sure of getting among the half-dozen the
chances are that a jockey will need to bustle his mount
from the start.
* • * *

powder Plot day. Backers of coincidences can find only
one horse in the race whose name seems to fit the occa-
sion—Jimmy Wilson’s Nitre, a very favorably handicapped
5-year-old.

“ The starting-gate was used for the first time
for Volodyovski’s Derby,” writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent from England, “ and the start was delayed half-an-
hour owing to sheer funk on the part of some of the
English riders. There is a good chance here for Austra-
lian trainers and for a couple of smart jocks used to the
gate. A good Australian trainer could show some of
them here many a wrinkle in training and placing horses.
Hickey, who has done so well with Australian Star and
The Grafter, has now got as many horses as he can attend
to, and the Days have a long string. There are no other
Australian trainers here.
“The English sporting press,” continues the
writer, “ laughed at Sutton Sievier for the extravagant
prices he gave for yearlings at the Duke of Westminster’s
sale last year. The laugh is now on the plunger’s side.
Sceptre, for which he paid £lO,OOO, got him his money
back first time of asking, and is, at current prices, worth
half as much again. For another purchase, that has not
started yet, he could get £BOOO, or £2500 more than he
gave. As a matter of fact, Sievier and his trainer (Mor-
ton) are among the shrewdest judges of thoroughbred
stock in England, and have not made a bad purchase yet,
or a bad sale either.”
* • ¥ *
The objectionable feature of Clause 54 of the
Federal Post and Telegraph Bill may be defined in one
word—snuffle. It is a snufflesome clause. If it is passed
and enforced it will wipe out almost the only perfectly
clean and honest gamble Australia possesses and leave
the fraudulent ones unharmed. It will suppress “ Tatter-
sail ” because his honest gamble is mostly carried on
through the post, while the swarm of illegal tote-shops,
betting-shops and bookmakingestablishments will survive,
because they can do enough business with their victims
personally to keep them going. In fact, the suppression
of “ Tattersall ” will probably do them a benefit; a good
deal of the money that now goes into his honest gamble
will go into their dishonest ones instead. Of course, the
answer of the Federal Ministry is that the objection-
able people are beyond its jurisdiction. They
can’t be reached _by postal regulation, but
only by the criminal code and the police,
and the Federal Legislature controls neither of these.
On the other hand, it controls the post office, and it can
reach Tattersall. So it proposes to suppress Tattersall’s
gamble, as the one within its reach, and is devoutly sorry
it can’t suppress the others also.
* * * *
This is logical, yet illogical. As it can’t sup-
press the harmful, dishonest gambles it is exceedingly
unwise to suppress the comparatively harmless and honest
one which competes with them, and by its competition
prevents them growing to huge dimensions. If Tattersall
is bad (which isn’t the present question), it is still unadul-
terated foolishness to abolish him and thereby promote
the growth of something much worse, which theQommon-
wealth can’t abolish. If a teetotaller has jurisdiction
over good lager beer and not over bad rum, and he
abolishes lager and thereby drives people to rum, he
isn’t a good teetotaller,
* * * *
Of course, the Commonwealth Govt, pleads that
five out of six State Legislatures don’t tolerate
Tattersall’s gamble within their borders (or are
supposed not to do so) —therefore it is, only
carrying out the will of the majority. Thus does
the State legislator’s hypocrisy come home to him. The
States that expelled Tattersall felt confident that some
other State would take him in. The legislator who voted
for his expulsion (and who, in most cases, took occasional
tickets in Tattersall’s himself) could say to his gambling
constituents, “ I’ve done you no harm ; you have only to
send your money to Hobart instead of Brisbane or
Sydney,” and they were satisfied. Also, he could say to
his pious constituents, “ I’ve done all that is possible in
the Good Cause. Of course, we can’t legally interfere
with Tattersall’s letters addressed to another State, but,
so far as our jurisdiction extends, the bad man has been
suppressed.” So he pleased both parties. Now a power has
arisen which can shut Tattersall right off, and it is no
longer possible to please both parties.
THE LATE PETER JACKSON.
Photo. by Brand, Park-st., Sydney.
Pugilist Peter Jackson died on Saturday night
last at Roma (Q.), a victim of consumption. Fifteen
months ago he returned to Svdnev on the verge of death
from pneumonia, contracted at Skagway while on the
road to the Klondyke with Paddy Slavin. ’Murkan
doctors held out little hope of Peter’s recovery except
through extra-skilful treatment and nursing. He added
tissue, and after six months was as heavy as ever and
expressed himself sound as a hell—bar an agonising
attack of sciatica. When travelling with Fitzgeralds’
circus he caught cold in Gippsland, and had to lie up in
Geelong hospital for weeks. Thence on it was all down-
grade with him. Three months in Queensland (two of
which were spent at Roma) failed to arrest the trouble.
Weight fell off alarmingly, and attending doctor (Le
Strange) held out no hope of ultimate recovery, though
thinking it possible that life might be prolonged a while.
Jackson’s career was. remarkable. His triumphs
over Patsy Cardiff, George Godfrey, Joe M'Auliffe, Paddy
Slavin and other good. men, and the manner in which
they were scored, demonstrated unquestionably that the
world hadn’t previously seen his superior. Though not
generally known, Jackson, prior to meeting Jeffries, was
champion of the world, for “ Jonnel ” Sullivan (when
America’s top-notcher) after agreeing to meet the big
black at the California A.C. for a 20,000d01. purse,
wriggled out of his engagement on the advice of friends’
who had seen Jackson at it, thus losing his title by
default. Jackson already owned the Australian and
British supremacies. Peter’s battle with Slavin in London
is always referred to as among the fiercest and most
scientific ever fought by big men. He did little or
nothing afterwards, and a few years “ high time ” in the
English capital told the inevitable tale. Peter returned
to America a wreck to all intents and purposes, land
supplied an easy stepping-stone for Jim Jeffries towards
qualifying to meet Fitzsimmons, Corbett, Sharkey and co
Chagrined and broken-hearted at his vanquishme'nt Peter
gave way to dissipation for months. Resultant enfeebled
system and consequent greater susceptibility to disease
were the primary causes of the famous black’s demise
while yet under 40.
* • • *
Vagaries of Sydney referees of bruising matches
are intolerable. Saturday night last, at Golden Gate
A.C., Harden, a big rough-’un, fouled negro Ike Stewart
frequently and badly when he found his prospects “ blue.”
Ultimately Harden was disqualified, and Stewart got the
cash. “What about bets?” asked someone. “Well,”
replied the responsible official, “ I don’t want vous to lose
ver money on a foul, so I’ll declare all wagers off.” But
what about those who had won their money fairlv and
squarely ?
* * m *
Herb. M‘Kell-“ Snowy ” Sturgeon fight didn’t
transpire Monday night last. Influenza gripped Sturgeon
and laid him up. Announced as possible that they’ll get
together Wednesday next week, but how they’re going to
doit if Snowy’s illness is as severe as stated Heaven
only knows.
* * * *
Second meeting of Mick Dunn and Otto Cribb
occurs at Sydney Gaiety A.C. Monday night, Cribb’s first
scrap since his return from ’Frisco. Bruising students
are agog to see how he’ll “ cut.” Most favor Dunn’s pro-
spects, believing that the licking by Thurston has knocked
the aggressiveness which brought victory Cribb’s way
right out of him. Dunn remembers many errors of
method and judgment, and thinks the adoption of differ-
ent tactics will surely pull him through. Anyhow he’s
extremely confident, and that’s a telling factor in a
fighter’s kit. It’s safe to foretell a record attendance at
this match.
Australian “feather” bruiser Hegarty was to
have met Yankee bruiser Tim Callahan for a big purse on
June 28 in America, and, provided the verdict came his
way, a match with the ’Murkan terror, Terry McGovern,
for the world’s supremacy was to have followed.
Arrangements were made for receiving the news here if
Tim succeeded, but as no information has come, it looks
as if things went the wrong way.
* • * •
Larry Foley, having retired from one branch of
the “ knocking-down ” profession, is now regarded as at
the head of another, and is at present in charge of the
day-labor destruction of the buildings on the block
resumed for the new Sydney remote Railway Station at
Belmore Park. These include the old Benevolent Asylum,
the Convent of the Good Samaritan, the Female Refuge,
the Police Barracks, the Tramway sheds and the old resi-
dence of late Inspector-General M'Lerie. This is Larry’s
biggest undertaking so far, and he points with pride to
stacks of First Fleet bricks without a “ frog ” in one of
them.
• * * *
Newcastle (N.S.W.) ’rastler Jim McDermott,
lately wrecked in Melbourne by Oriental Buttan Singh,
came another cropper (Saturday) in the coal city, when
Maorilander Tweedie downed him. Jim should stick to
coal hewing—he’s built more for that. Writer never had
too exalted an opinion of him since, after challenging
Austrian Hajek with much flourish, he backed down.
There was also that affair with Teuton Lorenz.

Largest coursing stake provided in Australia for
years was Mooriee Valley Cup t’other daj r . Nominations
were accepted last Sept., when 157 subscribed but 24 less
responded. Three agreed to retire, and two more were
balloted out, leaving an even 128. Best hounds in the
Commonwealth, and three from M.L. lined up. N.S.W.
and Vic. premier hounds—Braddon’s Blot and Aqua
Fortis respectively—opened favorites at eights. Gold-
smith-Red Rose dog, Aqua Fortis and General Booth—
Sally bitch, Amy Girl, met in the decider and the former
won well. Amy Girl is a November puppy and hails from
Berrigan (N.S.W.). Previously she had only shaped pub-
licly once—in Benalla (Vic.) Oaks which fell to her.
Moonee Valley Purse, at same meeting, was appropriated
by Verse (Black Star —Poetess) from Gippsiand, a full
sister to Planet and Lucky Star who were among the last
four in the Cup. All three are owned by Clarendon Bros.
* * * •
First Q-N.S.W. Rugby match this year drew a
huge crowd, officially given as 16,000, but apparently 4000
more—the great deadhead army finding no place in
managerial reckoning. Receipts, £650 ; weather splen-
did. Cornstalk team proved more than equal to
the occasion, despite general adverse criticism.
Bananalanders claimed theirs was the strongest
lot ever sent to N.S.W., and yet the home side had
them fairly soft, winning by 17 to 10. Throughout initial
half Q. men had a stiff southerly with them and notched
10 to 5. Subsequently N.S.W. attacked persistently and
evidenced great powers of combination, though working
together for the first time. Visitors only menaced
opposition territory once, when victory was absolutely
hopeless. Sydney dailies now concede.that N.S.W. team
“ fully justifies the confidence of its selectors.”
>•* * *
At inter-State football match Q. team kept
things humming throughout the first half, and inside 16
min., they scored 7 per medium of two fine place kicks at
goal by Spragg, who shaped in great fettle. Once Colton
went fairly through the defending force and secured a
good try. Wickham (N.S.W.) kicked a sensational
penalty goal and, later, added a try. N.S.W. rural
youth Finlay and bruiser Conlon literally ran over oppos-
ing halves—Gralton and Evans. Wickham (previously
whispered too old for the game) never showed to better
advantage.

Cabled no-balling of Englishman Mold by
umpire Phillips is not surprising : he did the same last
year and raised a sensation. Proposal that square-leg
umpire should have equal right with bowler’s umpire in
calling “no ball ” originated with Phillips. Mold is
among the ’Ome trundlers who have been served with a
year’s caution.
* * * v
Cabled that Surrey cricketer, Abel, in the
match Gents v. Players, notched 247. Last year he made
a new record for centuries in one English season, putting
up 12. Abel also occupied third place in the averages,
with 56-34 to Ranji.’s 87-57 and O. B. Fry’s 61-18, but in
aggregates, with 2592, he ran second to Ranjitsinhji with
3965. This 247 is Abel’s highest since his big effort of 357
(not out) for Surrey v. Somerset at the Oval in May, 1899,
which was, until O. Hill’s 365 against N.S.W at Adelaide
last year, the second highest individual score on record
in first-class cricket.
• * * *
Atop of Jessop, Lilley, and Hayward, previously
announced, it is cabled that Fry, Foster, and Tyldesley
will come with M’Laren’s team for Australia. Fry is an
all-rounder, excelling at long-jumping and footbail, and,
according to old man W.G., “ one of the best amateur bats
playing in first-class cricket.” He shapes free and hits
hard and clean. Foster is only 23, but last year he wiped
out the previous ’Varsity batting record with 171, and,
later, hit up 102 (not out), and 136 for Gentlemen against
Players. His last season’s average for nine matches was
77-50. Cricket authority “ Wisden ” says—“ Foster has
almost every good quality as a bat, strong defence, self-
restraint, a free, attractive style, and magnificent hitting
powers all round the wicket. Scarcely anyone, Ranjit-
sinhji excepted, hits with less apparent effort.” Tyldesley
has been operating for Lancashire since ’95. He scores
freely, puts heaps of power into his work, and fields ex-
cellently.
* * * *
Australasian golf premiership, at Botany last
week, fell to the Victorian crack, Mr. H. A. Howden,
who started golfing at North Berwick, Scotland, when
an urohin of five. First winner of the distinction was
M.L. rep. (Mr. Gillies) who scored at Bondi in ’99, and, on
his opening day’s play this time, seemed to have a good
thing. He put up a new amateur “ best ” (82) for the
links. Illness stopped Mr. L. A. Whyte, last year’s win-
ner, from defending his position. “ Victorians Brooks
and Riddell wrested the doubles’ championship of the
continent from 17 N.S.W. players, 7 Victorians, 2 ’Ome
men, and 1 each from Q., Tas. and M.L.

Roberts, playiflg at Melb. Athenaeum Hall
last Friday, showed a reminiscence of his real old
champion self. It was the last night of an exhibition
match with Smith, and Premier Barton and Go. had come
along on the understanding that the old man would
shine. And the “old man” shone. Taking up an un-
finished break of 110 he added 126 to it. After some
consistently good play, he ran out with an unfinished
break of 325. This was the Roberts of yore, able to
“ come off ” whenever he wanted to.
And now sticklers for the quite c’rect
thing say that the Duke isn’t the Duke of Corn-
wall and York, after all. He’s only Lord of the
Duchy of C., and Duke of Y.
John Norton’s State Rights crusade started
well at the big Sydney Protestant Hall meeting,
the other night. That is if size of audience can
be taken as a criterion. No doubt eternal watch-
fulness is needed to prevent the big Common-
wealth animal from kicking its own limbs into
splinters: even a well-intentioned beast may
tread on itself from sheer clumsiness. But Nor-
ton mistakes the off hind-leg of the gee-gee
for the whole quadruped, and his audience,
though entertained by John’s oratorical abuse of
Barton, was anything but enthusiastic over
his screeching denunciations of that Horrible
Ogre, the Federal Parliament. After having
attended a dozen debates in both Houses of the
Federal Parliament, present writer has no hesita-
tion in declaring that, if it comes to trusting any
legislative body at all, his faith is firm in the
ability of the Commonwealth Legislature to pull
Australia through, despite John Norton’s absence
therefrom. It is obviously true, as predicted by
Bulletin years ago. that while no mere State
might produce a brainy Parliament from among
its limited population, four million people would
produce a legislature at least four times more
brainy than any one State’s choice. So, despite
Norton’s yells about N.S.W. apathy and Want’s
exhortations to that State to “ Awake ! Awake!”
etc., these two ill-assorted Johns are likely to con-
stitute the whole procession of protest. N.S.W.
will probably continue apathetic, and won’t awake
or arise to any extent worth mentioning,

At Poverty Point.
“Westral” writes from Perth (W.A.): Nance O’Neil
swooped down on us here with a blaze, a bang, and a
turmoil of feathers, flesh and stormy silks that fairly
startled Perth. It was a new brand of acting. It seemed
to me I could hear, with the inward ear of the mind, ob-
servations jerked in, between sentences, calling our
urgent and immediate attention to her dress, her shoul-
ders, diamonds. All the time she seemed to be saying,
“ What do you think of my clothes ? ” Somebody or other
(couldn’t notice who, Nance took the whole circus to her-
self) would say :—“Magda, do you still adhere to your
resolve?” Then bang! swish, swish ! and Nance would
charge up the stage and show her rear elevation. Next,
posing up against a sideboard : “ Nothing can make me
change,” and then the unspoken but quite visible remark :
“ What about that for a pair of shoulders, eh?” “This
is final, then ?” Why, certainly— doesn’t this frock hang
all right in front?” “ And you will leave this house ”
Nance prances over to a table—“At once (leans over
table) — Say, how s that for a bust ? ” “ The happiness of
those dear to you'? ” (Back now presented.) “My dear
parson— isn’t this train a perfect peach ? —when
I came here (stretches out arm) — what about
this for a set of diamonds ? Twenty thousand
dollars if they cost a cent —to see my old home I was
actuated by a sense of affection— look at the embroidery
round the bottom ! —for my old father, and I fail to see
why I should sacrifice my art— You can't beat this lace
by much ! ” —and so on, right through the piece. It was
only when she had a row with the old man that she
warmed up to it and looked anything like a hot, violent
woman that has been out battling with the world. All
the rest was Art, not Nature. Self-consciousness floated
up all the time in her jerky way of saying : “ You say—
that you—have given up- your life —for me.” “ Magda’s”
a horrible play, anyhow. The flaring, flaunting returned
prodigal, ripping about in the quiet, homely scene, is an
oppressive picture. Nance’s tearing round in a willy-
willy all the time in “ Queen Elizabeth ” made Perth sit
back and gasp. They are swallowing her with a lot of
straining here, like the gnat and the Pharisee in the New
Testament. But we are a small community, and
when the gnat’s bigger than the Pharisee it is bad for the
latter.
Miss Elsie Hall, the young Australian pianiste, was
“ commanded ” last Sunday to play at Govt. House,
Adelaide, before the Dookanduteh and Lord and Lady
Tennyson. This is the first time in the history of
Australia that a woman has sat in a Professorial chair ;
Miss Elsie Hall is acting as locum tenens for six months at
Adelaide University in the absence of Professor Riemanns.
The Boucicault-Irene Vanbrugh wedding has been fixed
for “ the end of July,” and the happy pair-to-be are
under professional engagement to Charles Frohmann.
Just “ passed-out,” old native-born Australian actor,
J. J. Walsh. “ Jack ” was one of the most straightfor-
ward and honest men in the profession. Neither a foul
word nor any strong drink ever passed his lips. His first
engagement was with Gordon Griffiths, at the Victoria
Theatre, Sydney, where he afterwards appeared with G.
V. Brooke. He was the original Harvey Duff and the
original Peter Crawley in the Australian productions of
“ The Shaughraun ” and “ Never Too Late to Mend.”
Seems that the choristers at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) were
compelled to sing their “ Cavalleria ” music in English,
owing to the management having omitted to get the
necessary chorus parts from Italy. Chorus language is of
very little consequence in an opera, anyhow. It was not
until the stage crowd came out of church and began to
trill “a merry lay” to the “ happy Easter Day” that a
few people in the audience pricked up their ears, with the
result that they presently caught the lady-villagers sing-
ing “ Mistress Lola, this is no place for you ” Nobody
could mistake that for an Italian remark.
Even in “ Florodora," where the chorus gets more
“ fat ” than in the average English show of the kind, the
stage management is not half as keen as you find it in the
musical comedies from America. “ A Trip to Chinatown”
was the first striking illustration of the Yankee method
of making the background people work all the time for
the good of the business. And touching the latest
American comedy a London paper remarks —“ Every
nameless member of the co. contributes to the animation
of the scene. If the nodding of heads in time, and the
waving of red silk scarves, and the kicking of the chorus
have no definite meaning, these movements impart a
friskiness to the action. Better the volcanic energy of
the American girl than the inertia of the lovely ladies, in
beautiful dresses, who decorate the stage at so many of
our theatres.” Amen to that.
Of the musical critics who now denounce the tremolo
in the voices of Italian operatic artists, none had the
courage—if they had the inclination—to discern it in
Ella Russell. Writer saw Ella once only, in “ Faust.”
Throughout the opera her voice shook like a cinemato-
graph, and kept him off any other appearances of the
much-belauded pritna-donna.
Unsuccessful actor Earl of Yarmouth has left the stage
to take service with Kooseveldt and Schuyler, a well-
known N.Y. firm, as an agent for the sale of champagne.
Carlo Dani, Williamson’s second tenor, is the beau ideal
of an operatic tenor ; he has a good appearance, an easy
and refined manner, and a voice that flows like honeyed
milk. The sort of voice that men and women idolise.
“ With us, it is always a tenor,” says Magda.
“ J.A.C.” Melb.” (22/6/1). Writing of “old-time"
opera in Australia has omitted from his list of “ best-
known conductors of the past,” the names of, possibly,
the three best of the bunch, viz., Alberto Zelman, Paolo
Giorza, and John Hill—the latter one of lima di Murska’s
husbands.

after a five years’tour of the East from India to Japan,
and invites applications in another column from persons
willing to join a Dramatic, and also a Musical, Ballet and

George Conquest, son of old George and Mrs. Conquest,
died in London the other day, aged 64. Australia knew
him by name as a manufacturer and dealer in melodrama
•of the “ Surrey ” brand, and many a 4-act horror has been
produced in this country by arrangement with George
Conquest, Esq. His father (real name, Benjamin Oliver)
was a cobbler, with large business capacity and a turn for
the stage. Instead of sticking to his last the cobbler
broke out as a low comedian at the Garrick Theatre,
Whitechapel, and in due course married a clever panto,
columbine, who helped him to save money. The thrifty
ex-cobbler became lessee and manager of the Garrick.
He lived on the premises, and it was there that young
George first saw the light, and lived until—as a boy of 9
—he was rescued with difficulty from the burning estab
lishment. In ’5l Conquest the elder began to make his
fortune at the Eagle, a playhouse with tavern attached
in the City Road. The name lives in a Cockney nursery
rhyme which embodied a temperance moral —
Up and down the City Road,
In and out the Eagle,
That’s the way the money goes—
Pop goes the weazel !
But the solemn significance of the ancient doggerel has
long been lost. Conquest re-christened his theatre the
Grecian, and, 30 years later, his son, the late George, who
inherited the property at the old man’s death, sold it to
the Salvation Harmy, and bought the Surrey Theatre
with part of the proceeds. During those 30 years the
Grecian grew famous for its annual pantomimes, wherein
“ young” George Conquest (he afterwards trained up his
son, the younger George, to the business) developed won-
derfully the “vampire-trap” system of flying leaps and
dives. Every panto, at the Grecian had a flying demon
or two in it. Conquest was the “ star ” demon. He had
his mishaps; broke his nose beyond repair, broke his
collar-bone and his arm, and his ribs sometimes—bub
never broke his neck. And all this time he w'as making
plays, with and without assistance, for the use of the
company when the panto, season was “off.” The old
man had sent him to get a musical education. George
fils got hold of French play MSS., and made new melo-
dramas from them. Also, he got hold of Pettitt and Paul
Merritt (now long dead), and other writers in later years,
and collaborated with them. He must have had a hand
in the concoction of fully 50 melodramas, mostly
rubbish, but invariably actable. A remarkable
worker, with a great practical knowledge of all things
theatrical. From a multitude of ballet-girl novices,
George Conquest could pick the born artist with unerring
eye. His mother was the best ballet mistress of her day,
and George, in his youth, had seen hundreds of girls
licked into shape. One of those girls, by the way, may
be said to have created the “ royal ballerinas ” for J. O.
Williamson. Anyway, the W. and M. Firm’s dancers
were not a special feature of comic-opera productions
prior to the engagement of Marie Reddall (Mrs. Teddy
Royce) as instructress. She taught the famous original
“ Old Guard ” dancers, among them Miss Minnie Everett,
the lady superior of Williamson’s royal ballerinas nowa-
days.
Spruson, late M.P. for Syclnev-Gipps
division, hammered the last nail into his political
coffin when he said something to the effect that
the didn’t care a rap about the labor-vote — he
didn’t require it, and would walk into Parliament
over the dead body of labor. That ill-advised
snort brought the dead labor-body of Gipps elec-
torate back to life again, and it walked over
Spruson.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lee being ap-
pealed to by a deputation of returned contingen-
ters, is quite certain, on their statements, that they
are entitled to receive the Australian rate of pay
and the Imperial rate of pay as well—two pays, in
fact. And after all his complaints about the ex-
travagance of the See Government, he intends, in
bis just and generous indignation, to raise Cain in
the Assembly because the soldiers haven’t been
paid twice over.
A melancholy case is that of an old lady,
of 82 years, mother of a well-known Sydney tem-
perance-agitator, who died not long ago. She
was born in Germany, and arrived in N.S.W.
when but two years of age. She married a
German, who became naturalised, but did not
herself go through that form, and, as her hus-
band’s naturalisation does not affect her status as
a “ foreigner,” her application for an old-age pen-
sion has, under the statute, to be refused.
The introduction of lascars on Orient
steamers is a disgrace to Australian democracy.
For upwards of 20 years the Orient Co. has em-
ployed white crews, in competition with the
cheaper black crews of the P. and O. Co. ; and
Australia has not had the courage and
persistence to enforce a contract clause
compelling the P. and O. line to employ
white labor on mail - subsidised steamers.
Meanwhile, most of the wealthy Australian snobs
have travelled by the P. and O. line; and the
Orient Co., after doing more than its share in the
contest, has been compelled to follow suit with
black crews. The Commonwealth Senate threw
out the proposal to put an anti-blaclc labor
clause in mail contracts. It is not too late for
the House of Representatives.
The coroner’s jury has decided that the
fatal fire at the Auckland Grand Hotel was caused
by Jessie Minns and some other persons un-
known. This is what comes of having a vivid
imagination. If Jessie had simply stated in the
witness-box that she said her prayers as usual,
and went to bed in a nice white night-dress with
a snowy frill, and slept soundly, dreaming occasion-
ally about her mother, or her sainted aunt in heaven,
or her baby sister, until aroused by the call of
fire, and then she seized her cherished bible and
escaped—she would have attracted no attention.
But her tremendous fish-lies fixed public attention
on her, and this is the result. The person who
knows when to be obscure and pious and ignorant
and asleep gets out of many difficulties. If little
Jessie did set the hotel on fire she evidently did it
in order to get her name in the papers, and she
got there right enough.
Phil May’s health, it is plain from recent
London letters, is in a very serious state.
Vic. Premier Peacock has refused Cham-
berlain’s demand that the Governor’s allowance
shall be increased by £1000, in defiance of Parlia-

and memorable next time he meets the electors.
Returned contingenter Kenneth Mackay
warns Australians against Rhodes’ baits. “Rho-
desia wouldn’t stand stocking by squatters ; and
anyway it is impossible because full of stock
diseases. Pioneering there spells ruin. Further,
it is ruled by the autocratic chartered co.”_ A
similar warning was given by a returned miner
recently; so Australians who are fools enough to
be gulled by the promise of free land will deserve
to lose their capital.
What kind of management is it which sets
fire brigade men to do laborers’ work clearing out
the ruins of Hordern’s building? The Sydney
firemen aren’t numerous enough to do their own
work, which is putting out fires. Why not let
the job of digging out the ruins fall on the
Government Astronomer or the Auditor-General,
who have about as much to do with it as the
firemen ?
After their experience at Hordern’s, it
would be interesting to know how the Fire
Brigade people would propose to effect a rescue
from the tops of the large hotels of Sydney, or
from buildings like the huge piles erected by the
Life Assurance co.’s. The inability of the Fire
Brigade authorities to cope with an emergency,
following on the heels of the revelations about the
unpreparedness of the Navigation Department in
the hour of need of the passenger s.s. Manly, has
come as a shock to the public. Of course the
Brigade officials are little, if at all, to blame, for
time and again has the Supt. appealed for ad-
ditional men and plant, and additional room to
work in. Yet plenty money can be found for
“decorating” N.S.W. Lands Office with useless
alleged statuary.
“V.J.D.” : I knew well Charles Junor,
who was drowned the other night when stepping
ashore from North Sydney ferry. Met him first
in Melbourne where he came to see me when I
was ill. That was years ago. Afterwards we did
some work together. He was brimming over
with boyish spirits, and had, withal, a certain
rapid bird-like way of pouncing upon
ideas, and getting at the root of things.
He was also well read, and could write
well on many subjects, and was a good journalist
and a good fellow all round. I have noticed it as
a curious fact that, amongst my friends who have
died, the most genial and warm-hearted died
suddenly, and not in their beds. I think that, on
the whole, it is the better way. But it is not so
well for those they leave behind. Charles Junor
left behind him a wife and three young children.
Edwin J. Brady, author of “ The Ways of
Many Waters,” is now proprietor and editor of
that smart little Grafton (N. S. W.) paper, The Grip.
Every poet should make up his mind to save the
moneys he would otherwise la-vish on cigars, and
champagne, and fried fish and other luxuries, and
invest the same in a journal of his own. He would
then be able to print what he darned well pleased
and snap his fingers at cold-eyed editors. But, set-
ting aside this view of the matter, Mr. Brady is
not only a poet of no mean order, but also a clever,
capable, and industrious journalist, and an honor-
able man, and should make a success of The Grip.
The Bulletin wishes him all sorts of luck.
The 2lsb annual report of Sydney Chil-
dren’s Hospital shows that eminently deserving
institution to be in a fairly prosperous financial
condition ; though, of course, with _ Sydney s
rapidly increasing population, there is always
much room for further public assistance.
There were during the year 58!) little patients
under treatment, of whom the large proportion of
39(5 were discharged, cured ; and no fewer than
581 operations were performed, 221 of which were
upon in-patients.
Cabled that Santos Dumont, in a navigable
balloon driven by electricity, has risen from St.
Cloud outside Paris, made a tour of the Bois de
Boulogne, gone six times round Longchamps race-
course, circled the Eiffel Tower at a height of
340ft., and safely descended. Last week it was
cabled that a French submarine boat made
a successful voyage to Corsica. France’s birth-
rate, like Britain’s and Australia s, is falling;
but France still nourishes some of the best brains
in Europe.
N.S.W. Lands Minister Crick to a New-
castle audience: —
During his administration of the Post Office he had
several times received reports of embezzlements by officers
of the department, with requests for authority to prose-
cute. He always asked what salary the person was
receiving, and if be found that it was a poor one, he
said : “ No, sack him, and let him get a billet where he
will be paid a decent wage.” It was, in his opinion, much
less an offence for a man, whose wife and children were in
want, to take a few bob than many other offences that
were looked lightly upon by silver-tailed society. (Loud
applause.) He ought, perhaps, to be in gaol himself.
(Laughter.) Once, while he and a friend were travelling
in the back blocks, they were feeling very hungry. They
saw a sheep belonging to some squatter, and they vei'\
soon had mutton-chops for dinner. (Laughter.) He did
not see that he had committed any offence. He didn t
see why he should starve when there was a good sheep
there. ‘ (Applause.)
Later on, Crick became a lawyer, and happened
upon even fatter sheep.
The aDpeal for a re-consideration of the
case of Mrs. Cecile Anderson, now serving a 20-year
sentence for the alleged murder of Fraser, evoked
from Vic. Solicitor-General Wynne the cold-
blooded, cruel, stereotyped answer that lie would
be quite prepared to consider any new facts that
were laid before him. Wynne knows perfectly
well that the only point at issue is that the verdict
was an outrage when viewed in the light of the
old facts. The further outrage is, that a person
who is sued in the civil courts, and has a £500
verdict given against •him, can get three or four
re-hearings on the old facts (witness the case of
Huddart, Parker and Co. and the woman whose
husband was drowned in the Alert), and the per-
son whose life is involved can’t get one.
W. Hamilton, member for Gregory (Q.),
asserts that South Australia scoops in most of the
trade of extreme Western Queensland. During
drought-time it cost £33 to land a ton of flour
in Boulia by the Townsville-Winton route. The
same could be landed via Hergott from Adelaide
for £28. Queensland sugar can actually be
shipped round to Adelaide and sent into Boulia at
a less cost than it can be brought by rail and road
from Townsville. And yet the S.A. railage is
very much longer than the Q. railage, and the
length of road little different. Queensland shows
little business tact in this matter.

“Castro’s Last Sacrament and Other
Stories,” by Albert Dorrington (“Alba Dorion ”)
is the best book of short stories yet produced in
Australia. The S. Daily Telegraph recognises
the author’s “absolute genius.” TheS.M. Herald
says the volume “ contains dramatic pictures
which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,
post-free from Bulletin office for 4s. 6d.
Senator Smith, of Westralia, on the
Federal capital site :
The best thing to do was to drop the report into the
wastepaper-basket, and become their own commissioners.
They could then decide on a dozen or so sites, and leave
the Federal Parliament to select the best one. There
should be at any rate an area of 100) sq. miles, for if the 10-
nnle radius were adhered to they would have land-
grabbers and speculators buying up all the land outside
that radius, and forming suburbs in which people would
live rather than within the Commonwealth area. If they
had the large area suggested, they would be able to test
some of the great social problems now agitating the
world. It was of great imp irtance that they should have
a sufficient area to meet the great development which
must naturally take place in years to come.
Minister O'Connor, in his reply, said that the
question of 1000 square miles, or more, or les3, was
largely one of expense. In this matter two good
things are already noticeable. The gang which
wanted the Federal Government to rule, not own,
the Federal Territory, so that the boomers and
land-banks might grab all the promising allot-
ments in the new city even before the Govern-
ment got there, has been killed so dead that it is
hardly heard of now. And the ridiculous proposal
to limit the Federal Territory to 100 square miles
—64.000 acres, or little less than one-third the area
of Melbourne—so that even the capital itself
would presently have to spread into the adjacent
State outside Federal control, has almost dropped
out of sight, though a few interested parties try
now and then to keep it alive.

Benjamin Judkins, an Adelaide astrologer,
threw this bombshell into the holy city right on
the eve of York’s visit:
“To authors, journalists, preachers, statesmen, and
leaders of thought.—Are you building the ark of safety ?
If not, take solemn warning. It may not be known 'to
many that we are coming into the most startling astrolo-
gical changes the world has ever known. We may even
lose the sun, moon, and stars. All sections of society
will be profoundly affected by the mighty changes, which
will shake the world of letters, and men whose hearts are
not on the side of justice will surely melt. Times and
seasons change, and people with them. One thousand
nine hundred and one ends the age that Christ, the Bible
past-masters in the science of history, and star-gazers
have foretold. Next November a grand conjunction of
Jupiter and Saturn, which only occurs once in 2C years
will be seen in the western skv, about sunset; which is
ominous indeed, politically. But in December all the
great planets of the solar system will be in a direct line
with our earth and the sun in the sign Gapricornus for
the first time since the great flood in Noah’s day.
Raphael, the great English astrologer, says, in his
almanac, we are closing a cycle of 5000 years, hard as
iron, and black as hell. The ‘ Flaming Sword ’ of Chicago,
U.S.A., says we are entering the aquarial sign of the new
age, which will be one of light. Science will shine forth.
Sunlight is essential to organic life. Human beings, like
planets, fade after long confinement in darkness.”
It is certainly hard luck for S.A. that she has a
Premier named Jenkins and an astrologer named
Judkins.

Wore Them Out Herself.
There was a time when Mrs. Hayes considered
herself to be what she calls “a gone woman."'
She actually divided her clothes and other
personal effects among her children. Thank
goodness—but here is her story, told in her
own way ; by all odds the best way.
“ Three years ago,” she says, “I had dreadful
pains across the left side of my stomach and under
the shoulder-blades. My left side swelled up
fearfully. I was laid up weeks at a time, work
being out of the question. While these fits were
on I could neither walk, sit, nor stand with
comfort.
I was really ashamed to let the neighbours
see me crawling about; so I spent most of my
time lying down or leaning against something to
ease the dreadful pains.
“I had been a hard-working woman all my life
but now I lost my strength and dreaded to eat
anything, knowing the woeful suffering I was
sure to experience afterwards; as if eating were
somehow a crime against the laws of nature.
And at night I rolled and tossed about instead of
sleeping.
“ The doctor said it was indigestion, and no
doubt he was right, but he was not able to relieve
“I consi lered myself a ‘gone woman’ and told
my husband I was sure I could not last much
longer. Indeed, I was so fully persuaded of this,
that 1 actually divided my clothes and personal
effects among my children.
Thank goodness and Mother Seigel’s Syrup
I nave since worn out most of them myself.
“After a lot of coaxing and argument (for I
was tired of trying things, and hope had about
died away m my heart) I consented to take
oeigel s Syrup, although the doctor had advised
me not to touch it.
i “ T n °t quite sure of the effect of the first
bottle, but my husband insisted on my goino - on
with it. So I did go on with it, and after l°had
got through half the second bottle there was no
doubt of the result. I was much better : I felt it
and others could see it.
c. " I^'^ as hardl y short of a miracle, the wav
Seigel s Syrup brought me round. From a poor
weak, and wretched woman, unable to work or
scarcely to raise my hand to do the smallest
piece of work, it gave me back health and strength
restored me to ray husband and family, enabled
me to go on with my work once more, and, in
short, made me as well as ever I was in my life
“ I am now upwards of 60, and have reared a
large family. I have lived in the district about
of years, and am well known here.
“My husband and sons, as well as our grand-
children, work m connection with the coal mines
for which the district is noted. I have told all
the neighbours what Seigel’s Syrup did for me
and am perfectly willing that my case should be
published if you think it may be useful ( Mrs )
Julia Hayes, Mount Keira, Paradise, near
Wollongong, N.S.W. October 14th, 1899.
Mr. John Hickey, blacksmith, at the same
place, writes that he has known IVlrs. Haves all
his life, and (in common with many others) knows
her statement to be true. lie adds that she is
respected by everyone.*

ELECTRO-MEDICAL & SURCICAL INSTITUTE.

Specialties : Nervous and Chronic Complaints, Blood and Skin Diseases,
Heart; Kidney, Liver, Bladder; Rheumatism, Sciatica; &c., (fee.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Registration by the Govern-
ment Medical Boards of New South Wales, Victoria, South Australia, Greax
Britain and America, open to inspection at the Consulting Rooms
by our Physician-in-Chief.
TT is an acknowledged fact the world-wide over that no
single individual or combination of men can he ab-
solute masters of every phase and branch of art, science,
medicine, or other professed avocation of a limitless
sphere of operation and magnitude. At best the greatest
scientists of the day—men like Lord Kelvin and Edison.
: : : :
Yours truly . . .
HOWATiT) FREEMAN,
Sydney’s Leading Specialist. j
Professor of Electricity as applied Medically, Member
Dr. Harmon’s College, Washington Cor., Cal., U.S.A.
Director and Founder of the Institute.
for instance, whose fame is universally known—class
themselves as infants in scientific invention and research,
and modestly tell us that they have but a smattering of
knowledge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only to look at the fine buildings that occupy
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their professional
work. But to have the privilege of being shown througn
the building by the Specialists themselves, and to see ana
learn something of the real character of their vvide prac-
tice, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in-
dividual like the writer.
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles witn which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. The collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument conies to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Game
batteries, the finest french makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable ex e-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Aus r iba
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are in
view.
What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to lv alth and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “I can assure you,” said Dr. Wallace,
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fonder
/
■
Premises = Cor. Elizabeth and Bathurst Sts. (Opp. Hyde Park, Sydney
(All Trams Stop at the Door).
of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
phenomenal power.
Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions—that of prescribing for the ills and
•diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “gullibles” are not so plentiful as the}' used to
be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
ability.
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
| position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
| doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
I which have been the study of their professional lives.
I When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
j associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
i tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
i some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows.
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
be pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and Superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “ Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallace
Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”
Noble sentiments, thought the writer, as the time came
for his departure, and the Specialists returning to their

M.D., L.R.C.r. , L.F.P.S., L.M.,
