consulting-room. How many doctors in our midst can
we bracket in this category ? Dinners and drives and
conversaziones are more frequently uppermost in the
mind of top-loftical medicos than the speedy treatment
of sad and suffering patients.
Victoria’s five new Legislative Councillors
are of the usual variety—just as stupid and soggy
an ,. well-to-do as all previous batches, and just as
satisfied that a powerful Leg. Council is essen-
tial to the stability of the country and to the
maintenance of confidence and the promotion of
morality. A week or so earlier six new M’s.L.A.
were sent into the Assembly all in favor of a
popularly-elected Convention to frame a new
otate Constitution. The five M’s.L.C. are all
fiercely and fatly opposed to the Convention.
And the Argus observes, with the complacence
of a very large round man who has just eaten
deeply, that “ the country having spoken the Con-
vention idea ought now to go by the board.” The
M’s.L.A. who favor the Convention were elected
by the people, every elector being qualified to be a
member, so that the people could put up men of
their own way of thinking. The M’s.L.C. who
oppose the Convention were elected by about half
of the people, with the restriction that they were
only allowed to choose rich property-owners, aged
30 years or upwards. And they chose M’s.L.C.
who were opposed to the Convention idea, because
all the men they could find who were in favor of
it were barred out by the property qualification.
“Perce Abbott” holds forth to The
Bulletin :—
In listening to the monstrosities called gram, or grapbo-
phones, one is struck by the diabolical accent and intona-
tion of the cowboys or lumbermen who talk or sing into
them, r n 1 one wonders how such men get employment at
an occupation requiring a iittle education and elegance of
speech. First, when the “ apparatus ” is ready, a ruffian
howls through his nose in the voice of a door-man at a
dime museum—“Saung ! ‘ Mawlley awn ther Trawlley.’
Sung by Mr. Jawhn B. Woolfe fur the Amur’k’n Phunna-
graf Cnmanee uv Noo Yarls’n Shikoggo, acc imaneed awn
ther peeana by Purfesser Giesenberg.” This speech comes
out in little jerks as if the doorman’s talking “ fit
up ” were operated by tbe explosion of small plugs
dynamite. One unspeakable “ record ” haunts me like
the ghost of a tortured hyena. In it four strident
dingoes lacerate what is called an “ Edison quortette.’
It begins with a short chorus through the nose, and
then a person, whom I judge to be Jacob M'Closky,
drawls out through the same valuable organ : “ Neow,
boys’n gor's, hev suthin’ to d.enk on me. _ Whot’ll ye
hev ? ” Then a demon of the Hartz mountains screams :
“ I’ll tek a liT whesky.” An “Irishman ” says : “ Gimme
a gra' hi ■: gleass a beer an’ mixhed ale wid a drap a Mony-
gahaley in it.” This is supposed to be funny, for
the rest laugh. Then a woman’s voice, the most
fearsome Punch -and-Jud.v of all, yells: “Oh, ray!
gimme one a them, too.” Now Jacob comes in
again : “ l sea.v, Meabel, ain’t ye gonna gimme
that kess ye prawraised neow ? ” There is a smack and a
screech from “ Mabel,” who squeals : “ Goo’ ni’, Chorley !
Oh, I hev hed soch a lovely time,” and the nightmare
ends with “ Jingle Bells,” which sounds as if sung by a
chorus of lost souls in a lunatic asylum in hell. Patti
knew something when she refused to allow her match-
less voice to be reproduced in the awful thing. I once
knew a profane hut modest tenor—that is, he was modest
for a tenor, inasmuch as he never said he was better than
Sims Reeves, hut only admitted he was quite as good.
He was persuaded to sing “ Pretty Jane ” into a phono-
graph, and waited anxiously to hear his own sweet
tones. jThe operator brushed the record, fitted it
on, fastened up the fog-horn, turned a screw
and then let it loose. At first, there was a sizzling sound,
as of frying tough steak in a red-hot pan. It was so rea-
listic, you could almost smell the burning fat. The tenor
said, trembling, “That’s not me, is it?” “No, sh—
wait,” whispered the operator. Then came sounds as of
a man being badly hanged, trying to sing his dying con-
fession through a sausage-machine: “ Oh-z-Jane. Oh!
p.fzz. pret ssss-n;ver-zzzzz, shy yok yok yok, in the even-
ing whe:z:-bloom is-gloog gloog, bz-wlisss zif zzz
Just here, the tenor kicked the foghorn into the air and
swore for a minute or so. His remarks would have made
a better record than his song, and would have sold to
bulloekies at a pound each. At last he said, with tears in
his voice : “ Do I sing like that? If I thought I did, I’d
cut my blanky throat.”

The Bulletin has received from the
M r - John Parker, a pamphlet entitled
A Few Facts and Anecdotes regarding Blind
People and Blindness.” As Mr. Parker himself
is sightless, he knows from bitter experience what
he is writing about. The little production is full
of interesting information of how these much-
afflicted people read, write, take notes and find
their ways about cities. The price is one shilling,
a c ?P ies can now be obtained at Dymock’s Book
Arcade, Sydney. The work is more than worth
the money. At present, Mr. Parker is trying to
organise a Blind Concert Co. to tour the Common-
wealth,

and York were in Sydney prescriptions were
dispensed for Her Royal Highness at H. A.
Rose s Pharmacy, George-street. *
One of the biggest surprises of N.S.W.
w elections was the big majority gained by
Willis for The Barwon. He was opposed
by W. H. Wright, an old resident of the electo-
rate—a dull and dreary platform orator, but a
personal favorite, pledged to the Labor-party and
supported by N.S. W. Farmers’ and Settlers’As-
sociation. Willis’s supporters trembled, and
every nerve was strained on his behalf. Squatters
canvassed and coerced their employees, swagrnen
(who had their rights) were supplied with free
provisions ; pamphlets, newspapers, and letters
from leading politicians were profusely circu-
lated, and even then, at the eleventh hour, a close
contest was expected. When the voting at Wal-
gett (chief polling centre) was declared, Wright
wnr major,t 7 When all returns were in,
Willis was 3115 ahead. The result staggered both
parties.

A North Shore Restaurateur
Overcomes her Liver Trouble.
The Case of Mrs. F. FROGLEY.
(by our own reporter.)
The Post Office Dining and Refreshment
Rooms in North Sydney are situated at 87
Mount-street. This well-appointed restau-
rant was lately visited by a reporter, who
had an interview with Mrs. Fannie Frogley,
the wife of the proprietor.
“ I hear you were very ill some time ago ? ”
queried the scribe.
“ Yes. It is now a number of years since
my sickness began. I had a terrible ex-
ferience, and I feared my time was at hand.
was not married then, and I lived at
Braidwood. For years I was a sufferer
with indigestion, and as I grew older
so my ailments became more numerous
and obstinate. One doctor said my heart
was very weak, whilst another doctor said
I had heart disease. I really thought so
myself, and was in a state of constant fear
that something dreadful would suddenly
happen.”
“ Your liver may have been in a bad
state ? ”
“ I was sure of that, because the doctors
told me such was the case long before my
heart was said to be diseased. A peculiar
tasting gas was always arising from my
stomach, and my mouth was never without
the same forbidding flavour. Flatulence was
caused through my food fermenting, turning
sour, and feeling like a heavy load on my
stomach, instead of digesting. I felt the
wind roaming about my inside, and causing
the most acute pains, which were aggravated
by pain in the side and chest. Then I was
terrified through my heart palpitating
fiercely. Quite suddenly the attacks came
on, and each time I distinctly heard my
heart beating against my ribs for awhile,
then the violence abated gradually, and
again increased, till I was almostfrightened
to death with a dread that it would stop
beating altogether. My liver was un-
doubtedly diseased, otherwise my complaints
would not have been so many.”
“ Had you any more sources of trouble ? ”
“ I had. My appetite was not worth
calling one. It was fickle and poor for a
long time, then left me altogether. I was
not sorry, in a way, because everything I
used to eat did not agree with me, and my
sufferings were only intensified. I often
vomited what I ate as soon as down,
and fits of retching lasted some time, pro-
ducing drowsy, sickly headaches, which
made me awfully miserable. Pricking pains
assailed me under the shoulder and lower
ribs, my eyes were dim and weak, and I
became as white as death. I was so thin
that the people said the wind would one
day blow me away, and my system was so
completely robbed of strength that I could
scarcely walk. The least effort made me
feel faint and dizzy, and my exhausted con-
stitution was a martyr to colds. I could not
go out into the open-air without catching a
chill, which showed that I was in a dread-
fully weak state. My nervous system felt
the severe strain of my sickness, and as I .
was always trembling and shaking, no
wonder I was so terror-stricken. My mind
was tormented with horrible thoughts,
which kept me awafce night after night.
Another very strange thing happened. I
became quite deaf. The shriek of a steam
whistle could not penetrate my ears. Some
of the leading specialists were consulted,
and they all said the drums of my ears were
gone, and the sense of hearing would never
be recovered.”
“• That would have been a dreadful
affliction.” . ,
“So I thought. The very idea of it dis-
tressed me greatly, and I fell into a low
state of despondency. I lost all interest in
life, and became resigned to my fate. Con-
tinually ailing and suffering intense agony
made me almost wish for death to release
me, although in reality I feared it when my
heart troubles came on.”
“ Did the doctors give you any relief ? ”
“ Not a bit, although I took no end of
medicine from them. It was evident that I
was only throwing money away, bo I com-
menced doctoring myself. I tried almost
every medicine advertised ; but not one of
them brought the relief for which I was
vainly searching.”
“But you look the picture of health
now ! ”
“ And I feel as healthy as I look. Yes, I
am twice the weight of what I was when I
gave up buying useless physics. For seven
years I have not been troubled with the
slightest sickness—not even a headache. I
can distinguish sound as quickly as any-
body, and believe my former deafness was
caused through constantly catching colds
through my shocking state of weakness.”
“ How was such a remarkable recovery
effected?”
“In a very simple way. One day I was
sitting in my usual forlorn and disheartened
condition, when a knock came to the door.
A pamphlet was handed in, and I read
therein about the effects of Clements Tonic.
There was a case like mine, and the person
had been cured by Clements Tonic, a few
bottlea of which I bought straight away.
In two or three days I felt much better.
My stomach pains had then ceased, and my
appetite w r as good. Flatulence troubled
no more when my second bottle was finished,
and my heart beat with a healthy regularity.
The pains in my back were gone, ae were
also the aches in my head and limbs.
Clements Tonic made me stronger every
day, and it brought l ack all my vigor and
cheerfulness. How could I help feeling
happy when I knew I had found a medicine
which was surely supplying the qualities my
wrecked system required ? ( lements Tonic
cured me of every symptom, and even when
I was quite well I occasionally took a dose
of Clements Tonic to clinch the cure.
Clements Tonic has no equal, and I strongly
advise all sufferers to take it. I have
proved it.”
“I shall publish all you have said with
your permission ! ”
“ You may, as freely and as often as you
please.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Fannik Froqlby, of 87 Mount Street, North
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting: of throe folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to three, and
thatit contains and is a true and faithful account of nay
illness and oure by Clements Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish in any way my state-
ments—which I give voluntarily, without receiving
any payment; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of an Act made and passed
in the ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty,
intituled “An Act for the more effectual abolition
of Oaths and Affirmations taken aud made in the
various Departments of the Government of New South
Wales, and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof,
and for the suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial
Oaths and Affidavits."
Declared at North Sydney, this Bth day of October,
one thousand nine hundred, before me,
WILLIAM MASSEY, J.P.

which h® attributes much of the luek that has
come his way. It was bequeathed to him by
N.S. W. Chief Justice Stephen, who got it from
'/alley. As soon as he became the stick’s owner,

derful stick ! You must leave it to me by your
will.”
“ Medico ” writes
The new Finsen cure for lupui seems to have rather
more in it than the majority of “cures” with which the
daily papers dazzle us. Lupus represents the action of
the tubercle bacillus on the skin, and consists usually of
a spreading ulceration affecting the face. It is for this
reason responsible for some particularly evident and hor-
rible deformities, and no previous treatment has been
able to_ stop its gradual spread with any certainty.
The ultimate result is usually the erosion of a large
artery, or possibly a lingering ending, from pain and
worry. Most of the cases popularly known as “ cancer of
the face,” arises from lupus, although a similar condition
may be produced by a form of true cancer affecting the
skin, and known as a rodent ulcer. The Finsen treatment
depends upon the action of the chemical rays in electric
light to which the affected part is exposed. Like most
other “ cures,” the Finsen process must stand the test of
time and experience before it be adopted by the medical
profession generally.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue *
Fred. M‘Cubbin’s studio at Melbourne
National Gallery is a place for painful reflection
just now. Mac. is at work upon a vast portrait
of M'Oaughey, M.L.O., of N.S.W., who appears
on the canvas garbed in “ the Court dress which
he wore when presented to Royalty in London ” —
vide daily newspaper paragraph. The employes
of M‘Caughey commissioned the artist to exe-
cute this souvenir of the proudest moment in
M ‘Caughey’s life. Another and a very artistically-
completed picture in M'Cubbin’s studio is the
portrait of Charles Atkins, Esq., an active and
not unintelligent citizen in the lubricating-oil
line. Atkins (in oils) is not depicted as booking
an order, or writing one of his characteristic jin-
goistic letters of “ public-spirited ” suggestion to
the Argus. No ! To quote again the beautiful
language of the press par. : “The subject is re-
presented in evening dress . . . and there is some
clever texture painting in the rendering of the
coat.” A nice-complexioned pusher of machine-
oils is quite entitled to fancy himself in evening
dress, and get his portrait painted accordingly,
but why should such matters as Atkins and his
coat, and M‘Caughey and his court dress be
advertised to the glory of Australian Art ?


Thus said Canon Scott-Holland, preaching re
cently in London :
Home! “the very word is a mockery” to multitudes
under “ the conditions in which they live.” They know
nothing of home ; and if they knew it in its meaning as
an ideal .... it could only sound to them as the
jeer of a scoffing devil. What is home to the 800,000
people who are living in London more than two in one
room for every purpose of life —sleeping, eating, resting,
washing, dressing ; for all familiar intercourse and quiet
withdrawal from the loud world—one room ! Think of
it: one room for two or three persons, the only thing in
life they have — 900,000 people to-night in London have
that for their home. One room for three or four to live
and sleep in— 290,000 people have that to-night. One
room for four or five people to live in wholly, and 114,000
people will be like that to-night in London. One
room for six or 12 people to live in—the
only thing they have, and 27,000 people in
London to-night will have that—one room. In
St. George’s in the East a man with five daughters and
three sons, most of them grown up, have all to make
their home in one room. And as in London so in Bir-
mingham, 68,000 people live more than two in one room ;
in Leeds, 60,000 more; in Liverpool, 52,000; in Man-
chester, 41,000; and Sheffield, 30,000—one room. One
room that has not only to be kitchen, drawing-room,
dining-room, bedroom, laundry, and workshop often, but
also a sick-room for the hot, fever-tossed patient, for the
man dying there, or the woman with racking cough—one
room to live in, one room only to die in ; one room in
which the dead must be laid out while all the family eat
and sleep round the corpse. No place for the children in
the one room ; they are driven out into the street, which
ij their only playground, left to discover what life may
mean by themselves in gutter games, in hordes around
the corner of city court and alley.
The long arm of coincidence smites the
ballot-box. The Q. jury-year commences June 1
and ends May 31 of the following year. The
names of jurors drawn between those dates are put
into a box and retained until the end of the cur-
rent jury-year. At the expiration of the year the
names are put into the ballot-box and a full list
drawn anew. At the last drawing (Rockhamp-
ton), a number of men whose names appeared on
all four panels of the last Supreme and District
Court list of special and common jurors were all
actually drawn again.
It is part of the British Oonstitooshun
that an Englishman’s house is his castle, and that
he has the right to wallop his wife whenever he
thinks she needs it, wherefore a North Melbourne
citizen was muoh surprised when the police lately
invaded his private apartments and locked him up
for “ merely chastising his wife in a proper
manner ” —to wit, with nis fist. He was still more
surprised when the beaks fell on his constitutional
rights and fined him £2. As the lady promptly
paid the fine, she presumably thought the discip-
line did her a lot of good.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
A youth of about 14 gave himself up to
the Sydney police t’other day. Said his name
was Joseph Jones, but beyond that fact he could
remember nothing.. Address ? Didn’t know.
Parents living ? Didn’t know. How long in
Sydney ? No idea. Where did he sleep last
night ? Couldn’t say. A commiserating magis-
trate committed the lost waif to the care of the
Benevolent Society. The institution doctor made
his usual examination, and, finding the boy’s eye-
sight affected, ordered an operation. He was
being escorted to a city hospital for treatment,
when a little boy passing him yelled “ There’s
Harry ! ” Harry it was right enough, and he
was speedily grabbed by his mother, who had
been anxiously looking for him for days. The
youngster, tired of the humdrum of home, had
determined to get lost. It was a poor reward for
his vigorous foreign policy that the road to the
hospital led past his own door.
At a recent Vic. bye-election, a successful
candidate was supported by the Scripture League,
the W.C.T.U. and the Licensed Victuallers’
Association. An alliance as incongruous as that
of the clergy and the bookmakers who are moving
Heaven and earth to down Tattersall.
An unusual maintenance action was re-
cently heard at Wellington (M.L.). The parties
were divorced, and the wife got judgment for
arrears of maintenance up to time of divorce. An
application by defendant for cancellation of the
order was refused, the decree of divorce not yet
having been made absolute. The question of the
liability of the man for maintenance in the interim
was allowed to stand over, a test-c ■ j on the sub-
ject being as yet undecided.

Apropos law-reform. Judge Emden, of
the Eng. County Court Bench, a front-ranker so
far as commercial law goes, writes in Nineteenth
Century an article of the sort that doesn’t often
come from a lawyer. He suggests first the simpli-
fication of the law. Apparently he would like to
return to the ancient exclusion of lawyers from
Parliament, but doesn’t seem to think that pos-
sible. For practical purposes, he thinks most im-
provement is to be hoped for from paying lawyers
according, not to the length to which they drag
out the proceedings, but to the importance of the
matter in dispute—on the same basis as architects,
agents, &c., are paid. To simplify procedure and
for like purposes he would have a Minister for
Justice (but wasn’t Mr. Heydon N.S.W. Minister
for Justice?) and a permanent advisory board,
composed of men “entirely free from professional
influences,” which, he remarks, the Judges, as a
rule, are not.

“ It is astonishing to see
this little device at work
executing the masterpieces
of pianoforte literature
with a dexterity, clearness,
and velocity which no
pla, er, however great, can
approach. Every one who
wishes to hear absolutely
faultless, free from any
kind of nervousness, piano-
playing should buy a Pi-
anola. It is perfection.*’—
I. J. Paderewski.
Has arrested the attention of the
musical world. Its claims have been
investigated and verified by the great
pianists themselves.
This verification of its claims makes them interesting and
worthy of attention.
JHE PIANOLA is an instrument by means of which any one
can play the Piano.
This is accomplished by simply rolling the Pianola in
front of any piano, allowing the felt-covered fingers to rest
upon the keys of the piano, then inserting a roll of perforated paper
into the sockets, and operating the pedals. Playing-directions appear
upon the roll as the music unwinds.
Thus the Pianola provides a substitute for the human fingers and
a correct technique for any piece.
The artistic effects and vivifying personality are given to the ren-
dition by the player, who controls the expression. It is entirely subject
to his will. He may vary the touch from the most delicate pianissimo
to the “ lion’s touch of Rubinstein.”
This human element is the distinctive feature of the Pianola, and
removes it from the ranks of purely mechanical musical instruments. It
taxes the credulity c f all who have not heard it, but is substantiated by
the fact that the Pianola _
is the only Piano-Play< r
endorsed by musicians
of note.

A Pair of Hard Bargains.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CHAtfOKT for changey— white —dog—for—
brass monkey ! ”
The little Parsee boy stole along the deck of the
“ Gleam,” and peeped into the apprentices’ house.
It was Sunday morning, and the only occupant
was indulging in a “sailor’s pleasure” —which
means that he was turning-out the contents of his
sea-chest and re-packin'g it; for Billy, like all his
tribe, when he wanted anything from his chest,
stirred it up until the desired article came to the
top, and devoted part of the Sabbath to putting
the week’s chaos in order.
The beady black eyes of the little Parsee mer-
chant glittered covetously as he noted the beloved
“ Ingeeleesh flannelly eshirts ” ; but he knew
Billy, and was wary.
‘ ‘ Where your bruzzer ? ” he inquired.
“Hullo, Johnny !” cried the boy, looking up
from his work of folding and smoothing-out his
best brass-bound suit. “You want to do a
changey-for-changey ? What you got ? ”
“I got eseent, escarf, escap—eberything got.
Monkey no got, ” replied the youthful merchant,
setting down his tray. “ But where your looking-
glass bruzzer ? ”
“ He’s gone to church,” replied Billy, grinning,
“but I’ll do a changey with you.”
“ No, no; you no speaky true ! ” replied the
Parsee, with the gravity of a patriarch. “Sup-
pose your bruzzer go makey pray, then you go
makey pray, too —but I see you here! You no
go, your bruzzer no go. You two boys—but only
one face have. One time I come, you speaky me
‘ rooti munter.’ All right, I bring plenty rooti.
You show it me new flannelly eshirts, I speaky
you, ‘All right, I changey.’ Then you speaky,
‘ Look, esee if Captain Sahib come; cause he
bobbery plenty make if boy changey his eshirt.’
All right; I look esee, and then you speaky me,
‘ Quick, he coming jeldy jou ! ’ and you put flan-
nelly eshirt inside my basket and I go away
quiok. But wait; I find what welly bad boy
Escotchman tief you are. The eshirt you hide
quick in my basket look on top-side allee same
good new eshirt you show me for changey-changey.
But wait! It is wicked ;it is rolled up tight with
estring. I untie it, and behold ! it is a welly old
eshirt.”
The little merchant tried to express by a hideous
grimace of disgust the sort of old shirt that had
been substituted for the new one, and Billy
laughed, for, in his mind’s eye, he saw the little
Parsee gazing indignantly at that old tarring-down
garment of his brother’s, with its variegated
patches.
The twins prided themselves upon their origi-
nality in repairing their wardrobes, and would
scorn to put a grey patch on a white shirt if they
could steal a piece of blue or red bunting for the
job.
“You make big mistake, Johnnie,” replied
Billy, effacing the grin with a grieved expression.
“My brother gave you that old shirt, not me. I
wouldn’t do a thing like that.”
“ Oh, yes! P’raps you Bill-ee, p’raps you
Charl-ee —who know ? ” and the little Parsee ap-
pealed to the Universe with outspread hands.
“I’m Billy.”
“Oh, yes! Sometimes you Bill-ee, sometimes
you Charl-ee. You take my esoap, my eseent, and
you speaky me Bill-ee. You say, ‘ All right, Igo
get rupee from Captain Sahib.’ Bimeby I speaky
‘ You give it one rupee.’ Then you laugh, and
you speaky me Charl-ee. Oh, yes ! you Charl-ee
then, not Bill-ee. Oh, no, never I find Billee;
but it is finish ! Ino more changey-for-changey
with one Bill-ee; other fellow come, make two
Bill-ees. Then plenty changey-for-changey I can
do.”
A hand stole round the door-post and lifted the
little Parsee’s hat off, exposing his clean-scraped
head ; but he kept his eyes fixed upon his stock.
“ Ah ! Charl-ee come back—finish makey pray,”
he said without looking round.
Charlie entered, and rubbed his hand vigorously
over the little bald pate.
“ You haven’t shaved your napper this morn-
ing, Johnny. Stand still while I strike a match
on it.”
“No, no,” cried the little merchant with a
vicious gleam in his eyes; “you makey jokey, I
makey business. If I none can do—then—
Salaam ! ” And the little fellow bowed gracefully
and prepared to pack up his display of gaudy
rubbish. But the twins protested that they were
anxious to do business—they always were.
Charlie, whose hands were hovering about the
tray under the owner’s watchful eyes, suddenly
swooped upon a gaudy penholder of Brummagem
birth and examined it with extravagant expres-
sions of admiration.
“How much you want for this, Johnny?” he
inquired eagerly.
The Parsee boy smiled the smile of an indulgent
parent whose infant son asks him to reach down
the moon.
“That pen for Captain Sahib,” he answered;
“not for poor boy. Money you no got to buy
him. You buy little commoney pen ashore,” he
added in a soothing tone.
Charlie shot at his brother a swift glance, which
was answered by a wink.
“Is it gold?” asked Billy in an awe-stricken
voice, taking his turn at admiring the pen, which
he recognised as an old friend of the “ any on this
card for a penny ” variety.
“Yes, all solid gold,” replied the merchant,
taking it quickly from him and replacing it re-
verently in the tray. “ But, come, I show you
little thing so cheap for boys,” he added, sitting
down cross-legged to business. But the twins
gazed at the gorgeous penholder, and refused to
be put off with anything less valuable.
Billy took his best pilot-cloth jacket, which he
had just carefully brushed and folded.
“ Here, Johnny, I changey this for the pen.”
The little Parsee was delighted, and would have
closed the deal right there, but out of the corner
of his eye he saw Charlie make a hasty dive at his
chest, and he quickly dissembled his satisfaction.
“Hold hard, Johnny,” cried Charlie excitedly.
“I spoke you first for that pen. You make
changey with me. See ! I give you this ! ” He
produced a whole suit and laid it before the grati-
fied merchant. But the Parsee youth had got his
professional “’Tis naught, saith the buyer” ex-
pression well under control now, and, glancing
coldly at the offering, he turned to Billy with—
“ And what more you gib, Bill-ee ? ”
Billy promptly placed two Crimean shirts and a
blue necktie on top of the pilot-jacket.
But Johnny waved them away. “ This welly
fine pen. You no savee how many rupees my
countrymen gib for him. Only one more can find
in Bombay. But I likee you, Char-lee and
Bili-ee—you number-one boys. What more you
gib, Char-lee?” Charlie went one better with
three shirts and a pair of shoes ; but Billy—after
another examination of the preoious pen, and a
renewed assurance that it was real 25-callot gold—
outbid his brother by two undershirts.
So, assisted by the Parsee’s eloquent description
of the many Captain Sahibs who longed vainly to
Eossess that pen, the fierce competition raged
etween the twins ; until, in reply to the monoto-
nous “What more‘you gib?” they sat on their
empty chests, peering eagerly over their respec-
tive stacks of clothes, at the pen, which the little
Parsee had placed artfully where the sun made it
look more like gold than a sovereign.
“ Well,” exclaimed both lads together, “which
lot you take ? ”
The trader looked critically at the bargains
before him, making a mental calculation
of the value of each. It was a big deal
—the biggest he had ever dreamt of—and
he exulted not only in the profit of it, bu t in the
knowledge that his good old father, who had
trained him to bargain and lie ever since he could
toddle, would praise him and boast of his son to
all the elders in the Bazaar.
But these thoughts were not reflected in his
grave little face, as he said to Billy, “You got
big bag ? ”
“ No,” replied Billy.
He turned to Charlie. “You got him bag ? ”
Charlie laughed uproariously. “The little
beggar wants a bag to carry off the plunder.
Here, then.” He emptied out a capacious soiled-
linen bag, and placed it on his pile.
Billy was putting his clothes back into his
chest.
The little Parsee handed the penholder to
Charlie with a polite salaam.
“Here, you take, but not tell Captain Sahib, I
sell him so cheap. He welly angry suppose he
know he lose that No. 1 gold-pen.”
But Charlie pushed the pen away with almost
Oriental dignity. “ No, Johnny,” he said,
sitting down firmly upon his clothes. “ When it
comes to taking that beautiful pen for these old
clothes, I feel I can’t do it. It would be taking
advantage of your kindness. Besides, a pen like
that is too beautiful for a boy like me. It would
make me feel too proud.”
The merchant turned eagerly to Billy and
clutched the clothes that still remained out of his
chest. “Here, you take it. You welly good
No. 1 boy.”
“Hands off!” was the indignant rejoinder.
“You too greedy, Johnny ! Suppose you no ask
me for that bag, I would have done a changey,
but now too late.”
“ Well; look, I gib it now,” cried the little
merchant, almost in tears at seeing his grand
bargain melting away.
“No, you’ve lost the deal through being too
greedy,” replied Billy, calmly going on with his
repacking.
“But, wait; you too much hurrjr make,” pro-
tested the now trembling trader in persuasive
tones. “Look! I show you.” He took a pair of
socks off the fast dwindling pile and laid them
aside with an air of extreme sacrifice. “There,
now, I take these, and I makey you present of
this never-can-get-better gold pen.” But the
generous smile that accompanied the offer changed
to an expression of blank despair as Billy closed
the lid of his chest, and replied, with brutal
frankness, “ You’re an awful little liar, Johnny !”
Tears were in the disappointed Parsee’s eyes.
He fully believed that if he hadn’t asked for that
accursed clothes-bag the deal would have come-off
—for the twins were artists at such deceptions.
He turned to Charlie to make a last effort, but
Charlie had put all his trade back into his chest;
and, in his abstraction, a few trifles from the
curio-basket had gone with them.
“ Halves ! ” said Billy, as soon as the disgusted
little merchant had gone ; and Charlie produced
two cakes of scented soap and a packet of Turkish
delight.
But, later on, Billy found that he was short of
a Crimean shirt.
“The little beggar scored, after all,” he re-
marked.
“They generally do,” groaned Charlie, with
his mouth full of Turkish delight^
CRIPPS CLARK.
“ Melbourneite ” to The Bulletin :
People who contemplate supporting the forthcoming
bazaar in aid of the Abbotsford (Vic.) convent should re-
member the charges made against it over two years ago,
and never yet refuted or even contradicted. Abbotsford
Convent runs a big washing-establishment, to which all
local good Catholics are supposed to send their linen. It
is under no Factories Act, being exempt as an alleged
charitable institution, and the proprietors of private
washeries allege that the godly institution works its
“ rescued ” women 12 to 15 or 16 hours a day for nothing
but their tucker, even utilising the reformed sinners to do
the laborious work of stoking the boilers, in order to
avoid the expense of employing men. These charges
were made in Melb. City Police-court, and were repeated
in a pamphlet issued by one of the washing-establish-
ments in question ; yet not a word of contradiction has
been given, while the allegations have been corrobor-
ated by Magdalenes who relapsed to sin rather than find
frace per medium of being sweated for the glory of God.
Iverybody who thinks of subscribing to the bazaar under
the impression that he is assisting a charitable movement
should paste this in his hat.
The Bulletin would like to know what are really
the exact facts of the case.
A man appeared before the Wellington
(M.L.) Magistrate's Court a week or two ago, to
plead his case against a grocer who was suing him
for victuals supplied. Defendant heaved himself
up and reasoned with the Bench: “You see,
your Washup, my case is unique in this country.
I’ve only bin married six years, and me wife has
brought home seven youngsters, four of ’em were
twins, and me an’ the missus is quite worn-out
with rearing them kids. Now I’m very sorry to
be in this position, but prices have gone up lately,
and I can’t pay the grocer and rear all these
children too. It’s quite impossible, yer Washup!”
“You provide the children, and expect others to
provide for them ? ,f said the Court in a severe tone
of voice. “That’s all right,” said the man;
“other people’ll get the benefit of them young-
sters. I won’t. And if yer goin’ to argue
about it on a business bottom, well, those as
benefits, by ’em ought to keep ’em. But I
ain’t doin’ that. What I says is, that I can’t
pay this yer grocer and rear these seven
youngsters, too. I know we ought not to have had
’em, but it was me misfortune and not me fault
that I married a woman whose mother had 21
children. If there was any statesmen in the
country they’d pay people like us something out
of the Treasury for the service we’re doin’ the
State in giving it seven new citizens.” The
Court chewed its pen-handle, and then made an
order for ss. per month, and the parent and pa-
triot left the court declaring he could never pay it.
This is the sort of case where the horrors of
public ingratitude show up strongly. Maoriland
papers are in a state of deep anxiety about the
declining birth-rate, which simply means that
they are shocked and alarmed because people
don’t have larger families. And when one citizen
does have a large family, and can’t pay his grocer’s
bill in consequence, he is held up to ridicule by
these same papers in a way calculated to make
other parents decide to keep down the birth-vate.

An Ode.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Liberty cried her message to the Earth,
And half her voice was heeded: “You shall live
And prosper each according to his worth,
Nor shall you once receive until you give.
Fate’s sad old prophecy will be complete,
And every man shall sweat before he eat.”
But Liberty is silent; on her breast
A star and ribbon charm her voice to sleep;
“ Empire ” is branded on her bursting vest
(Alas for Liberty grown fat !) —and deep
Her thunderous guns for Five-Feet-Nothing roar,
While Princes mince on carpets to her shore.
How often, in a thousand fruitful years,
Will greatness mount upon a guarded throne ?
William Charles Edward where are Crom-
well’s peers
Daring a nation to take back its own ?
Our Cromwells (knighted) urge us to obey
A spectre—at a thousand pounds a day.
I hate all Kings : they are born of shams and lies,
From that cracked Kaiser with the seething
brain,
Or bloody Abdul with his weary eyes,
To Wilhelmina—giggling, vapid, vain ;
Or that old tired spirit, who will not yield
The sceptre which Unreason soon must wield.
If you or I had lived in other days,
When thrones were stakes to win, for who fought
best,
We might have striven for a prince’s praise
And been—good Lord ! —as “loyal” as the rest;
But dismally the tardy trumpets ring
When ’tis the tailor’s craft proclaims the King.
Where sturdy monarchs played a kingly game,
And, losing it, they jested with their death,
Our tinselled puppets venture but to claim
A little surfeit of the people’s breath
To fill their bubbles, till that coming morn
When Time shall laugh his parasites to scorn.
V.
PAGAN.
The Old Man’s Collapse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“What on airth jer want to get married for,”
growled the old man.
John Davies, his son addressed, was unable to
give any reasons for such a step, or if he could,
preferred not to.
“ Ain’t you got a good ’ome ? ”
John sheepishly murmured “Yes, father.”
“ Ain’t you got all yer want ? Beer ? Bacca ? ”
Really John had not all he wanted, because
there was Susan Earl. But being accustomed to
take his opinions from his father, he agreed.
“Well, what on airth more jer want? You’ll
’ave the farm when I’m gone, and not a farden
before. Plenty of time fer you.” Davies senior
considered that this settled the debate, and John,
from weakness of will and habit, dutifully accepted
the closure.
Mr. Davies, who farmed 300 acres in northern
Tasmania, had at intervals during nine years so
delivered himself. Davies senior was big and
burly; whiskers covered his waistcoat and he was
exceedingly domineering. Although mutton-
reared, his offspring was beefy, stodgy, tomato-
faced and very obedient. John was thirty-one.
Nine years ago the united hopes of John and
Susan Earl soared balloon-like into ether. John
cheerfully argued that, as he had worked for his
father ever since work could be got out of him, for
just his tucker, clothing and pocket-money,
simple justice required that his parent should now
pay up arrears of wages in a lump sum, such, as
would keep their balloon inflated. John also per-
suaded himself and Susan that his father would
be sure to give him a partnership or start him on
another farm.
When these things were mildly suggested to
Mr. Davies by his son, there was a scene.
“What on airth d’yer want to marry for?’*
raved the old man. “ Blamed nonsense—/ never
married ! ”
John was aware of the discrepancy of his birth.
He felt, however, he could hardly use that as an
argument with Susan.
“ Look ’ere,” declared the old man. “ Unner-
stan’ me. Not a farden while I’m alive ! You
kin clear and git fer yerself if yer wants marry-
ing.”
So John pacified Susan, and persuaded her to
agree with a, policy of patience. They had now
been pursuing that policy for nine years, and
Susan was sick of it. On the other hand, John
had'sunk into a stupor of resignation. Now and
again Susan impelled him to attack the old man,
but each appeal was more faint-hearted than the
former. He wu beginning to see with his father
that, after all, a man can do very well without
marriage.
This was not the view of Susan. She was not
by any means a dull girl, and she could see her
balloon so bedraggled and full of holes that it
would probably never again be sufficiently inflated
to soar. She was earning a scanty living at dress-
making, and lived with her mother at East Morn-
ington, two miles from the Davies’s farm.
Susan’s mother possessed a high appreciation of
the deference due to the Earl family. Conse-
quently, she emphatically disapproved of the
dalliance of John, and sought to turn Susan’s eyes
in the direction of young men more eager for the
happiness she could confer.
“It’s no use, mother,” said Susan firmly. “I
admit I don’t think much of John now. But it’s
too late to think of anybody else. Remember I’m
thirty ! ”
“Bless me, so you be now,” admitted Mrs.
Earl. “All the same, you’re throwing away the
beat years of your life waiting for a fool of a man
without enough in him to frighten a crow. I’d
frighten ’im.”
“Better frighten the old man,” said Susan
quietly.
“ Pison ’im ! ” declared her mother warmly.
“No; frighten him,” said Susan. “If he isn’t
dying he ought to be. You can always manage
that sort when they’re ill.”
Mrs. Earl looked admiringly at her daughter.
“ Bless me, if I don’t believe you’re right,” she
said. “I’ll just run around and ask one or two
if they’ve noticed how bad the old man’s been
ooking lately.”
* * » *
A few days later the old man came home from
Mornington at tea-time. The two men sat down
together to tea and mutton. The younger began
to tell of happenings on the farm.
“Them steers got inter the big paddock,” he
remarked.
Instead of wanting to know why the (swear)
fence wasn’t (double-barrelled swear) looked-to,
the old man nervously played with a mutton-bone.
“Sow got inter the cabbages,” went on the
son. Such a thing ordinarily would have turned
the old man’s wrath and profanity full-on. He
seemed not to hear.
“ John ! ” he ejaculated, after a silence.
“Well?”
“ D’yer think I look sick ? ”
“ Dunno,” said John in astonishment.
The old man groaned. Presently he said :
“ Met Tom Merry just now.”
“Oh !” uninterestedly.
“ He thought I was looking queer.”
“ You’re getting old, guv’nor,” remarked John
irreverently, and his father sighed.
A couple of days later the old man startled the
young one by asking if he ever had pains in his
back.
“ No,” said John.
“Ah,” sighed the old man, dolefully. “Just
here,” he said, contorting himself in an endeavor
to point to the middle of his back.
“ Dancin’spots afore yer eyes?” went on Mr.
Davies, with a ghostly look.
“No.”
“Feel yer tucker ain’t doin’ yer no good?”
“No.”
“George Peters and Sam Evans and two or
three others remarked on it,” continued the old
man. “I’m breaking-up.”
* • * *
That evening John saw Susan.
“How ill your father looks !” remarked Susan,
sympathetically.
“Don’t notice it,” said John; “but he’s com-
plaining of pains.”
“Where?” interpolated Mrs. Earl.
“In his back,” replied John.
“Ah,” said Mrs. Earl, dejectedly; “just like
my poor James—and he was gone, as you might
say, in a minute.”
“ Everybody says he’s looking right-down ill,”
declared Susan.
“O, he’s all right,” said John, with an attempt
at dismissing the subject.
“Anyway,” went on Susan tartly, “We’ve no
reason to hope he is. It’s time we got married, if
ever we’re going to. P’raps you’re thinking
better of it.”
John vigorously repudiated such a thought.
“Very well, then,” said Susan. “You leave it
to mother and me. He’ll be easy to manage
when he’s ill. All you’ve got to do is to see he
doesn’t think he’s better.”
John went home thoroughly interested, and
gave his relative a searching examination. The
old man pretended not to notice it. He picked
up a newspaper.
“How d’e feel, father,” asked John, mourn-
fully. - - -
“Queer, John,” growled the old man. “The
pains in me chest is somefin orful.”
John looked supremely unhappy. “I see a
man died suddenly in Launceston yesterday,” he
murmured, sadly.
The. paper rattled in the old man’s shaking
hand.
“You d-d-don’t think,” he stammered.
“ Can’t never tell in them cases,” replied John,
solemnly. “It says in the paper that pains in
the back killed thousands.”
“I se’ed that myself,” the old man agreed,
almost inaudibly. “ They alius put it in when
anybody recovers.”
• * * •
Old man Davies got rapidly worse. He went
once into East Mornington, and came back de-
claring he wouldn’t go into the place again.
“ Everybody can see I’m breaking-up,” he
growled piteously.
“Yes, father,” admitted John. “ Why don’t
you have the doctor ?”
“ Doctors ain’t no good for this,” groaned the
invalid. “It’s internal. Jimmy Higgs told me
ter-day.”
The'following day the old man refused to leave
his bed. John went into East Mornington and
brought back Mrs. and Miss Earl.
“Susan’s come to nurse you, father,” he ex-
plained.
“It’s no use,” groaned the invalid. “I’m
breaking-up, break-ing—up.”
At first he objected with quite old-time vitupera-
tion to Mrs. Earl’s presence ; but he quickly came
to see she understood his malady better than her
daughter. He took a morbid delight in hearing
Mrs. Earl’s lugubrious descriptions of the death-
scenes of the late Mr. Earl, who apparently had
perished in great agony from the exact disease
which was ravaging himself.
The young couple were, consequently, left to
themselves.
“You leave it to mother,” said Susan, confi-
dently. “She’ll get round him. She’s going to
tell him how poor father’s last days were spoilt by
his remembering how he had been hard on others.”
Susan’s confidence seemed justified. With
great difficulty and much groaning the invalid was
brought on to the verandah one morning.
“John,” he said, meekly, “I’m thinking I’ve
bin ’ard on you and Susan. I kin unnerstan’ now
how you wanted ter git wed.”
“Yes, father,” said John, expectantly.
“P’r’apsMrs. Earl and me ’ull make it better
fer yer.”
“ Susan’ll be very glad,” remarked John.
“Yes,” murmured the old man, “me and Mrs.
Earl’s bin’aving a talk, and we’ve fixed it all up.’
The lady mentioned just then emerged from the
house dressed for an excursion.
“John, my dear,” she said, “bring round the
buggy. I’m going to take your father for a drive.”
John expressed astonishment. His father, how-
ever, peremptorily declared he was lots better,
and could climb into the buggy easily. He was
hoisted in somehow, and Mrs. Earl drove away,
John told Susan the good news, and then went
hoeing turnips until dark. His mind was rather
in a whirl, and hoeing acted as a sedative. It
struck him that there were a number of things to
settle first ; but, all the same, it did seem as if he
and Susan would be married at last,
His mother-in-law-expectant was presiding at
the tea-table when he got indoors. His father
was eating cold mutton with an appreciation he
hadn’t shown for weeks. •
“Gome, my son,” called Mrs. Earl, tenderly.
“I told you I’d be able to call you ‘my son
before long.”
“ Where’s Susan ? ” asked John. He realised
that there was danger yet if they allowed his
father to recover,
Mrs. Earl’s face fell.
“She’s gone home,” she explained, forcing a
boiled egg upon John to distract his attention.
“What for?” pursued John,
The old man banged the table with his clenched
fist. “She’s an ongrateful lot,” he declared,
“That’s wot she is. Mrs. Earl’ere, as was, and
now is Mrs. Davies and yer mother,, an’ me
offered ’er a ’ome ’ere so that you and ’er could
live comfertable like ’til we wuz gone and yer
could fix things, but she up an’ runs off.”
“And most insultin’,” added the new Mrs.
Davies dabbing her eyes plaintively,
John gazed in wonderment from one to the
other.
“Your pa married me ter-day, John,” she ex-
plained, “and I’m sure I’ll be a good mother to
you.”
John concentrated his gaze on his half-eaten
egg. For ten minutes he was silent. Then he
comprehended enough of the situation to say he
was “damned!”
Susan returned to dressmaking, and John con-
tinues to plod along for his tucker and a reduced
beer-allowance, while the old man, under altered
treatment from his wife, quickly recovered his
normal health and more than his normal temper,
T. H. BRYANT.
